Reader’s guide
This thesis was conducted within the framework of the InnoBYG project "Design for Disassembly of
Prefabricated buildings" that started March 2017 and is going to last for a year. The writer of this thesis
produced deliverables both individually and as a part of the project coordinator’s team. The "Project
coordinators" are members of the two knowledge institutions of the project, DTU and KADK. The term
"Other stakeholders" in Figure 1 is referring to project participants from the industry, including archi-
tects, engineers, manufacturers and others.

Figure 1: Thesis analysis
Firstly, based on an extended literature review from the writer of this thesis took place and concluded
on a wide range of "Rules of Thumb" [Deliverable 1], that refer to guidelines on implementing DfD.
In parallel, Line Kjær Frederiksen, teaching assistant from KADK, and the author of this thesis, had
a round of interviews with experts from the "Other stakeholders" and made an initial mapping of the
challenges regarding DfD. In the workshop that was organized from the project coordinators, included
presentations on DfD projects, and further mapping and discussion of the challenges. The interviews and
the workshop contributed with inputs based on which, the "Main challenges" derived [Deliverable 2].
1
==PAGE:4==
Next, based on: 1.the proposals for the project that were documented in the workshop, 2.the stakeholders
that declared interest during the workshop and 3.according to the framework that was set by the project
coordinators for the next steps of the project, it was decided to design a case house that fulﬁlls the DfD
principles. Other inputs to the designing process, except from the workshop results were 4.knowledge,
experience and the interests of the "Other stakeholders" regarding the use of speciﬁc materials and
the functions and purposes of the house, and ultimately 5.the results of the literature review regarding
guidelines and technical advice. Consequently, a house that ﬁts to the DfD principles was designed and
was the Deliverable 3. Finally, an LCA of the case House was performed Deliverable 4 and compared
with the results of the "Reference House" and the "Upcycle House" Deliverable 5.
2

==PAGE:6==
Abstract
The building industry is a typical example of a "take, make, dispose" linear economy, that produces the
1/3 of the total waste volume in Denmark. Considering that the expected rise of global middle class
by 3 billion people until 2030 and the corresponding ampliﬁed need for doubling the infrastructure and
the number of buildings worldwide in 40 years, the current business model of the building industry isn’t
viable. As a result, Design for Disassembly aims to close the loop in the building industry by designing
buildings that can facilitate renovations and eventually dismantlement (in parts or whole) for "recovery
of systems, components and materials" and parts that are easier to be rearranged, recovered, and reused.
In addition aims to add ﬂexibility to the buildings, which is the main cause for building demolition.
In danish content, InnoBYG launched the project "Design for Disassembly of Prefabricated buildings"
that aims to optimize the resource consumption in the building industry by identifying, analyzing and
developing new methods to utilize prefabricated buildings based on Design for Disassembly (DfD) prin-
ciples. This thesis took place within the framework of this InnoBYG project and its speciﬁc goals were
ﬁrstly to create a list of "Rules of Thumb" (RoT) as guidelines for working with DfD, secondly to map
the challenges regarding the sector-wide implementation of DfD and ultimately assess the environmental
performance of the case study house ("DfD House") that derived within the framework of the InnoBYG
project.
RoT derived by an extensive literature review and they aim serve as a tool for the easy communication
of the DfD principles, and as a brief guide for implementing DfD. RoT have a generic nature and can be
applied theoretically to all the building layers except the "Staﬀ(furniture)" according to the classiﬁcation
of the building layers from Brand (1994). Due to their structure, RoT enable the prioritization of the
preferred EoL scenario (such as reuse, over recycling).
Challenges derived by a wide range of interviews with professionals from the industry and the choice
of interviewees aimed to represent all the potentially aﬀected parts of the building sector. In addition,
challenges were also discussed in the InnoBYG workshop named "DESIGN FOR DISASSEMEBLY i
præfabrikeret byggeri", that took place in KADK on April 14, 2017. The main groups of challenges, were
acknowledged to be the "Business case", "Lifespans of Materials", "Detailing", "Reuse of components
and Materials", "Manufacturing of Products" and "Prefabrication". Among them, the "Business case"
related group was considered to be the most diﬃcult to be tackled as it includes some major challenges,
such as the lack of an existing business model and the long time frame of the investment.
Furthermore, in parallel with this report, a case house was designed within the framework of the In-
noBYG project. The case house is assessed for its compliance with the RoT and the aims of the project.
In addition in this thesis an LCA of the case house was performed. For the LCA, two basic scenarios were
examined and their diﬀerence derived by the choice of the future "marginal energy source", which is the
energy source that is considered to be substituted by the energy utilization of the wood and wood-based
products at the End-of-Life stage. The two selected energy sources were natural gas and biomass. The
ﬁnal results showed that the case house had a carbon footprint between the two reference buildings,
but on the other hand the case house had 4.5 to 5 times more impacts in the Eutrophication category
comparing to the Reference House.
Considering also the low data quality and the high uncertainty of the LCA, a concrete decision can’t be
reached whether the case house performs better or worse than the two reference buildings in general. For
such a conclusion, toxicity impacts categories shall be examined as well, while impacts from maintenance
shall also be added. In addition a higher quality of input data would also mitigate the uncertainty in the
calculations. Ultimately, the case house, and speciﬁcally its wooden elements, are performing as a carbon
pool during their lifecycle. Although the advantages from temporary carbon storage is under discussion,
a shift of the traditional building methods to the wide use of wooden and wood-based components,
could make our cities to perform as permanent carbon pools and this could thus lead to unquestionable
environmental beneﬁts.

1
Introduction
The construction sector is responsible for 42% of EU ﬁnal energy consumption [EC (2011)], 35% of all
greenhouse gas emissions [European commission (2007)] and more than 50% of all extracted materials
[EC (2011)].
These impacts derive by the fact that the construction industry nowadays is a linear
economy, as the existing recycling procedure is actually just down-cycling with huge value losses and
questionable beneﬁts [MacSporran (1994)]. Considering the steep increase of the population globally
and the continuous resource depletion, the need to "close the loop" in construction is growing. These
facts make the construction industry a ﬁeld of special interest for sustainability improvements and are
triggering numerous initiatives during the last decade. Under this framework, designing buildings for
disassembly (DfD) could boost the reuse of fully functional building components multiple times.
1.1
The InnoBYG project
This thesis is part of a greater project of InnoBYG named "Design for disassembly in prefabricated build-
ings". The duration of the project is one year starting on the 1st of March 2017. Knowledge institutions
of the project are DTU, in behalf of which the author participated in the project, and CINARK. The
purpose of the project is to optimize the resource consumption in the building industry by identifying,
analyzing and developing new methods to utilize prefabricated buildings based on Design for Disassem-
bly principles. In addition, the project aims to promote direct recycling and reuse of building materials
and elements, ensuring the retention of the embedded value both from an economic, environmental and
cultural perspective. Furthermore the project aims to uncover what new types of factors and new multi-
disciplinary collaborative models are necessary for the development of new working methods that focus
on circular recycling of building materials and elements according to the DFD principles. The ultimate
target is to drive development in the construction industry forward by providing concrete suggestions for
future construction to strengthen the common reference frame
1.2
Thesis description and goals
This master thesis, aims ﬁrstly to map the current literature on DfD and to create an extensive list of
"Rules of Thumb" regarding its application. Furthermore based on a round of interviews with experts
and the hosted “Design for Disassembly" workshop, this thesis aims to map and analyze the challenges
regarding the implementation of DfD sector-wide. These results were used as an input for the case study
house that is being designed, but also for the rest of the project.
In addition, within the framework of this thesis, an LCA is performed for the case house in order to
assess its performance, identify any problems in its performance and to assess diﬀerent scenarios. In
addition, the environmental performance of the house is compared with two other single family houses.
The two houses are “the Reference House”, which is a typical danish single-family house, and “the Upcycle
House”, which is considered as the top performing single-family house. The aim of this comparison is to
measure and analyze the environmental performance of a house based on DfD principles and organize
this knowledge and ideas for further research as inputs towards the improvement of the case house and
also towards the progress of the wider project.
In conclusion, the wider aim of the thesis is to strengthen resource optimization in buildings by identifying
the challenges regarding DfD, creating a framework for its implementation, while forming and analyzing
potential solutions.
1.3
Research questions
The main research questions of this thesis can be summed up to be:
"Which are the key principles for implementing Design for Disassembly?"
"What are the main challenges regarding the industry-wide implementation of Design for Disassembly?"
"What is the environmental performance of the case house"
"How does this house performs environmentally comparing to the Reference House and the Upcycle
House?"
8
==PAGE:11==
2
Theory
During last decades, the resource depletion, the optimization of resource management and the rising envi-
ronmental awareness drove industrial ecosystems towards utilizing the unwanted output from production
systems as resources for others. This trend has decreased the environmental burden that our society
would have to handle otherwise by a signiﬁcant margin. However the adoption rate of this strategy in the
construction industry has lagged behind other sectors, as there hasn’t been enough research conducted on
that front. Industrial ecology can help the industry to improve signiﬁcantly the construction industry’s
environmental performance [Crowther (2000c)].
2.1
Environmental footprint of constructions
Buildings are the place where people in the western world spend more than 90% of their time [Jastrup
and Drique (2013)]. According to estimations from the danish state (2014) the global middle class is
expected to grow by 3 billion people until 2030. These numbers reﬂect an ampliﬁed need for doubling
the infrastructure and the number of buildings worldwide in 40 years [Danish Government (2014)].
Figure 2: Statistics for the construction industry (adapted and translated from Birgisdottir (2015)
In global context, construction sector accounts for approximately 40% of energy use, 30% of energy-
related Green House Gas (GHG) emissions, 12% of water use, 40% of waste, while 10% of the workforce
are working on the ﬁeld [UNEP (2015)]. Consequently, changes in the built environment can contribute
signiﬁcantly in tackling severe environmental challenges [UNEP (2015)]. Considering that two billion
urban inhabitants are expected to be added by 2030, mainly in the rapidly growing cities of Asia, Africa
and Latin America, there will soon be an urgent need for more sustainable buildings that can improve
the sustainability performance of the whole nation [UNEP (2015)].
In the European level, the building industry is a signiﬁcant pillar of the economy, generating 10% of GDP
and employing 8% of the workforce in the Union [European commission (2007)],[Birgisdottir (2015)].
However construction and use of buildings also are responsible for 42% of EU ﬁnal energy consumption
[EC (2011)], 35% of all greenhouse gas emissions [European commission (2007)], more than 50% of all
extracted materials [EC (2011)] and for 20-35% of the environmental impacts in several major categories,
such as global warming, eutrophication, acidiﬁcation, resource and water consumption and waste gener-
ation [JRC (2006)],[Birgisdottir et al. (2013)].
In case of Denmark, energy consumption for the heating, ventilation and lighting of buildings accounts
for 40% of the overall energy consumption [EFKM (2014)] and demolition waste was the biggest waste
stream, accounting approximately for the 35% of the total waste volume [Miljøministeriet (2016)].
9
==PAGE:12==
Consequently sustainable construction can boost resource eﬃciency and tackle environmental challenges,
while simultaneously mitigating potential future costs. As a result, working systematically within the
mindset of sustainability in order to develop eﬀective, eﬃcient and competitive solutions in the future is
increasingly necessary [Danish Government (2014)].
2.2
Construction waste management
It is clear that resource management in the construction industry is far from optimized and thus in need
of a more thorough analysis. Such an analysis of the modern waste management tactics in Denmark is
presented below.
2.2.1
Waste streams
In Figure 3, there are demonstrated the distributions of the waste fractions of the total waste volume
of Denmark from 2012 to 2014.
Waste from construction industry are the largest waste stream and
constitute approximately up to the 1/3 of the total volume. Among them, concrete, iron and steel and
bricks are the largest fragments while also there are large quantities of mixed construction and demolition
wastes, including concrete, bricks, tiles and ceramics, accounting for approximately 400,000 tonnes in 2014
[Miljøministeriet (2016)].
Figure 3: Primary waste generated (excl. soil) distributed on main sources and treatment form [Miljømin-
isteriet (2016)]
Furthermore construction waste accounts for the second largest share for recycling, 87%, but it is stated
that this is primarily due to the recovery of the crushed building waste and asphalt [Miljøministeriet
(2016)]. However, this percentage refers to weight and doesn’t account the huge loss of quality and eco-
nomic value in the process [Jensen and Sommer (2016)].
"Concrete crunched into road ﬁll in fact has an average value of ﬁve euro per ton, a value 50 times lower
than that of a new concrete element. Almost all building waste today is being downcycled into the lowest
value possible. [Jensen and Sommer (2016)]".
Concrete is being crushed at a percentage rate of 90% [Vandkunsten A/S (2016)]. Similarly also crashed
brick is usually used as a substitute for gravel and stone [Miljøministeriet (2015)]. The objective of in-
creasing the quality of recycling materials, by removing hazardous substances, will decrease the overall
recycling percentage and the present objective is to maintain it to at least 70% [Miljøministeriet (2016)].
10
==PAGE:13==
Even further, the modern way of handling waste is also severely questioned by a research conducted quite
in the past, when a government research organisation in Australia named CSIRO compared the energy
expenditure of using recycled concrete to the use of new quarried aggregate [Crowther (2000a)]. The case
study concluded that the use of recycled crushed concrete used 37% more energy and this was primarily
due to increased transportation requirements [MacSporran (1994)]. Although the assessment was limited
to energy consumption, it still shows that this way of recycling might not even be an environmentally
beneﬁcial option [Crowther (2000a)]. This study of MacSporran [1994] stated that:
“with all other factors remaining unchanged the recycling option becomes favourable (breakeven) when the
(demolished) concrete rubble has to be transported to a (landﬁll) tip more than 13km from the demolition
site”.

Recycled building materials enclose generally less embodied energy than new materials and recycling
oﬀers easily obtainable manufacturing feedstock [Durmisevic (2006)]. Recycled feedstocks usually require
substantially less energy than virgin resources [Mumma (1995)]. Table 1 demonstrates an overview of
energy needed for new material production and energy savings by using recycled materials.
2.3
Legislative initiatives
The Directive 2008/98/EC set the framework regarding waste management in EU. The hierarchy re-
garding the suitability of waste treatment methods is demonstrated in Figure 4 and set reuse as the top
priority regarding waste management.
Figure 4: "Moving up the waste hier-
archy [European Commission (2010)]"
"Roadmap to a Resource Eﬃcient Europe" [EC (2011)] iden-
tiﬁes housing industry as one of the three key sectors which
are typically responsible for 70-80% of all environmental im-
pacts.
Importance of energy eﬃciency and renewable en-
ergy use in buildings is strengthened and supplemented by
resource eﬃciency and lifecycle economic assessments [Bir-
gisdottir et al. (2013)].
The overarching aim is that by
2020 the renovation and construction of infrastructure projects
will be more resource eﬃcient and will utilize a life-cycle
perspective as common practice [EC (2011)].
New build-
ings shall be nearly zero energy and extremely material ef-
ﬁcient, while policies regarding renovation of existing build-
ings will be set so that they can become modernized [EC
(2011)].
In danish context, the danish waste plan from 2013-2018 named "Danmark uden aﬀald" also takes into
consideration the RIO +20 international environmental agreements and the EU’s 7th EAP, which is the
11
==PAGE:14==
new framework for EU’s environmental policy and green targets for 2020 and the long-term vision up to
2050 [Miljøministeriet (2014)]. Rio + 20 Conference highlighted the need to trigger a green transforma-
tion worldwide, including also the commitment to the 3Rs (reduce, reuse and recycle) [UN (2012)], while
the Commission’s proposal for a 7th Environment Action Programme, entitled "Living well, Reviews
within the limits of our planet", focuses on the gains of the circular economy [EC (2012)].
"Danmark uden aﬀald" highlights the main direction of Denmark on waste management in the long term
which is to recycle much more waste than incinerating, while minimizing landﬁlling [Miljøministeriet
(2013)]. Furthermore, the ongoing increase in prices for used resources is also noted as a motivating
factor to work on this direction as it is a potential new market on which danish corporations could thrive
[Miljøministeriet (2013)]. In the ﬁrst version of the resource plan, the Danish government highlighted the
importance of construction and demolition waste, as the biggest waste fraction, and the improvement of
the re-cycling quality of construction and demolition waste, such as concrete and brick, was highlighted
among the initiatives [Miljøministeriet (2013)]. Main aspects of this plan included the safe conduction
of waste management, the huge beneﬁts from reusing speciﬁc resources and the problem of downcycling
[Miljøministeriet (2013)]. The main target is to improve recycling quality by 2018 while simultaneously
maintaining the high recycling rate to at least 70% [Miljøministeriet (2014)].
In the latest version of the guide Miljøministeriet (2015), the importance of cooperation among stakehold-
ers is highlighted due to the complexity of the endeavor. Furthermore, on this direction, the design for
disassembly practice is also mentioned, while the use of two new indicators is established [Miljøministeriet
(2015)]:
• The number of buildings where the building materials are mapped prior to the demolition
• The fragment of construction and demolition waste which is reused for new purposes (materials
recovery)
2.4
Why buildings are demolished
Buildings were demolished in the European Union at an annual rate varying between 0.025% and 0.23%
of the total stock in 2003 [Meijer et al. (2009)]. Main reasons were often urban renewal, low occupancy
rates, lack of market demand or even prospect of satisfying higher building demand [Meijer et al. (2009)].
In general market demand and land prices are often the main reasons instead of technical quality. [Meijer
et al. (2009)]. Furthermore today’s fast-cycling trends regarding market needs make the prediction of
future scenarios of the buildings’ use highly uncertain [Durmisevic (2006)]. These uncertainties derive
not only by the future functional organisation needs, but from their use in general after 10 or 15 years
[Durmisevic (2006)].
According to a study in Holland, changing sequences in dwellings continue to increase, as dwellings with
design life of 50 years tend to start alterations within 3 years [RIGO (1999)]. A whole dwelling was
estimated to fully transform within 25 years and the pulse of change was forecasted that would accelerate
[RIGO (1999)]. According to the same research, in 73% of the cases, the initial spatial organisation of
dwellings didn’t meet the user needs [RIGO (1999)]. 30% of families in the studied buildings would like
to move because their dwellings can’t be adjusted to their needs and another 45% would stay if making
the adjustments was possible [RIGO (1999)]. Most frequent changes of the users were identiﬁed to be
the wall add or removal [RIGO (1999)].
The main obstacles for buildings’ transformation are often [Durmisevic (2006)]:
• Spatial inability to adapt in a new use concept
• Inﬂexibility of load-bearing structure
• Inﬂexibility of installation systems
• Lack of accessibility to installations
• Lack of space for new installations
All in all, lack of ﬂexibility of buildings regarding spatial organization and general use changes is a crucial
reason buildings are demolished, while simultaneously there is a tendency for quicker changes in market
needs.
12
==PAGE:15==
2.5
Circular economy
Today’s "take, make, dispose" linear economic model is based on large quantities of easily accessible
and cheap materials and energy. Considering that the annual consumption of fossil fuels, minerals and
biomass in Europe is estimated to double by 2050, and humanity already exceeds a range of planetary
boundaries, today’s production methods can cause an ecological and economic impasse [EU (2015)]. It is
estimated that maintaining the modern standard of living can be feasible in the future by consuming
only one tenth of the resources used now [EU (2015)].
The only way to achieve this is by "decoupling economic growth from resource use" [EU (2015)]. In
this framework the concept of circular economy emerged as a more appealing and viable alternative and
businesses are already exploring it [EMF (2015)]. Circular economy is:
"a continuous positive development cycle that preserves and enhances natural capital, optimises resource
yields, and minimises system risks by managing ﬁnite stocks and renewable ﬂows. It works eﬀectively at
every scale".
Circular economy is restorative by design, and aims to preserve permanently the highest possible value
and utility of products and materials [EMF (2015)]. The concept can be distinguished in a technical and
a biological cycle as in Figure 5. The system diagram demonstrates the continuous ﬂow of biological and
technical materials through the ‘value circle’ [EMF (2015)].
Figure 5: "Circular Economy System Diagram value: recovery for biological and technical materials
[EMF (2015)]"
13
==PAGE:16==
2.6
Designing buildings for disassembly
The building industry is a typical example of a linear economy and a ﬁeld with huge potentials regarding
resource eﬃciency. Circular economy principles highlight the importance of an improved handling of the
resources and a more holistic perspective of the lifecycle impacts and the resource ﬂow in buildings. In
contrast with current building practice that focus mainly on the act of construction, a new concept that
focuses on the act of deconstruction and the handling of the buildings’ parts after the end of the build-
ings life was introduced [Crowther (2000b)]. Deconstruction is the dismantling process of a building that
enables recycle or reuse of the materials [Rios et al. (2015)]. This process essentially alters the traditional
waste management processes in the building industry and can increase the material and component reuse
[Crowther (1999a)].
Figure 6: "Traditional building practice com-
pared to design for disassembly [Adapted from
Crowther (2000c)]"
Design for Disassembly (DfD) is a building prac-
tice that aims to:
"ease the deconstruction pro-
cesses and procedures through planning and design
[Rios
et
al.
(2015)]".
Consequently
DfD
tries
to close the loop in building management against
the traditional linear approach by designing build-
ings
that
can
facilitate
renovations
and
eventu-
ally
dismantlement
(in
parts
or
whole)
for
"re-
covery of systems, components and materials" and
parts
that
are
easier
to
be
rearranged,
recov-
ered, and reuse (ﬁgure 6) [US EPA (2008), Guy
and Ciarimboli (2006), Fletcher (2001a), Crowther
(2000c)].
Furthermore
it
can
be
also
consid-
ered as "a strategy to facilitate maintainability and
adaptability during the building’s operation period"
[Nordby (2009b)] and is directly linked with ﬂex-
ibility of buildings’ functionality and use that af-
fects crucially the lifespan of a building due to
inability
to
adapt
to
market
demands
and
user
needs [Durmisevic (2006)].
All in all DfD prin-
ciples
can
assist
environmental
savings
[Durmise-
vic (2006), Crowther (2000c), Sassi (2008), Thor-
mark
(2001)]
as
well
as
lifecycle
costs
savings
(Chini
et
al.
2003,
Sassi
2004,
Durmisevic
2006),
satisfy market needs for ﬂexibility [Durmi-
sevic (2006)] and provide social and human health
beneﬁts [Sassi (2004), Crowther (1999a), US EPA
(2008)].
Figure 7: "Integral chain management [Dorsthorst
et al. (2000)]"
Based on the circular economy system diagram re-
garding the technical metabolism, Figure 7 demon-
strates the new ﬂow of building materials that en-
ables numerous life cycle turns according to a spe-
ciﬁc hierarchy.
This speciﬁc ﬂow is called Delft
Ladder and adds both the material and the build-
ing cycle [J. Kristinsson (2001)]. Delft Ladder tries
to map all the stages and the material ﬂows and
match them to the appropriate action to prevent
waste [J. Kristinsson (2001)]. In this Figure, the
waste treatment methods are also added and pri-
oritized. Top priority in building management is
considered the prevention of waste and the build-
ing preservation as a whole [J. Kristinsson (2001)].
It is highlighted that this step must be considered
in the design phase [J. Kristinsson (2001)].

2.7
Time related building layers and service lifes of materials
As Durmisevic (2006) highlights:
"The system conﬁguration (..) deﬁnes a life cycle of buildings and their impact on the environment, the
economic system, and the quality of life. Thus, it is not only a type of material(s) but also an arrangement
of materials that determines the life cycle of buildings and their products".
Figure 8: The six S’s system [Brand (1994)]"
Stewart Brand introduced in 1994 the six S’s
system that describes buildings as a conjunc-
tion of “shearing layers of change” which are
in constant friction, as the faster changing lay-
ers are controlled by the slower changing [Guy
and Ciarimboli (2006), Brand (1994)].
Con-
sequently the ﬁrst step would be to decou-
ple the slow and fast changing layers [Kibert
et al. (2003)]. Durmisevic (2006) highlights that
the diﬀerent functional/
use
lifecycles are
only one dimension of the need for transforma-
tion.
Durmisevic (2006) introduced another dimension, the technical life cycles, which refer to the diﬀerent
materials that make up a system. For example, the window frame and the glass panel that are part of the
facade have diﬀerent durability and "therefore should be seen as an independent part of the structure".
Consequently, Durmisevic highlights that "Brand’s shearing levels of change that tears buildings apart
should be looked at beyond the use time levels of the building parts, since independence is also needed to
separate components, which have diﬀerent technical life cycle". Also Durmisevic pinpoints that "building
materials have diﬀerent life cycles and that durability of most of materials is longer than durability of
their functions forms the bottleneck for transformation".
In Danish context, Statens Byggeforskningsinstitut (SBi) in the 2013:30 report "Levetider af bygningsdele
ved vurdering af bæredygtighed og totaløkonomi", deﬁnes except from the functional and the technical
lifespans, the aesthetic service life and the economic service life. Aesthetic service life is deﬁned as
"the period starting when a material or component is installed in the building, to the time where it
is no longer economically justiﬁable to maintain and replace parts of the building component" [Matzen
(2017), Aagaard et al. (2013)]. Economic service life "is deﬁned as the period starting when a material
or component is installed in the building, to the time where it is no longer economically justiﬁable to
maintain and replace parts of the building component. The economic service life is determined by price
and interest rate conditions at the given moment. The economic service life will often be shorter than
the technical service lifetime" [Matzen (2017), Aagaard et al. (2013)]. These 4 lifespans form the actual
lifespan of a material according to SBi (2013) (Figure 9).
Figure 9: Example of distribution of lifetimes for a given building component [Translated from Aagaard
et al. (2013)]
15
==PAGE:18==
2.8
Prefabricated buildings
In general prefabrication oﬀers signiﬁcant potentials to DfD, has often crucial advantages against the on
site manufacturing. Regarding this project, one of the main expected business partners is Scandi Byg,
which is a Danish company that specializes in industrialized module buildings in timber and is subsidiary
of the MT Højgaard a/s.
One of the main advantages of working with prefabricated elements and even buildings compared to
traditional ways of constructing, is the ability to manufacture oﬀ-site. Consequently, the building site
will be cleaner and safer as "people work in a clean, warm, enclosed environment instead of having to
work up in very tall heights in all weathers, when it’s very cold, when it’s wet, when it’s dark sometimes"
[Horswill (2017)]. This fact can also lead to higher accuracy and quality [Hechler et al. (2016)]. This is
the case not only for the main building but also for the installations and the doors or windows [Horswill
(2017)]. Furthermore except from safer, the assembly and disassembly process becomes quicker [Jensen
and Sommer (2016)]. In addition standardization of components’ production at standard sizes can mini-
mize wastage and oﬀ-cuts, while simultaneously increases the reuse potential [Fletcher (2001a)].
On the other hand prefabricated buildings may imply long transport distances that increase the cost and
the environmental impacts due to fuel emissions, while transportation could also imply limitations to the
sizes of the elements [Nordby (2009b)]. Moreover although prefabricated elements are often rated highly
for their reuse potential, in some cases their recycling potential is quite low as it depends from their
synthesis [Sassi (2002)].
Ultimately, regarding the construction materials, for steel and timber buildings, prefabrication of elements
and even whole buildings is already the dominant way of manufacturing. In case of concrete, the use of
prefabricated elements oﬀers naturally higher disassembly potential but only if members can be joined
with mechanical connections. Further analysis on the challenges regarding prefab was performed through
the interviews and the workshop, while beneﬁts from prefabrication regarding the diﬀerent materials is
further discussed in Chapter 4.1.
2.9
Demolition versus deconstruction
Typically today only selective deconstruction takes place, according to ﬁnancial and legislative criteria,
and this typical demolition plan is presented in the left side of Figure 10. However, implementing DfD
guidelines would shift this process to the future deconstruction plan, that is presented in the right side of
the Figure, and would practically be quite similar to the ﬁrst in terms of tools and techniques used [Jensen
and Sommer (2016)]. This deconstruction plan was presented by Kingo Karlsen for "De Fire Styrelser"
project and it is projected that 70% of the concrete will be directly reused as structural elements [Jensen
and Sommer (2016)].
Apart from the huge ﬁnancial and environmental beneﬁts of ‘De Fire Styrelser’ case, beneﬁts from decon-
struction are even more. Speciﬁcally, Naber (2012) ) discussed the important beneﬁts of deconstruction
comparing to demolition on several areas. Speciﬁcally, deconstruction is beneﬁcial regarding dust pro-
duction, vibration levels and safety of surroundings, while noise levels are similar [Naber (2012)].
On the other hand, deconstruction process has to be carefully engineered as safety of workers is in
stake [Naber (2012)].
16
==PAGE:19==
Figure 10: Current demolition plan and Future deconstruction plan according to Kingo Karlsen [Jensen
and Sommer (2016), Figure by Diraoui and Larsen (2016)]
2.10
Assessing environmental performance of buildings
During the previous decades, the impacts of the building industry were dominated by the energy use in
the operation phase. However the adopted legislation regarding energy consumption and the simultane-
ous greening of the energy grid mix raised the relative importance of the rest impact sources [EC (2011)].
Characteristically in an average building in UK, the corresponding ratio of energy consumption in the
use phase comparing to the embodied energy is approximately 60/40 and in the future the embedded en-
ergy will probably become the most dominating factor [EC (2011)]. Consequently the prevalence of high
performing "low-energy" buildings raised the importance of building components in the environmental
performance of buildings [Sartori and Hestnes (2007)]. LCA is established as the most appropriate method
to estimate the potential environmental impacts of buildings [Blengini and Di Carlo (2010)] and the opti-
mization of environmental performance of building envelopes with the use of LCA has been acknowledged
as a priority [Azari et al. (2016)].
17
==PAGE:20==
In danish context, the Green Building Council (GBC) Denmark was founded in 2010, aiming to support
green transition by spreading sustainability in the construction industry and assist it to thrive in the
sustainability ﬁeld globally, while setting the corresponding standards [GBC (2017b)]. As the role model
scheme for the danish certiﬁcation scheme, the German DGNB International certiﬁcation was selected
and built upon according to danish legislation and conditions [Rasmussen et al. (2013)]. DGNB was
preferred among the top international sustainability standards (DGNB, LEED, BREEAM and HQE)
because it is considered that it supports innovation, it has a more holistic perspective as it considers all
the aspects of sustainability and also partly for being future-proof [GBC (2017a)]. Furthermore DGNB
International aims to fully comply with the European CEN/TC 350 standards [Rasmussen et al. (2013)].
Both schemes use the LCA methodology but the main diﬀerence is that the CEN/TC 350 standards uses
a broader framework for evaluating sustainability comparing to DGNB International that has benchmark
values and system boundary settings, making the DGNB International scheme more a translation in an
applicable scheme of the CEN/TC 350 standards [Rasmussen et al. (2013)].
Consequently according to the literature review and the danish certiﬁcation schemes and legislation, LCA
is the optimal choice for performing the environmental assessment of the case house that was designed
through the InnoBYG project. Last but not least and regarding the speciﬁc project, LCA enables the
comparison with the houses that are considered as the most appropriate to be compared, as there are
already available the corresponding LCAs.
2.10.1
What is LCA
"Life cycle assessment (LCA) is the process of evaluating the eﬀects that a product has on the environment
over the entire period of its life cycle" [UNEP (2003)]. International Reference Life Cycle Data System
(ILCD) Handbook from the European Union Joint Reasearch Centre (JRC) provides detailed technical
guidance on LCA based on the ISO 14040 and 14044:2006 standards. The LCA framework as deﬁned by
the ILCD Handbook is the following:
Figure 11: The framework of LCA [JRC (2006)]
According to the Handbook, life cycle inventory analysis "catalogues the ﬂows crossing the system bound-
ary and provides the starting point for life cycle impact assessment". Goal and scope are deﬁning the
product system are crucial for the interpretation and the use of the results.
2.10.2
LCA of buildings
The European Committee for Standardisation (CEN) released in 2011 a new standard, EN 15978, for
measuring the environmental sustainability of buildings. EN 15978 deﬁnes the phases of the building life
cycle that should be included in an Life Cycle Assessment (LCA), and which processes are included in
18
==PAGE:21==
each phase [Birgisdottir et al. (2013)]. In case of Denmark, DGNB certiﬁcation includes broadly the same
processes, as DGNB wants to fulﬁll this standard as far as possible and these processes are presented in
Figure 12. As the Figure shows, DGNB includes less stages than CEN/TC 350, as at ﬁrst it was decided
to include only the processes of the greatest importance for practical reasons and also to handle lack of
data [Birgisdottir et al. (2013)].
Figure 12: The phases of a building’s lifecycle and with X are noted the ones that are also included in
DGNB certiﬁcate [GBCD (2014)]

3
Methodology
3.1
"Rules of Thumb (RoT)"
3.1.1
Deﬁnition
Figure 13:
The included layers of
Brand (1994) for the RoT.
Rules of Thumb" are guidelines for implementing Design for Dis-
assembly and are the result of wide literature review. Such ex-
amples of guidelines are widely presented in the literature on the
ﬁeld.
RoT focus on the "Structure", "Skin", "Services (Instal-
lations)", "Site" and "Space plan" of buildings according to the
classiﬁcation of the building layers of Brand (1994) (Figure 13).
"Space plan" is included in terms of building adaptability to dif-
ferent functions and uses of space. The "Stuﬀ(furniture, textiles
etc)" are considered to be out of the scope of this report and thus
aren’t discussed.
3.1.2
Aim of the Rules
The guidelines aim primarily to "close the loop" in the building industry through the proper implemen-
tation of DfD. The guidelines try to shift the current EoL situation which is dominated by the waste and
downcycling of valuable resources to a more sustainable EoL as it is described by the recycling hierarchy.
The ﬁnancial and time perspectives, which are strongly interrelated, were also included as RoT aim to
assist the eﬃcient and economically feasible assembly and disassembly processes. In general, RoT are
aimed to be used as a tool for the easy communication of the DfD principles to professionals and as a brief
guide for implementing DfD. According to Nordby (2009a), ideally a list of DfD guidelines should aim to
help the easier communication of the guidelines to an audience, while it could simultaneously serve as an
assessment tool of existing solutions and as a checklist for the designers after the necessary adaptations.
3.1.3
Structure of the rules
RoT in the dominating majority of the literature have a generic character because "the huge variety of
materials and types of products used in buildings requires many diﬀerent approaches to designing for
deconstruction that, nevertheless are frequently generic in nature" [Addis and Schouten (2004)]. The
book of Addis and Schouten (2004) is the only case in the literature in which a wide range of cases and
guidelines are adapted for most of the building layers and for several materials.
Consequently for this report, the generic guidelines were preferred similar to the literature and due to
the limited literature on guidelines for speciﬁc materials and the limited time resources. However it was
also decided to perform an adaptation of the generic guidelines for the most common structural frames
and for installations and to present them brieﬂy along with some examples of application. The structural
frames were selected due to their importance -as the choice of the rest of the elements has to be adapted
on them-, and also they are interesting to be examined as they perform quite diﬀerently with regards to
DfD. The adaptation to the installations took place as they were considered of high interest for the wider
project from the workshop and due to their very diﬀerent nature comparing to the rest of the building
parts.
As a result these two sets of guidelines were considered:
1. Generic guidelines
2. Speciﬁc guidelines for common structural frames (steel, wood and concrete) and for installations
3.1.3.1
Structure of the general "Rules of Thumb"
Through the literature, several ways of
structuring the guidelines were identiﬁed and aimed to assist the designers working with them. The
diﬀerent sets of guidelines that derive from the literature, don’t diﬀer essentially on their context but
mainly on their categorization method. A common contradicting point in the literature that leads to a
variety of structures is the deﬁnition of the hierarchy of recycling for the buildings.
20
==PAGE:23==
Hierarchy of recycling
Crowther (2001) concluded that understanding the hierarchy of recycling is necessary for thorough un-
derstanding of DfD and as a result structured the guidelines according to the "technical level of reuse".
Similar approaches are followed by Sassi (2002) and Addis and Schouten (2004). These classiﬁcations aim
to target a particular outcome, as for example increased rates of material recycling by prioritizing the
corresponding guidelines [Crowther (2000b)]. This structuring technique could also be considered similar
to the "scale of application" [Crowther (2000b)].
On the other hand Fletcher (2001a) builds directly on industrial ecology and uses the four end-of-life sce-
narios similar to industrial ecologists: "reuse", "repair", "reconditioning" and "recycling" of materials.
The scenarios are grouped into material level (recycling) and product level (reuse, repair, recondition-
ing), while a building level is added on the model in order to include so the "time related building layers"
theory and adjust that way their analysis to the built environment. Similar approaches are followed also
by other researchers, such as Durmisevic (2006) and Nordby (2009a).
Crowther (2001) opposes to Fletcher (2001a) that the sub-assemblies of the components may be out of
the direct control of the building designer and as a result it would be appropriate to combine product
repair and remanufacture to the production of "new" components. Consequently the technical results can
be used for deﬁning the EoL scenarios and this correspondence is demonstrated in the following Table.
Table 2: Possible "technical results" and the corresponding End of Life or "technical level of reuse"
scenarios proposed by Crowther (2000c)
Technical results
EoL scenarios / Technical level of reuse
Reuse of a whole building
Building reuse
Production of a "new" building
Component reuse
Production of "new" components
Material reuse for producing a new component
Production of "new" materials
Material recycling
Categorization methods in the literature
The main ways of dividing guidelines for DfD are presented in the following Table.
Table 3: Criteria for structuring DfD guidelines and the corresponding reference
Criteria for structuring guidelines for DfD
Reference
Component-Building-Industry & Criteria
Nordby (2009b)
Material-System-Building & Use-Technical Lifecycles
Durmisevic (2006)
Technical levels of reuse (EoL scenarios) & Building layer & Material
Addis and Schouten (2004)
No categorization - Assessment of technical levels of reuse side by side
Crowther (2001)
Technical levels of reuse (EoL scenarios)
Crowther (2000b), Sassi (2002)
Material-Product-System
Fletcher (2001a)
Scale of application (materials - connections - building)
Dowie-Bhamra (1997)
Technical beneﬁts (ease of handling / separation etc)
Jovane et al. (1993)
Chronological order
Kahmeyer and Leicht (1991)
The variety of diﬀerent structures demonstrates that there is no widely accepted way of structuring the
guidelines. In addition, as Crowther (2000b) highlights, "the complexity of the design process makes the
development of any design tool or set of guidelines diﬃcult. Speciﬁcally, some of these structures are
considered not appropriate for buildings as for example using the chronological order as building design
is an iterative process [Crowther (2001)]. Regarding the cases of Dowie-Bhamra (1997) and Jovane et al.
(1993), only limited information were available through third parties.
In the rest of the cases, a main diﬀerence is that Addis and Schouten (2004), Crowther (2001) and Sassi
(2002) include the desired EoL scenarios (e.g. recycling) for deﬁning diﬀerent groups of guidelines. This
structure allows the prioritizing of speciﬁc guidelines in order to achieve a speciﬁc EoL scenario. Speciﬁ-
cally Addis and Schouten (2004) has one of the most analytic structures that could easily help any user to
21
==PAGE:24==
work with DfD would be the triple "EoL, layer and material perspective". This structure can facilitate an
extended analysis for a range of materials and products, along with guidelines on how to combine them
according to the desired EoL scenario. That kind of analysis needs though extended time frame and wide
cooperation of professionals, while the literature is quite limited. In case of Sassi (2002), the guidelines
derive through an assessment tool that assesses the suitability for the desired EoL scenarios. . However
this structure doesn’t ﬁt to the aims of the RoT. In general, a negative aspect of this categorization is
that some methods that apply in all occasions are often repeated for each EoL scenario ("no hazardous
materials" guideline in Sassi (2002)), or for each layer (same guideline in Addis and Schouten (2004)).
Crowther (2000b) suggested similarly to the previously mentioned authors a range of guidelines according
for each desired EoL scenario. However, Crowther (2001) concluded that this type of categorisation "was
eventually considered to be undesirable due to the exclusivity of the categorisation method". Instead
Crowther (2001) suggested that the more appropriate way of representing "the relationship between prin-
ciples and the recycling hierarchy, would be one which allows for principles to be relevant to all end-of-life
scenarios". As a result a simple enlisting of the guidelines and assessing each one for its contribution on
each desired EoL scenario would be the optimal structure.
In another case, Durmisevic (2006) considers the use and technical life cycles of building elements and
their relation so that guidelines can adapt to them. However as already explained in chapter 2.7, the
lifetime of a building component depends in practice by a wide range of factors and Aagaard et al. (2013)
and Aagaard et al. (2013) suggests that aesthetic and economical lifespans shall also be considered. Con-
sequently, the structure proposed by Durmisevic (2006) could be considered as inappropriate, at least in
a danish context, as it omits signiﬁcant factors and it also could be proved quite complex in implemen-
tation, while the lack of lack of data could be insurmountable.
Fletcher (2001a) on the other hand, presents purely guidelines for each level (material - product - build-
ing) without examining the exact EoL scenarios each of them assist, not enabling so the user to work with
a speciﬁc EoL scenario. Nordby (2009b) aims to create a "multi-purpose" system that could serve also as
an assessment tool. EoL scenarios are demonstrated similar to Crowther (2001) ) but that guidelines are
not assigned to them. The grouping of guidelines under speciﬁc "criteria" (e.g. "ﬂexible connections") are
an interesting grouping that could assist the easier communication of guidelines. However since grouping
is limited to 2-3 guidelines, it could maybe just add further complexity. Furthermore, a disadvantage
of this method is that the general categorization of component - construction - industry as leads to the
repetition of guidelines in both "component" and "construction level".
Concluded structure
Based on the aims of the thesis and the mentioned analysis, it was concluded that the categorization
of Crowther (2001) is the most appropriate as it enables the prioritization of EoL scenarios and is an
eﬃcient way to present the guidelines as it avoids repetition and unnecessary complexity. As a result,
all the general RoT are assessed for their contribution to each desired EoL scenario or "technical level of
reuse" as Crowther (2001).
Considering the relatively big number of the principles and in order to avoid a chaotic presentation, the
guidelines were splitted to diﬀerent tables. These tables shouldn’t be considered as a catego-
rization of the guidelines and the diﬀerent tables aren’t intended to be used individually.
The tables are splitted according to the typical "Material - Component - Building" classiﬁcation, while
"Connections" were also added as a separate category due to the numerous relevant guidelines. When a
guideline refers only e.g. to components, it is clearly stated.
Relevancy
Ultimately, the contribution of a guideline to a speciﬁc EoL scenario was assessed to be: "Highly relevant",
"Relevant" or Not Relevant "NR", similar to Crowther (2001). "Highly relevant  " is considered
when the guideline is mandatory ("Don’t use hazardous materials" for "Material recycling") or makes
critical diﬀerence for a speciﬁc EoL scenario ("Manageable weight and size of materials and components"
for "Material reuse").
"Relevant •".
When the guideline would be helpful towards a scenario and
even mandatory under circumstances ("Accessibility to connections" could be mandatory for "Material
22
==PAGE:25==
recycling" only for diﬀerent materials). Furthermore it could be signiﬁcant but only for a limited range
of materials ("Chemical bonds weaker than materials connected" for "Material reuse" as for brick walls.
3.1.3.2
Structure of the "Rules of Thumb" for speciﬁc structural frames and "Services
layer (installations)"
These rules are also mentioned in this report as material-speciﬁc guidelines for the structural frames
or layer-speciﬁc for the installations. These guidelines are examples of guidelines that are referring to
speciﬁc structural frames and layers of the building.
These guidelines are mostly adaptations of the
generic ones. Consequently they are just enlisted as examples and don’t follow the previous structure in
order to avoid repeating and adding complexity. Furthermore they are also presented along with some
examples of existing projects and interesting technical solutions.
3.1.4
Assessment tools
Literature review identiﬁed several assessment tools that are based on some of these guidelines and try
mainly to assess the DfD potential of diﬀerent technical solutions that have already been designed. The
tools diﬀerentiate on the way they are deriving, their applications, their structure and the way they
calculate the ﬁnal score. These tools are introduced in Chapter 4.1.4 and more analytically in Appendix
A. The tools and a list of potential improvements are further discussed in the Discussion Chapter.
3.1.5
Search tools
The main search engines for the literature that were used are Google scholar and DTU Findit. Further
sources of knowledge regarding the guidelines were the interviews and the workshop on DfD. The main
keywords for the search were: "Design for Disassembly in buildings", "Guidelines for designing buildings
for disassembly/ deconstruction", "Design for Disassembly in architecture", "DFD", "Reuse of building/
structural materials", "Reuse of concrete/ steel/ timber elements", "Guidelines/ Key principles / Strate-
gies regarding Design for Disassembly", "Flexible structures", "Design for Flexibility / Adaptability",
"Circular economy in the building industry", "Closing the loop in the building industry", "Construc-
tion waste management" and others. Often the search was performed by using the same terms in other
languages.
3.2
Framing the InnoBYG project
The initial aim of the wider project of InnoBYG was to map and discuss the challenges and opportunities
regarding DfD and especially on the case of prefabricated elements. The process was speciﬁed and planned
by the two knowledge institutions, DTU and KADK, and executed in their behalf from the writer of this
thesis, Msc student of DTU, and Line Kjær Frederiksen, research assistant at KADK. The process had two
main parts. Firstly, an interview round with representatives from a wide range of stakeholders identiﬁed
the main challenges. Next, the challenges were introduced and further analyzed during a workshop that
took place on April 14, at the Royal Institute.
3.2.1
Interviewing process
The initial round of interviews aimed to include representatives from all the potentially aﬀected parts of
the building industry. These parts were identiﬁed to be the following:
• Architects and Engineers
• Clients
• Contractors
• Policy regulators
• Product Manufacturers
Most of the interviewees were architects and engineers with experience on DfD. Martha Lewis specializes
in the development of architectural design with a focus on sustainability and materials, and works for
23
==PAGE:26==
Henning Larsen Architects [Lewis (2017b)]. Mrs Lewis is both LEED Green Associate, DGNB Interna-
tional Consultant and authorized DGNB Auditor [Lewis (2017b)]. Duncan Horswill is the Director in
BIG Engineering and was previously CCO at Søren Jensen Engineers. Horswill’s approach to engineering
focuses on integrating technical knowledge into the architectural design process and focuses in the role
of engineering, technology and sustainability in building design [BIG (2017)]. Jørn Kisslinger is Director
of Sustainability at JJW Architects, Mauro Lucardi is an architect at JJW with wide experience on
sustainability and project manager, and Mathilde Landgren is a PhD student working on integrating
sustainable design methods [JJW (2017)]. Leonora Charlotte Malabi Larsen is sustainability consultant
at MT Højgaard and her master thesis on DfD, along with Sara Diraoui - student assistant at MT Høj-
gaard -, won the ﬁrst prize of Green Challenge 2016 for "Best Masters Thesis Concept". Leonora mainly
analyzed the inﬂuence of DfD in the DGNB certiﬁcation scheme.
As a representative of the clients, Henrik Bang, the "Secretariat" of Bygherreforeningen, was interviewed.
Bygherreforeningen is an association of construction clients in Denmark and represents a signiﬁcant frac-
tion of the market [Bygherreforeningen (2013)]. Next, Michael H. Nielsen, the director of "Dansk Byggeri"
was interviewed. Dansk Byggeri or "Danish Construction Association" represents "major building con-
tractors, small and medium-sized construction companies and manufacturers of building components",
corresponding to approximately 6.000 companies and 90.000 workers [Dansk Byggeri (2017)]. Their aim
is to create and sustain the conditions in which the member companies can compete and prosper and
promote their members’ political interests [Dansk Byggeri (2017)]. Behalf of the building product man-
ufacturers, Kasper Sánchez Vibæk from Kvadrat Soft Cells - disassemblable acoustic panels producers
- explained challenges and opportunities of DfD from a manufacturing perspective [Kvadrat Soft Cells
(2017)].
The interviews weren’t based on a strict questionnaire but instead, they were adapted to the perspec-
tives of the interviewees and to their main expertise and experience. Some questions were about well
known challenges that derive by the literature such as material choice and detailing. Ultimately, all the
interviewees were further asked about how these challenges change when working exclusively with prefab-
ricated elements. The full transcripts of the interviews are demonstrated in Appendix B. Analytically,
the interviewees were the following.
