3.2.2
The workshop
The workshop was named "DESIGN FOR DISASSEMEBLY i præfabrikeret byggeri" and took place in
KADK on April 14, 2017. The workshop was hosted by KADK and DTU as part of the InnoBYG project
and there were 33 attendants from both private and public sector, universities and relevant organizations.
The aim of the ﬁrst part of the workshop was to make all the attendants familiar with the DfD concept,
the progress on the ﬁeld and a range of existing applications. The second part aimed to further discuss
the challenges on the ﬁeld and receive proposals regarding the next steps of the project in the second part.
Furthermore a general aim of the workshop was to identify the interested stakeholders from the industry
in order to cooperate with them in the next steps. The schedule of the workshop is demonstrated in
Figure 14.
24
==PAGE:27==
Figure 14: Presentation of the workshop’s schedule [Slide of Ulrik Stylsvig Madsen]
In the ﬁrst part a presentation of the project from Ulrik Stylsvig Madsen, lektor in KADK, took place
along with presentations of relevant cases and projects from Line Tebering, Jeppe K. Hjort and Casper
Østergaard Christensen, while Kasper S. Vibæk presented DfD from a producer’s perspective by demon-
strating the Kvadrat Soft Cells’ acoustic panels case.
In the second part, the attendants were asked to mention their opinion about the most signiﬁcant chal-
lenges regarding diﬀerent aspects of DfD and propose ways that the project could handle them. These
aspects were decided by Ulrik S. Madsen, Line K. Frederiksen, research assistant in KADK, and the
writer of this thesis, and were considered that can include all the signiﬁcant challenges mentioned in the
interviews. The 4 aspects that were chosen were:
• Market potential
• Detailing
• Materials
• Materials’ Lifespans
"Market potential" focused on the challenges that are related with the business case of DfD and tried
to map the most signiﬁcant market - economic characteristics of DfD. In addition, "Market potential"
included the impacts that DfD can have on the current business models of all the stakeholders of the
building industry. "Detailing" included technical challenges regarding both structural and architectural
detailing and how these are changes in case of prefabrication. "Materials’ lifespans" were separated from
"Materials" due to the importance and the wide range of the deriving challenges. "Materials" focused on
the rest of the desired material properties and their relation to prefabrication on material and component
level. "Prefabrication", which is also considered a signiﬁcant aspect, was set as a supplementary question
in the cases of Materials and Detailing. "Product manufacturing" was also partially considered within
the other topics as it isn’t one of the main focus points of the InnoBYG project. For each aspect of
DfD there were 3 questions with the 3rd being always on how should the project handle these challenges.
Each question was accompanied by a range of keywords to further deﬁne the question and assist the
conversation.
The attendants were divided in groups of 4 or 5 people and in all tables were both architects and engineers
in order to have a more productive discussion with a more holistic perspective. Each group had one hour
to discuss the speciﬁc aspect of the DfD case that was assigned to them.
3.2.3
The case study - "DfD House"
As part of the greater project a case study is derived. The case study house was designed according to the
DfD principles and was assessed for its environmental performance during this thesis. The "DfD house"
as it is being called in this thesis, was designed by Jonas Nyholm, as part of his thesis for his Bachelor
degree in Architectural Engineering from DTU Diplom Bygningsdesign. The design of the house was
25
==PAGE:28==
reviewed by Søren Nielsen (Vandkunsten A/S) and was also further reviewed by Rasmus Kristian Holst
(Søren Jensen Engineering), Lotte Bjerregaard Jensen (DTU) and other. For the design of the house
the recommendations of the workshop and the DfD guidelines were acknowledged as much as possible.
For this reason most of the participants in the design of the building were already familiar with DfD or
became through literature research on the corresponding topics, the workshop and the ongoing meetings.
One signiﬁcant parameter during the project was also to identify and incorporate the interests of the
industrial stakeholders. This fact assisted to the consideration also of the challenges regarding the market
potential of DfD, while industrial stakeholders also provided invaluable real life experience and scientiﬁc
knowledge. The main structural material was chosen unanimously to be Cross Laminated Timber (CLT)
as it is a rapidly emerging material that can be used as an alternative for concrete and also because timber
structures have a high DfD potential. These facts combined, reveal the high sustainability beneﬁts that
could derive in the construction industry.
3.3
LCA Methodology
For this project, as already mentioned in Chapter 2.10, the LCA method and the framework proposed
by the DGNB standard is acknowledged as the most appropriate method for assessing the environmental
performance of buildings and it is introduced in these chapters as well.
In this Chapter, the basic
characteristics of the LCA that is performed for the case house are introduced.
3.3.1
Intended application
The intended application of this LCA is to:
• Quantify the environmental impacts from the case study house
• Identify the hotspots in its environmental performance
• Assess the inﬂuence in impacts from the reuse of building elements
• Compare the environmental performance of the case study house with the Reference House and the
Upcycle House
3.3.2
Tool and database for assessment
For this assessment the LCA software that was used was OpenLCA 1.6 from GreenDelta and was the
latest available version. The software was preferred due to its well known credibility, user friendliness
and also for the fact that it is available without any subscription. The fact that OpenLCA is a freeware
makes the developed model to be easily accessible to all the partners for any checks, changes, expansions
or further improvements. The used database was Ecoinvent 3.1.. For the reference houses the DGNB
database and the DGNBlca tool were used [Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013)].
3.3.3
Limitations
Method-related
A signiﬁcant limitation for this study was the tight time schedule as the case study was designed in
parallel to this thesis. Furthermore, the choice of data was usually generic, on European level, and con-
sequently the ﬁnal material choice from speciﬁc producers may lead to some discrepancies. Ultimately,
the results could be diﬀerent if another method was used.
Assumption-related
LCA is a complex and data heavy method. Given the fact that the ﬁnal material choice from speciﬁc
producers didn’t take place on time, several rough assumptions and estimations were necessary in order
to calculate key numbers. The list of assumptions is demonstrated in Appendix M, they are analyzed as
well in Chapter 5.4.3.
26
==PAGE:29==
Impact-related
The overall environmental performance of the comparison with the Upcycle house is limited on their
carbon footprint as only this category is available from Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013). Although the
carbon footprint is often a representative category and also helps the communication of the results to the
wider audience, this fact poses a severe problem as major diﬀerences in the rest of the impact categories
may be neglected. For the reference house, more results were available from Rasmussen (2015) report,
that refers to an updated version of the reference houses so that it complies with the energy requirements
of 2015. However this report didn’t include the results for the toxicity categories. In addition due to the
use of EPDs for CLT and wood ﬁber insulation, the results of the case house for the toxicity categories do
not include the impacts from the production of these products. Toxicity categories may follow a diﬀerent
trend from the rest impacts categories and could lead so to signiﬁcant ﬁndings according to Rasmussen
et al. (2013).
3.3.4
Functional unit
In order to enable the comparison among buildings with diﬀerent gross areas, the functional unit is set
as:
"The environmental impacts from the building materials per 1 mˆ2 of the building’s gross ﬂoor area per
year."
3.3.5
Service life and reference study period
The lifespans of the materials were based on the SBi data, as they were documented by Aagaard et al.
(2013) similar to the LCAs of the reference buildings, the Reference house and the Upcycle house, for
which also SBi performed assessments [Rasmussen (2015), Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013)]. As already
mentioned in Chapter 2.7, Aagaard et al. (2013) there is a range of lifespans that aﬀect the actual lifespan
of the elements: functional service life, technical service life, aesthetic service life and economic service
life. The ﬁnal tables of SBi are based on the following Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013):
• "Economic service life: No evaluation is made of mean service life, only an evaluation of the relative
importance of the shortest of the evaluated, relevant mean service lives. Relevant only for SfB main
groups 3-6."
• "Aesthetic service life: No evaluation is made of mean service life, only an evaluation of the relative
importance of the shortest of the evaluated, relevant mean service lives. Relevant only for SfB main
groups 3 and 4, and for visible building elements in main group 2."
• "Functional service life: For each SfB group of building elements a functional mean service life is
estimated."
• "Technical service life: For each building element and material a technical mean service life is
estimated."
3.3.6
System boundaries
For the LCA of the case study house all the phases that are included in the DGNB certiﬁcate were used,
except from the energy consumption as no estimation of the energy consumption for the case building
was available [Figure 15]. However for the Upcycle House, only the carbon footprint from the materials
is known anyway.
27
==PAGE:30==
Figure 15: The system boundaries for the LCA of the case study house
3.3.6.1
Production phase (A1-A3)
Production phase includes the extraction of raw materials, their transportation to the production site
and ultimately the production into products. These three modules were merged under one phase of the
lifecycle in the OpenLCA model since the Ecoinvent datasets that were used typically have them merged
as well. For the production phase, the inventory that was used is demonstrated in Appendix D.
3.3.6.2
Use phase - Replacements (B4)
In the "Use" stage, the operational energy use wasn’t included as there were no estimations for the
case house. Consequently only the "Replacements" module was considered. "Replacements" include the
production of the components that will have to replace the initial ones during the lifespan of the building.
These replacements derived according to the lifespans that are demonstrated in the inventory (Appendix
D).
According to EN 15978, replacements shall be considered based on the lifespan of the building
component [Aagaard et al. (2013)].
Replacements can be ignored when the new components aren’t
expected to have a lifespan within that building of more than the one third of their normal life expectancy
according to the lifetime tables Aagaard et al. (2013). New building parts were assumed to have the same
lifespans and be produce by the same technologies as the building parts they replace although, there
will probably be a development in their characteristics (e.g. higher lifespans) and improvements in the
production processes [Aagaard et al. (2013)] or even totally new materials. It should be highlighted that
if the module of "Maintenance" was included it could have quite high inﬂuence in the toxicity related
categories due to wood maintenance, but it isn’t included in the DGNB boundaries (Figure 12). Sathre
and González-García (2014) highlights the importance of maintenance in the toxicity impacts of the wood
products and mentions that "research is underway to develop eﬀective wood preservation methods that
do not add to the toxicity burden in the environment".
3.3.6.3
End of Life phase (C3,C4)
The end of life phase includes waste management of all the building components including replacements.
The two modules that are included, are C3, waste recovery, and C4, waste disposal [Aagaard et al. (2013)].
Waste recovery entails all waste treatment processes of waste fractions that derive from the deconstruction
phase such as collection, sorting, preparation for reuse etc. Waste disposal entails all the processes that
derive by the ﬁnal disposal of materials (e.g. incineration, landﬁlling etc.). Although the two modules
are discrete, the database used doesn’t distinguish them and thus they are modelled as one, similar to
A1-A3. Waste treatment processes were based on the literature (e.g. CLEAN (2014), Miljøministeriet
(2014)) and were also set similar to the examples of Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013) and Rasmussen
(2015) in order to assist the comparison of the houses and to achieve compliance with the guidelines of
SBi. The waste streams and the processes used for modelling their treatment are stated in D. Due to
the beneﬁts from DfD, it was assumed that the whole amount of steel could be recycled and that the
whole quantity of wood and wood-based products would go for to waste management facilities for energy
recovery. Furthermore, it should be highlighted that the used process for the EoL of wooden elements of
28
==PAGE:31==
the case house was "treatment of waste building wood, chrome preserved, municipal incineration" and it
is the only available process for incineration of preserved building wood waste in Ecoinvent 3.1. database.
The preservation of wood waste was considered as the lifespans in the SBi Tables include maintenance.
In the case of wood ﬁber insulation, the process for non preserved wood waste was considered, since the
product doesn’t any maintenance. Practically the diﬀerences among the two processes are negligible. The
fact there was only one process for wood building waste created some discrepancies that are analyzed in
Chapter 3.3.8.
3.3.6.4
Potential for reuse, recovery and recycling (D)
D phase includes the net beneﬁts arising from the product reuse, recycling and energy recovery. In case
of reuse, impacts from the production and ﬁnal disposal of products are allocated in the next product
system as well according to ISO 14040 (2006). Reuse cycles indicate the number of product systems
among which the impacts are distributed. All the next product systems along with the corresponding
calculations and assumptions are stated analytically in D. Energy beneﬁts from incineration are also a
source of controversy in the calculations, as in case of buildings in Denmark, the energy grids in 120 years
should be predicted. Tellnes et al. (2014) suggests that beneﬁts from energy recovery by substitution
shall not be included as "EN15804 deﬁnes these beneﬁt beyond the life cycle of the product in order to
avoid double counting", however a wide range of literature that is mentioned below and EPDs (such as
IBU (2014), IBU (2013), Yeh et al. (2013)) include it.
For this report, in case of wood incineration, energy beneﬁts are considered to replace the "marginal
energy source" similar to Gustavsson et al. (2006), that represents the source that is most likely to be
increased/decreased in case of changing demands. However, forecasting the future marginal energy source
and the corresponding technology is quite challenging when considering the 120 year lifespan of the case
house or ever worse when considering that a two times reused element would reach a lifespan of 360
years. According to Energistyrelsen (2010), 63% of the electricity production in Denmark in 2010, was
produced from thermal sources through CHP plants (Cogeneration of heat and power). CHP facilitate
the simultaneous and more eﬃcient production of heat and power comparing to dedicated heat and power
plants and mitigate overall costs, emissions and fuel consumption [Turconi et al. (2014)]. In addition,
"CHP is expected to increase in the future, i.e. doubling the current capacity in the EU [Turconi et al.
(2014)]".
According to Gustavsson et al. (2006), "the coal-ﬁred plant represents current marginal (electricity) pro-
duction in northern Europe", while "they might be used in the coming 40–50 years for marginal electricity
production in Europe" [Gustavsson et al. (2006)]. Natural gas would be an alternative choice for the
future as well as it has a low carbon content and the natural-gas power plants can have a satisfying
conversion eﬃciency [Gustavsson et al. (2006)]. Hence, natural gas-ﬁred that combine CO2 capture and
storage as well, could be also an option for future marginal electricity production [Gustavsson et al.
(2006)]. Fouquet et al. (2015) considered a "pessimistic and business as usual" and a "very-optimistic
and 440 ppm CO2 electricity mix". The ﬁrst scenario of Fouquet et al. (2015) assumes "that economic
conditions do not stimulate innovations and that the few developments in electricity production lead to a
mix dominated by fossil fuels", while the latter scenario assumes "technological breakthrough is assumed
and even though the share of fossil fuel does not decrease, oil power plants are replaced by gas and coal
technologies and Carbon Capture & Storage (CCS) technologies are used and ﬁnally, the target of 440
ppm of CO2 in the atmosphere for 2050 is reached" based on predictions from NEEDS (2009). Cherubini
et al. (2009) highlights the complexity of energy balances and concludes that "diﬀerent combinations
of feedstocks, conversion routes, fuels, end-use applications and methodological assumptions lead to a
wide range of results". Similarly, Jørgensen et al. (2014) highlights the importance of selecting waste
management type as according to E et al. (2002), diﬀerent waste treatment choices of a biobased product
may lead to so diﬀerent GHG impacts similar to the diﬀerence between the same biobased product and
its reference fossil fuel product.
Summing up, considering the current and projected heat and power grids and the goal of eliminating
energy production from non-renewable sources in Denmark and the fact that CHP is rapidly increasing,
for this report two diﬀerent basic scenarios were considered for substitution at EoL stage and are demon-
strated in Table 5. Similar to Fouquet et al. (2015), a "business as usual" scenario and a more optimistic
one were set.
29
==PAGE:32==
Scenario 1 represents the "business as usual" scenario. Scenario 2 considers that in 2050 the use of fossil
fuels will already be almost extinct [Nygaard Rasmussen (2012)], and the marginal electricity source is
assumed to be produced from wood chips (biomass). In case of CHP natural gas plants, "emissions and
eﬃciencies are adjusted to Swiss emission regulation (LRV, Luftreinhalteverordnung) valid in year 2000"
[Ecoinvent (2017)] and it was assumed that the eﬃciency will remain the same. The speciﬁc processes
used from the Ecoinvent database are demonstrated in Appendix D in Table 24.
Heating value of wood waste
The Ecoinvent process for building wood waste isn’t fully appropriate for the case study since it refers
to the average wood waste and the corresponding impurities. This fact leads to mismatch between the
actual caloriﬁc value and the one of the process since in the case study DfD guidelines were implemented.
Consequently it is assumed for this study that the heating value in waste wood doesn’t diﬀer signiﬁcantly
from wood chips. Accounting the preservation of the wood and any other impurities, the heating value
per kg of waste wood is assumed to be 5% less comparing to wood chips, while the moisture content is
considered to be 10% in the average wood waste according to the Ecoinvent database. The heating value
of wood is 19,2 MJ/kg dry wood or 16.4 MJ/kg wood waste (with moisture) while the heating value in the
process according to Ecoinvent varies from 15.36 MJ/kg to 13.99 MJ/kg. Regarding the rest of the incin-
erated waste, the heating value of wood ﬁber insulation is 16.22 MJ/kg (with 20% moisture) [IBU (2014)].
Eﬃciency of the incineration plants
Eﬃciency of the incineration plants can cause signiﬁcant discrepancies in the results and to aﬀect crucially
the comparison, especially in cases where fossil fuels with high impacts are concerned. The Ecoinvent
process for the waste wood considers eﬃciency below 30%, while the "overall eﬃciency (sum of electricity
and heat) of newly installed CHP systems in the European Union varies from 60% to 90%" [Turconi
et al. (2014)]. In case of scenario 2, the "heat and power co-generation, wood chips, 6667 kW, state-of-
the-art 2014 - DK" process was used and considers eﬃciency of 60% (Electric eﬃciency 0.15 and thermal
eﬃciency 0.45), that is only on the down limit of the projections by Turconi et al. (2014). In order to
avoid adding controversy by adjusting further the eﬃciency of the second process and since it is already,
even marginally, within the predictions, it was decided to keep this process as it is and instead to adjust
the eﬃciency of the process that refers to the waste building wood. The incineration of wood chips was
assumed that must be more eﬃcient than incineration of wooden elements due to total lack of impurities.
Consequently, eﬃciency of waste building wood incineration was set to be 5% less, equal to 57% (Electric
eﬃciency 0.14 and thermal eﬃciency 0.43). Combining with the heating value of waste wood, the factors
for each kg of waste wood (10% moisture) are 0.14*16.4 = 2.296 MJ/kg for electricity and 0.43*16.4 =
7.052 for heat. It should be highlighted that this assumption is a source of controversy and uncertainty
for this LCA and a sensitivity analysis is performed in Chapter 5.4.2.
3.3.7
Reuse scenarios
As already analyzed in Chapter 2, DfD aims to enable the full exploitation of materials’ lifespans through
reuse. For the case study building, according to Aagaard et al. (2013) all the building elements except
from most of the load bearing structure and the inner walls have lifespans that are shorter than the
building’s or undeﬁned. On the other hand, the lifespans of the outer and inner walls and the wooden
structure in the roof are set by Aagaard et al. (2013) (SBi Tables) as equal to the typical building’s and the
potential lifespan that could be achieved through reuse isn’t examined. However as it is also mentioned
in Chapter 4.1.2.3, there are documented examples of DfD and reuse of wooden elements and buildings
since 16th century [Crowther (1999b)]. For CLT, IBU (2013) states that "when used as designated, there
is no known or anticipated limit to its durability", while other EPDs mention no lifespan (FPInnovations
(2013), Yeh et al. (2013), EGOIN (2012)). Wood ﬁbre insulation can also be reused, since according
Table 5: Scenarios for marginal energy sources that are substituted due to energy from incineration.
Electricity production

Biomass (Wood chips)

==PAGE:33==
to IBU (2014), "provided they are untreated and not damaged, PAVATEX woodﬁbre boards can easily
be collected separately and reused or used further for the same application". However considering the
eﬀect of moisture, SBi tables consider reduced lifespan for wood related insulation in the roof and similar
eﬀects and reduced lifespan were also assumed in this report for the insulation under the ﬂoor. In case
of the ground screws that are used for foundations, if they are properly maintained every approximately
20 years, their lifespan could be unlimited [Krinner (2017)]. Considering that parts of the house can
be detached by the user, it is considered that a part of the ground screws could also be maintained.
Ultimately, the ﬂoor frame could also be theoretically reused but due to high exposure to moisture, its’
reuse potential is ignored for this report. Summing up, the following scenarios were considered for this
report.
• Scenario 1: No reuse
• Scenario 2: One reuse cycle of outer and inner walls (CLT and insulation), wooden beams in the
roof and 25% of ground screws
• Scenario 3: Two reuse cycles of outer and inner walls (CLT and insulation), wooden beams in the
roof and 25% of ground screws
The lifespans that correspond to these scenarios are demonstrated in Appendix D.
Due to the high
uncertainties regarding the actual lifespans of the materials and all the uncertainties that are linked with
these lifespans, reuse scenarios aren’t included in the basic scenarios.
3.3.8
Carbon sequestration
Carbon storage from the atmosphere derives through photosynthesis as trees grow Wilson (2008). Bio-
genic carbon ﬂuxes are often omitted from LCA studies on the basis that CO2 sequestration is equal
or greater than the released CO2 over the life cycle and consequently carbon neutrality is often claimed
[Brandão et al. (2013), Renouf et al. (2015), Cherubini et al. (2009)]. There are numerous positive argu-
ments for temporary carbon storage as it saves time for technological progress on the ﬁeld and postpones
radiative forcing, while it may assist permanent carbon sequestration [Brandão et al. (2013)]. In addi-
tion, temporary sequestration can mitigate the risk of exceeding potential tipping points [Dornburg and
Marland (2008), Marland et al. (2001), Fearnside (2008)], while a fraction of radiative forcing is avoided
[Brandão et al. (2013)].
Furthermore Moura Costa and Wilson (2000) and other researchers suggest
that carbon storage can compensate cumulative climatic impacts that derive by CO2 emissions. If every
wood-framed building is being replaced with a new similar building, then this would lead to a "permanent
step change in carbon stock" [Gustavsson et al. (2006)].
On the other hand, a range of researchers question the eﬀectiveness of temporary carbon storage and
sequestration to the mitigation of climate change [Korhonen et al. (2002), Meinshausen and Hare (2000),
Kirschbaum (2003), Kirschbaum (2006)]. Considering the equilibrium of carbon concentrations in the
atmosphere and the oceans, sequestration of atmospheric carbon can also lead to reduced carbon uptake
from the oceans, and thus the release of temporary sequestrated carbon could increase radiative forcing
[Brandão et al. (2013)]. This fact highlights the importance of the relation among timing of releasing
emissions and tipping points [Brandão et al. (2013)].
Generally, the ILCD Handbook suggests that delayed emissions and temporary carbon storage shouldn’t
be considered except if it’s within the goal of the study [JRC and Sustainability (2010)]. The International
Organization for Standardization in its standard for carbon footprinting, ISO 14067, the "Product Life
Cycle Greenhouse Gas Accounting Standard (GHG Protocol)" by WRI and WBCSD and the updated
PAS 2050 require no credit for temporary storage [Brandão et al. (2013)], however they suggest inclusion
of the biogenic GHG ﬂows in the carbon footprint and separate demonstration from the fossil based GHG
ﬂows. Since then the handling of carbon storage in LCA and carbon footprinting, was analyzed at an
expert workshop that took place in the Joint Research Centre but no consensus was reached [Brandão
et al. (2013), Guest et al. (2013)]. The most updated product category rules for "wood and wood-based
products for use in construction" and sequestration standards, are BS EN 16485: 2014 and BS EN
16449:2014. These standards are unanimous that carbon storage in products must be included in EPDs
[Wood for Good and PE INTERNATIONAL (2015)]. This was reviewed and approved by CEN/TC 350
WG3 (Committee on Product Level (EPD’s , communication formats etc)) which developed EN 15804
[Wood for Good and PE INTERNATIONAL (2015)]. Based on the EN 16485, Kuittinen and Linkosalmi
(2015) recommended the following method to report biogenic carbon:
31
==PAGE:34==
• "According to a product category rule, a wood’s biogenic carbon content (kg CO2) can be calculated
to be included as a negative value to the global warming potential (GWP) of the A1 stage".
• "The same biogenic carbon content (kg CO2) must be removed as a positive value from the system
at the latest in the C3 stage".
• The sum of the biogenic carbon content in wood is zero.
• "If the wood ends up as fuel for energy generation at stage C, it is possible to report, in module D, the
emissions of the substituted fossil fuel as a negative value and make it a part of the global warming
potential (GWP) to correspond to the same energy content. This is known as the substitution
eﬀect".
Similarly Gustavsson et al. (2006) assume a 100 year lifespan, where at Year 100 the harvested forest has
completely regrown, consistent with the Nordic forestry practice for an average 100-year rotation period,
and thus all the released carbon from the initial harvest will be recaptured by the end of the building’s
life. In addition, Gustavsson et al. (2006) assumes that the captured carbon in the wooden building
materials is released at Year 100 through incineration and decomposition, leaving the net carbon stock
unchanged considering the whole lifecycle. Furthermore Gustavsson et al. (2006) assumes that all the
rest biogenic carbon that is not sequestered, decays or gets burned during Year 0 and although this isn’t
strictly true, it is further assumed that "the timing of decomposition does not aﬀect the validity of the
life-cycle emission results, and that the amount of long-term sequestered carbon is negligible" Gustavs-
son et al. (2006). In other cases dynamic approaches are suggested to be followed "for a more consistent
analysis of emissions ﬂows and global warming impacts over time" [Fouquet et al. (2015), Helin et al.
(2013)]. Dynamic LCA requires an inventory of annual GHG emissions and sequestration [Penaloza et al.
(2016), Helin et al. (2013)].
Ultimately, when considering temporary carbon storage, the used timescale for the assessment is crucial
[Jørgensen et al. (2014)]. In LCA, global warming potential (GWP) often applies a time horizon of 100-
year horizon, similarly to Kyoto Protocol [UNFCCC (1998)]. This horizon rather reﬂects a middle choice
among the three GWP horizons presented by the IPCC and isn’t scientiﬁc based [Shine (2009)]. This fact
results temporary carbon storage impacts that occur after this horizon not to be included [Moura Costa
and Wilson (2000)]. Consequently the time horizon leads to diﬀerent accounting periods and could hide
long-term impacts as a fraction of them takes place even centuries later [Jørgensen and Hauschild (2013)].
Summing up, for this report the most updated product category rules and sequestration standards, BS
EN 16485: 2014 and BS EN 16449:2014, and more speciﬁcally the example of Kuittinen and Linkosalmi
(2015), since the analytic rules aren’t available. However when using the Ecoinvent 3.1 database, it can
be easily identiﬁed that the biogenic carbon content of the only process that refers to building wood
waste, isn’t matching with the credited biogenic carbon content of the several wood products. This mis-
match though between the wooden products and the EoL process doesn’t only depend to the inherent
content of biogenic carbon. Actually, this content is only slightly diﬀering by the content of the waste
wood process and speciﬁcally, when the average carbon content of each wood product is approximately
0.494 kgCO2-eq/kg of dry wood, the carbon of the waste building wood process is 0.4843 kgCO2-eq/kg
of dry wood according to Ecoinvent. The main diﬀerence derives because there is a quantity of stored
biogenic carbon that is allocated the wood products but isn’t actually in sequestered within the product.
According to Vogtländer et al. (2014), in Ecoinvent data "1 kg of a wooden end-product relates to many
kilograms of wood in the forests" and for example mentions that "1-kg d.m. planed timber is related to
(..) 6.97 kg CO2 storage in the forest". Consequently, Ecoinvent allocates to each end-product a higher
quantity of biogenic carbon than the one that is actually stored.
As a result for this report, two calculations took place. Firstly, in order to achieve a neutral biogenic
carbon balance over the whole lifecycle, similarly to the guidelines, the diﬀerence between the input of
biogenic carbon in all the wood products, and the output from the EoL process, was calculated. The dif-
ference was added to the emissions of the waste wood treatment process, in the C stage. This calculation
is presented in Appendix F. For the calculation of the biogenic carbon that is credited to the products,
the ILCD GWP (including biogenic CO2) and GWP (excluding biogenic CO2) impact categories in the
GaBi software were used. However the results were also veriﬁed with OpenLCA and manual calculation
in some cases as well.
32
==PAGE:35==
Next the calculation of the carbon that is actually stored in the case house took place and excluded
any carbon stored in other by-products. The quantity of this carbon was measured according to the
inherent carbon content of each product and its dry density, similar to the literature, and refers only to
the products of the A stage, since the B stage includes only replacements of materials. The calculation
of the actual carbon storage within the case house is demonstrated brieﬂy in Appendix G and the ﬁnal
result is presented in Chapter 4.6.4.1.
3.3.9
EPDs used
In some cases, the materials that were used for the LCA weren’t available in the Ecoinvent 3.1. database
and EPDs had to be used. This was the case for two signiﬁcant materials that were used in the case
study, CLT and wood ﬁbre insulation. For both materials, only their production phase was taken from
the EPDs, while for the EoL stage and the "Next product stage", they were modelled similar to the rest
of the materials in order to achieve higher cohesion and uniformity in the report and also in order to
apply the desired EoL scenarios for all the materials. The corresponding EoL scenarios and processes are
demonstrated in Appendix D, Table 24.
3.3.9.1
Cross Laminated Timber (CLT)
For CLT the most updated EPDs are the following:
• IBU (2013)
• Yeh et al. (2013)
• FPInnovations (2013)
• EGOIN (2012)
However FPInnovations (2013) and EGOIN (2012) are only referring to a speciﬁc producer and factory
and are slightly valid in the chronic limits. On the other hand FPInnovations (2013) refers to the UK
consumption mix and is a more representative EPD. However the assessed CLT product is a 5-layer
product. However for the case study house a 3-layer CLT product is used. IBU (2013) represents the
average CLT product and is referring to the 90% of the German production. Furthermore it uses by far
the most updated data and comparing to the ﬁndings from the literature, it has a relatively conservative
approach. Consequently the values from IBU (2013) were chosen and are presented below.

3.3.10
Data
Considering that the case house was being designed along with this assessment, a wide range of data was
based on the most updated designs and assumptions. The used data included:
• Composition of components
• Material types
• Material quantities
• Physical properties of the materials (weight, density etc)
• EoL streams and waste treatment scenarios
• Material and component lifespans
The quality of the quantiﬁcation of the materials is typically speciﬁed as high, medium and low. According
to Diraoui and Larsen (2016) high quality data refer to project speciﬁc information (such as tender
material, ﬁnal BIM model), medium quality refer to general information regarding amounts and element
compositions that needed further assumptions and estimations and low quality refer only to assumptions
and estimations based on literature, information from manufacturers or professionals from the industry,
EPDs, technical datasheets or the judgment of an LCA practitioner. Considering that existing designs
that were used for this LCA weren’t the ﬁnal ones, the deriving data were considered as medium quality.
Based on this criteria, data quality of the inventory and the corresponding sources are demonstrated in
Appendix E. In the following Table, there is demonstrated an overview of the data quality regarding
the diﬀerent parts of the building. As Table shows, the data for the inner and outer walls are of higher
quality, as they derive by the most updated designs, while for the rest of the house, data are based mainly
on assumptions.

3.3.11
Impact categories
For this report CML baseline 2001 methodology was used for the life cycle impact assessment. This
methodology considers ﬁrstly all the impact categories that DGNB mentions. Additionally the depletion
of fossil and non-fossil resources is also considered as they are crucial for the DfD theory and also DGNB
highlights these problems [Diraoui and Larsen (2016)]. Ideally, according to F. Rasmussen et al. (2013),
the use of the 4 toxicity impact categories could be useful because toxicological impact proﬁles of the
building may not follow the trends of the rest of the impact categories. Speciﬁcally building materials tend
to contribute more to all toxicological impact categories comparing to energy consumption in contrast
with the rest impact categories [Rasmussen et al. (2013)]. However toxicity impact categories aren’t
included in the DGNB scheme and the EN 15804 [Rasmussen et al. (2013)], while EPDs also typically
don’t include them. For this report toxicity impacts are included but they don’t consider the impacts
from the production of wood ﬁbre insulation and CLT as they aren’t stated in the EPDs. Analytically
the CML-Baseline 2001 impact categories that are included in this thesis are the following:

3.3.12
Normalization and weighting
Normalization enables the comparison of the magnitude of impacts across diﬀerent impact categories.
However, normalization of impacts increases uncertainty in the results [Sohn et al. (2016)] and thus the
normalized results are only a side-result to be used as "food for thought", while the rest of the results
didn’t undergo any normalization.
Normalization and weighting of the results took place according
to "World, 2000 [year]" normalization and weighting set of CML (baseline) impact assessment method
(Version 4.4. of January 2015), which is included in OpenLCA 1.6.2.. The factors that were used are
presented in the Table below.

3.3.13
Reference buildings
For further assessing the environmental performance of the case house, a comparison was performed with
two reference buildings. The choice of the buildings was made similar to Brejnrod (2015), where the two
houses serve as critical benchmarks for assessing the environmental performance of the house. The ﬁrst
building is a standard house that represents the typical Danish single-family house in terms of size and
construction type. The second building represents the state-of-the-art building in terms of environmental
impacts from materials.
3.3.13.1
The reference house
Reference house represents a typical Danish single family house and is built and insulated to meet energy
requirements of 2015 for newly built houses [Sohn et al. (2016)]. This house "is the mean-value house
at the core of BE10 calculations" and was used as a basis for comparison of the mini CO2 houses.
Aggerholm (2013) mentioned in the SBI 2013:25 report the most signiﬁcant construction details after
adapting the house to BE15 energy class and demonstrated the plan and the views of the house (Figure
16). Speciﬁcally the gross ﬂoor area is 162 mˆ2 and it has an open attic, insulated on the loft. The external
walls are insulated cavity walls, having light concrete inner wall of 100 mm and half stone brick external
facade. Foundations upper part is made of insulated light concrete aggregate blocks. Ground slab is
100 mm concrete and ﬂoor heating and insulation are beneath. The partition walls are constructed of
light concrete blocks. Window area is approximately 32.9 mˆ2. There is natural ventilation with external
air vents located in the living rooms. The heat supply is either ground coupled heat pump or district
heating. All ﬁttings and pipes are insulated according to DS 452. The most updated and trustworthy
environmental assessment of the reference house using LCA was performed in SBI 2015:09 by Rasmussen
(2015). The results from this study were used for comparison with the case house.
3.3.13.2
The Upcycle House
The Upcycle house is one of the 6 one-family houses that were built by Realdania Byg and are called
"Mini- CO2 Houses". Their gross area varies from 129 to 150 mˆ2 and were built with scope to be sold
after to ordinary families. All the houses aimed to have reduced carbon footprints, each one in its own
innovative way [Kleis (2013)]. According to [Kleis (2013)] the Upcycle house is a single-storey house with
a gross ﬂoor area of 129 mˆ2. All the used building materials are either upcycled materials or reused
items. In the house, a greenhouse and an outdoor patio were built as integral parts. The foundations are
recycled steel helical screw piers, while the load-bearing structural elements are 2 reused 40-ft. high cube
shipping containers. Facades are faced with a composite material and the roof consists of aluminium
sheeting. Paper wool is used as insulation for the ceiling and the walls. The internal walls and the ﬂoors
of the containers are faced with OSB wood panels and the windows are triple glazed. The LCA of the
of the upcycle house was also conducted by Freja Nygaard Rasmussen and Harpa Birgisdóttir as part of
the Realdania Byg project "MiniCO2-husene i Nyborg" and was the best performing house in terms of
global warming potential impacts among the 6 houses of the project [Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013)].

4
Results
4.1
Rules of Thumb
In this chapter, the "Rules of Thumb (RoT)" regarding implementation of DfD in buildings is presented.
In the next sub-chapters the general guidelines, a range of guidelines for common structural frames and
installations, and the existing assessment tools regarding DfD are presented respectively.
4.1.1
General Rules of Thumb
In the next pages of this chapter the general RoT are demonstrated in the following Tables:
• Materials Table 11
• Connections Table 12
• Components Table 13
• Building Table 14, 15
Each guideline is assessed for its contribution on any of the desired EoL scenarios as several guidelines
contribute to more than one. The desired EoL scenarios are the "recycling hierarchy" and are presented
again in Figure 18. It is important to mention again here that material reuse and component reprocess
and remanufacture are in the same category according to this systematization.
Figure 18: Potential End of Life scenarios including technical levels of reuse [adapted from Crowther
(2000c)]
Similar to Crowther (2001), the contribution of a guideline to the EoL scenarios was assessed to be:
• "Highly relevant  ". When the guideline is mandatory ("Don’t use hazardous materials" for "Ma-
terial recycling") or makes critical diﬀerence for a speciﬁc EoL scenario ("Manageable weight and
size of materials and components" for "Material reuse")
• "Relevant •". When the guideline would be often signiﬁcantly helpful towards a scenario and even
mandatory under circumstances ("Accessibility to connections" could be mandatory for "Material
recycling" only for diﬀerent materials). Furthermore it could be signiﬁcant but only for a limited
range of materials ("Chemical bonds weaker than materials connected" for "Material reuse" as for
brick walls.
• Not normally relevant.
Guidelines are presented in a loosely hierarchical way, based on the frequency that they are mentioned
in the literature, the importance that is attributed to them and on the range of their application.

4.1.2
Rules of thumb and examples for common structural frames
4.1.2.1
Concrete
Concrete is the most common construction material and simultaneously the most challenging to design
for reuse [Webster and Costello (2005), Fletcher (2001a)]. The structure is contiguous with no convenient
joints for separation, custom-designed for speciﬁc application, while structural parts are diﬃcult to move
[Webster and Costello (2005), Fletcher (2001a)]. Furthermore testing of reinforcement and the condition
of an element is quite diﬃcult. The main alternative is crushing them in order to be used as road ﬁlling
or aggregate for new concrete and separating the steel reinforcement for recycling [?]. On the other hand,
precast concrete has higher reuse potential as it has standard sizes and reinforcement while members can
be joined with mechanical fasteners [Fletcher (2001a)].
In case of concrete additional speciﬁc guidelines are:
• Avoid cast-in-place members [Webster and Costello (2005), Addis and Schouten (2004)].
• Use removable, durable, mechanical fasteners. Stainless steel is an appropriate material choice for
fasteners as it allows thermal movement and cracking can be avoided [Webster and Costello (2005)].
• Develop new solutions for connecting precast plank and tees in order to replace topping slabs.
The in-plane diaphragm forces and the out-of-plane shear forces could be resisted by mechanical
fasteners and removable materials such as plywood on sleepers could be used for a smooth sub-ﬂoor.
Also in parking garages, precast joints could be left exposed [Webster and Costello (2005)].
• Include information about concrete strength and member reinforcement in the member label [Web-
ster and Costello (2005)].
• Eliminate basements and below-grade construction as foundation walls and deep footings can’t be
easily salvaged. Preferable solutions are precast ongrade slabs, precast foundation walls or shallow
precast footings [Webster and Costello (2005)].
• Use simply supported beams for allowing easy disassembly without damage [Hechler et al. (2016),
Addis and Schouten (2004)].
Peikko systems
Peikko is a Finish company that operates worldwide and introduced mechanical connections for joining
concrete elements and composite structures. The motivation was the cold Finnish weather that isn’t ap-
propriate for traditional site-casted connections. Mechanical joints for precast elements resulted in easier,
faster and cheaper constructions. The main challenge for these joints are that they are casted in cement to
prevent screws from loosening and sound from escaping, but a dissolvable binder could solve this problem.
The ‘Tenloc Panel Connector’ (Figure 19a) is a latch-type panel connector and is one of the mechanical
connection joints for large concrete structures. This joint connects vertically precast elements, parapets
to façades and also walls to columns in a quite quick procedure. The system has a latch part and an
anchor part. After the latch is tightened, with a ratchet key, into the anchor part, connections are ﬁnished
by grouting the latch boxes. In Figure 28b it is demonstrated the creation of instantly moment-resisting
stiﬀcolumn connections with the use of four bolts on the HPKM column shoe. Column shoes are in the
precast concrete column and anchor bolts are casted within the foundation or another column. Fixing
is achieved with tightening the nuts onto the bolts and then the joint is grouted and acts as a typical
reinforced concrete cross-section. [Peikko (2016)]
In Figure 19c Deltabeam is demonstrated. It is a composite beam, widely used for slim-ﬂoor construction,
in multi-storey buildings of any type. A great advantage of Deltabeam is that it can help the creation
of large open spaces due to its composite action between steel and concrete. Its shallow design leads to
decrease in the ﬂoor height and minimizes conﬂicts with HVAC systems. Deltabeam can be combined
with all general concrete slab types. Ultimately, in Figure 19d, Corbeau PCs (Figure 19d) are an example
of support for Deltabeam.
Wall-to-wall connection
44

Reusable foundations
Figure 21: Precast manhole rings could
be ﬁlled with crushed concrete and used
as reusable foundations [NPCA (2012)]
Foundations are cast into the ground and hardly removable.
Contractors often remove the top 1 or 2 meters and leave
the rest behind [Fletcher (2001a)]. Specially in major cities,
land contamination from foundations has become a signif-
icant problem for groundwork contractors and there is no
documentation for their position [Fletcher (2001a)].
Wil-
son (2000) introduces the term solid pollution to describe
this problem. Furthermore this leads contractors to seek for
green ﬁeld sites in order to avoid such problems [Gorgolewski
(1999)].
Consequently not reusing the foundations causes
both loss of proﬁts and also indirect environmental impacts.
For this problem, Gorgolewski suggested 3 solutions: "sheet
steel piling wall", "continuous bored pile concrete wall" and "cast in situ reinforced concrete cantilever
45
==PAGE:48==
wall". In another approach, the Japanese pavilion during Expo 2000 used for its foundations concrete
blocks and also a boarded scaﬀold frame containing sand [Fletcher (2001a)]. Ultimately a quite interesting
solution is the use of precast manhole rings [Figure 21] ﬁlled with crashed concrete which is a technique
that takes place in UK [Horwill (2017)].
4.1.2.2
Steel
Steel is one of the most common structural materials and has an extremely high potential for reuse.
Steel members are durable, versatile and have standard system of shapes being so easily reused [Silver-
stein (2009)]. However, steel is often downcycled and DfD can tackle this problem [Jensen and Sommer
(2016)]. When applying DfD to steel structures, the main priority is proper connection design. Con-
nections constitute up to half of the construction cost and their design determines the reusability of the
elements. Welded connection aren’t removed cleanly, and bolted connection leave holes for ﬁxing. In the
following Table a comparison of welded and bolted connections is demonstrated.
Table 16: Comparison of welded and bolted connections [Silverstein (2009)]
Issue
Welds
Bolts
Engineering
Adequate strength
Adequate strength
Brittle failure
Ductile failure
Financing
High set-up cost
Zero set-up cost
Low cost per connection
Higher cost per connection
Construction
Accessibility an issue
Accessibility less of an issue
Skilled labor
Unskilled labor
Hard to certify/inspect
Easy to certify/inspect
Disassembly
Diﬃcult to remove
Easy to remove
Minimal damage to structural elements
Elements may require patching
For DfD in steel structures the additional speciﬁc guidelines according to Webster and Costello (2005),
Silverstein (2009) and Addis and Schouten (2004) are:
• Use bolted connections.
• Avoid conventional composite ﬂoor systems that use welded studs or cast-in-place concrete.
• Use precast decks.
• Avoid weird shapes and short ﬁller pieces.
• Use regular spacing.
• Note steel grades and shape designations on members.
• Avoid spray-on ﬁreprooﬁng as it is diﬃcult to remove, adds weight and volume, can be damaged in
transport and it is an obstacle to refabrication.
• Bolt holes can be pre-drilled to allow only a minimal set of connectors.
• In shear beam-to-column connections, angle cleats provide fully bolted interfaces. If the number of
bolts required is prohibitive, extended ﬁn plates should be considered
• In beam-to-column moment connections proper member speciﬁcation and the column orientation
can minimize the need for stiﬀeners.
• Become innovative in connection design (standardized systems of components, clamped friction
connections etc) in order to prevent remanufacturing
House R 128
R 128 House is a four-storey structure and is designed for disassembly by Werner Sobek. The house
is constructed with prefabricated components and has a simple cubic shape to allow prefabrication,
standarization and increase reusability potential of the elements [Sobek (2009)].

4.1.2.3
Wood
In Europe, the use of technologies regarding disassembly and reuse of wooden elements and whole build-
ings are documented since 16th century in Switzerland and all over Europe [Crowther (1999b)]. Similarly
47
==PAGE:50==
the traditional Japanese timber architecture uses a construction technique that enables expansion, re-
modelling, removal and reconstruction according to users’ lifestyles [Kikutake (1995)]. Japanese domestic
buildings use a primary structural frame of major timber members and a secondary timber frame accord-
ing to spatial requirements of the users and can be disassembled and remodelled without any eﬀects on
the primary structure or waste of building materials [Itoh (1972)].
In case of wooden buildings additional speciﬁc guidelines according to Webster and Costello (2005) are:
• Prefer screws and bolts over nails.
• Implement robust moisture management techniques for protection from decay, insect damage etc.
• Use timber-frame construction, heavy timber, instead of dimension lumber. Avoid fragile elements
such as I-joists.
• Label elements with species and grades.
• Use prefabricated elements for enabling deconstruction on the ground and minimizing waste.
An interesting dispute in the literature was about the optimal size of the beams, as Addis and Schouten
(2004) proposes the use of long beams as their reuse would be easier by trimming them to the desired di-
mension, while also long beams could help the creation of large column-free spaces that could be adapted
easier to any desired functions. On the other hand, it is well acknowledged in the literature that element
must have manageable weight and size in order to avoid using the crane and avoiding thus expenses,
personnel and time needed for the construction and deconstruction processes [Crowther (2000a), Vand-
kunsten A/S (2016), Guy and Ciarimboli (2006)].
OPEN_1 House
Figure 26: Exploded view
of OPEN_1 House [Guy
and Ciarimboli (2006)]

plicitly the six “shearing layers of change” framework of Brand, used
oﬀ-site prefabricated elements and its primary principle is “disentan-
glement” (Figure 26) [Guy and Ciarimboli (2006)].
The design en-

can be accessed from the wall surface [Guy and Ciarimboli (2006)].
Repair or upgrade of the sub-systems can take place without alter-

Ul-

(2006)].

4.1.3
Installations
Almost all buildings allow the HVAC equipment to be maintained and replaced as its service life is much
shorter than the rest of the building [Addis and Schouten (2004)]. However this often takes place in
expense of the reusing potential of other material as traditionally wiring, ducts and pipes are attached
to other elements with welds, clips etc [?]. Furthermore piping and wiring inside wall, ﬂoor or ceiling
cavities decreases accessibility and damages overlying elements, while reclaiming and separating them
during disassembly is also tough [?]. Consequently proper integrtion of services can be crucial for the
process and the results of the disassembly [?, Addis and Schouten (2004)]. Some guidelines for DfD and
installations as they derive from the generic rules are:
• Separate building layers and systems (i.e. electrical, mechanical). Disentangling utilities from the
structure’s walls, ﬂoors and ceilings eases repair, reuse and recycling of components [EPA (2008),
EPA (2004), Addis and Schouten (2004), Guy and Ciarimboli (2006)]
• Reduce plant needs of the building by using solar shadding, passive cooling and natural ventilation
[Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Reduce plant needs of the system by using under-ﬂoor plenum over ducts and on ﬂoor plants to
reduce the amount of distribution [Addis and Schouten (2004)]. Gaisset (2011)]
• Use a series of decentralized ventilation units around the building instead of one very large mechan-
ical plant in order to take it apart and reuse it easier in a new building [Horswill (2017), Addis and
Schouten (2004), EPA (2004)]
• Use motion or voice control instead of traditional switches for electrical and plumbing systems.
• Standardize pipework (lengths, ﬁttings etc) [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Use prefabricated pipework for interchangeable, repeated assemblies to increase reuse [Addis and
Schouten (2004)]
• Use pipe ﬁttings that are easy to disconnect/ reconnect in contrast to welded or soldered connections
[Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Reduce number and types of polymer used to assist recycling [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Use standard air handling units that have variable speed drive motors to allow reuse under diﬀerent
conditions [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Pay attention to proper separation of the materials as HVAC systems include a high variety of
materials [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
49
==PAGE:52==
• Maximize lengths of pipework so that they can be easily reused by being trimmed to the desired
length [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• Design ductwork supports, grills, volume control dampers, ﬁre dampers for support[Addis and
Schouten (2004)]
• Use prefabricated elements for the vertical service risers with mechanical joints to enable and assist
deconstruction [Addis and Schouten (2004)]
• The circular cross-section air ducts shall have a small range of standard lengths and sizes and
integrated seals and clamps. Gradually decreasing cross-section in long distribution runs, could
be achieved by using identical ducts in parallel and reducing ducts number. This could enable
deconstruction and reassemble in new conﬁgurations [Addis and Schouten (2004)
OPEN_1 House
In Open_1 House an open raceway was built in interior walls base and is easily accessible by removing
the cover (Figure 29) [Guy and Ciarimboli (2006)]. This conﬁguration avoids entangling them within the
wall cavity and neither exposes them. The ﬂoor plenum system facilitates all the ductwork and other
utilities and is concealed by modular prefabricated ceiling panels that are laid into a wood frame that is
exposed. Plumbing goes through speciﬁc zones.
Figure 29: Removable baseboards enable access to wiring in Open_1 House [BAZED (2006)]
Exposed installations
Exposed installations are quite common in modern architecture. Installations are exposed mainly in the
interior of a building and even at the exterior. The most famous building where the installations were
set externally is Centre Georges Pompidou in Paris (Figure 30). The diﬀerent systems are painted with
diﬀerent colors to be distinguished. The largest ventilation components and structure were painted white,
elevator and stairs silver gray, plumbing and ﬁre control piping green, ventilation was painted blue and
electrical elements yellow and orange. Another interesting case is the building of Lloyds Headquarters in
London. In the interior of buildings, it is more common in cases of non residential houses.
Figure 30: The exterior of the Centre Georges Pompidou is dominted by the installations
50
==PAGE:53==
4.1.4
From guidelines to assessment tools
The need to assess a technical solution regarding how much it ﬁts to the Design for Disassembly concept
and how satisfyingly it performs comparing to other alternatives led some researches to introduce such
assessment tools.
Thormark 2001
In 2001, Catarina Thormark documented a simpliﬁed outline of a method to assess the ease of disassembly
based on: the risks in the labour environment, time resources and equipment needs, accessibility to joints
and damage level of the material during disassembly. Regarding material recycling and combustion, the
corresponding parameters aren’t presented. Furthermore the research sets some unsolved problems to be
investigated such as the proper choice of criteria for each assessment, whether minimum criteria should
be set for any categories and if diﬀerent weights must be assigned for each parameter [Thormark (2001)].
Thormark’s outline of the assessment method is demonstrated in Appendix A, Table 46.
Sassi 2002
Paola Sassi proposes the assessment of "suitability for reuse/ recycling/ down-cycling" (Appendix A -
Figure 47). The wide range of parameters is divided to 2 weighted categories, a technical related and
cost related category, with the ﬁrst being the most highly weighted. However, within the categories,
criteria scores are distributed evenly. For each criteria, the rating is speciﬁed by deﬁning the optimal and
the worst scenario. Similar to Thormark, Sassi sets no minimum requirements and although weights are
introduced, they are limited to the two categories and not for all the parameters.
Durmisevic 2006
Durmisevic introduced a knowledge model regarding assessment of Transformation Capacity (TC) of
structures and focuses mainly on disassembly potential (Appendix A - Figure 48). Durmisevic (2006)
divided the model to four levels of abstraction, with the fourth one being the Transformation Capacity.
Main indicators are considered the independence and the exchangeability. Next, these two indicators are
further divided to material levels, hierarchy (technical decomposition) and interface (physical decompo-
sition). The ﬁrst level is the sub-aspects (criteria) and the speciﬁcation of their impacts.This input-level
has 17 determining factors -independent variables- and 14 dependent variables.
Anne Sigrid Nordby 2009
Anne Sigrid Nordby in 2009 stated that in the previous tools, there was no direct connection between
the assessed parameters and the speciﬁc design guidelines and aimed to create an assessment tool from
directly transforming the DfD guidelines. The suggested systematization aimed to create a multipurpose
system, used for communicating the DfD principles, being used as an assessment tool for designing build-
ing components and ultimately being used as a checklist for designing new ones. The tool acknowledges
3 levels: component, construction and industry. Nordby fully omits time use and other economic related
criteria as it ﬁnancia cost is considered irrelevant considering the environmental beneﬁts. The overview
of the tool is demonstrated in Appendix A, Figure 54, and a case where it is used is presented in Figure
55. Weighting of criteria is assigned by the user as it is considered that it has a huge importance for
the quantitative result and should be adjusted to the objective of the assessment and the function of the
assessed objects, and consequently the weighting shall vary for each assessment.
Summing up, all these tools try to identify the optimized way of assessing the DfD potential of building
materials and components, or transformation capacity in case of Durmisevic (2006), while Nordby (2009)
introduced also criteria for buildings and industry-wide initiatives. The tools also diﬀer on the number of
the criteria, with Durmisevic (2006) creating the more analytical one, and only Sassi (2002) introduced
two standard weightings for technical and ﬁnancial related criteria. On the other hand Nordby (2009a)
proposed that the weights must be adjusted to the cntext of each assessment and the case object. Fur-
thermore Nordby totally ignores the ﬁnancial criteria although their importance is well acknowledged in
the report as they were considered of minor importance comparing to the environmental beneﬁts of DfD.
51
==PAGE:54==
4.2
Interviews results
Interviews resulted in the ﬁrst mapping of challenges in working with DfD within Denmark and are brieﬂy
demonstrated below. Challenges can be categorized under 6 main topics that are interacting: Business
case, Detailing, Material reuse, Materials’ lifespans, Material manufacturing and Prefabrication. The
analytic explanations of these challenges are demonstrated in Appendix C and the transcripts of the
interviews in Appendix B.
4.2.1
The business case
Building up the business case is maybe the main challenge as it can inﬂuence signiﬁcantly the market
trends and lead the building industry to reshaping.
The main challenges that derived regarding the
business case are the following:
• Lack of a business case or a business model [Bang (2017)]
• The lifespan of the investment is incompatible with the developers’ perspective [Bang (2017),
Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• The lifespan of the investment is incompatible with the clients’ perspective - DfD in parts dilemma
[Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Lack of a large player to guarantee the beneﬁts and handling of the problems in the long term -
Material ownership [Bang (2017)]
• No existing market for disassembled structural components [Horswill (2017)]
• Financial criterion at the ﬁrst steps [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Consultants as well as manufacturers have to rethink and adapt in order their business to survive
[Lewis (2017a)]
• There is a wide range of choices in working with sustainability and clients are not yet quite familiar
with DfD [Bang (2017)]
• DfD could unite all sustainability issues [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017), Lewis (2017a)]
• Not being mandatory by the regulations [Bang (2017)]
• Cultural challenges [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Changes in all the current chain (design - manufacturing - assembly - disassembly) [Nielsen (2017)]
• Be further credited by the certiﬁcation schemes [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017), Malabi Larsen
(2017), Bang (2017)]
4.2.2
Materials’ lifespans
Material lifespans are critical for forecasting and setting the allowed times a material can be reused and
for estimating environmental impacts, ﬁnancial revenues, etc.
• Lack of unbiased, accurate and widely accepted data [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Lack of standards for making assumptions regarding lifespans [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Need for regionalizing lifespans in Denmark based on climatic data [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
52
==PAGE:55==
4.2.3
Product and material reuse
The section regarding materials includes a wide range of challenges regarding the reuse of the materials.
Furthermore a subchapter with the challenges regarding the manufacturing of products that are suitable
for DfD is included.
• Need for documentation of existing materials and their properties (composition, strength etc)
[Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017), Nielsen (2017)]
• How documentation shall be stored [Nielsen (2017)]
• Old parts may become obsolete with time and incompatible with the regulations [Nielsen (2017)]
• Reusability of structural systems [Horswill (2017)]
• Legislation limitations in reusing materials [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
4.2.4
Product manufacturing
Materials’ manufacturers have to deal with a diﬀerent range of challenges regarding the production of
materials or the adjustment of the existing ones in order to facilitate the DfD principles.
• Combine DfD with practical and ﬁnancial aspects [Vibæk (2017)]
• Huge alterations in the production line and the supply chain in case of an existing product [Vibæk
(2017)]
• Identifying the needed level of disassembly for expanding the product lifecycle [Vibæk (2017)]
• Deciding the optimal solution for collecting or not the used products can be ﬁnancially and envi-
ronmentally blurry. [Vibæk (2017)]
• Contamination aﬀects reuse [Vibæk (2017)]
4.2.5
Detailing
Structural and architectural detailing face a range of challenges regarding technical, practical and leg-
islative issues.
• DfD must be considered from the early stages for both structural and architectural designs [Horswill
(2017), Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Material choice is complex as legislative requirements (energy eﬃciency, air-tightness, etc), main-
tenance, environmental impacts and other parameters have to be also considered [Lewis (2017a),
Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017), Nielsen (2017)]
• Most architects aren’t familiar with the DfD and even having an external auditor isn’t enough
[Lewis (2017a)]
• Considering future structural needs (overdesign) [Horswill (2017)]
• Connecting precast concrete elements in a way that allows easy disassembly and reuse [Horswill
(2017)]
• Limited inﬂuence of architects to the ﬁnal material choice and to the working site (contractors
choose the speciﬁc materials) [Lewis (2017a)]
• Material choice for the non structural part doesn’t take place in the beginning - has to adapt to
existing designs [Lewis (2017a)]
• Not explicitly documented with speciﬁc examples and applications by DGNB [Malabi Larsen (2017)]
53
==PAGE:56==
4.2.6
Prefabrication related challenges
This section of the challenges refers the limitations that could derive from the use of prefabricated ele-
ments.
• Contractors are sceptic regarding untested prefab solutions and prefab elements in general [Kisslinger
and Lucardi (2017)]
• Previous problems with modular toilet / bathroom units create a bad background for prefab [Bang
(2017)]
• Limitations regarding size and transportation through regular streets [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
• Prefabrication was always linked to cheap and low quality ways of building [Kisslinger and Lucardi
(2017)]
• Buildings are customized for the initial design and reconﬁguring is diﬃcult [Horswill (2017)]
• Will standardization limit architecture [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
4.2.7
DfD and DGNB certiﬁcation
Most of the interviewees acknowledged the importance of the DGNB certiﬁcation as a driver for sustain-
ability initiatives. Consequently, based on the freely accessed "Guide tilDGNB for bygninger", which is
an introduction to the DGNB guide, and an interview with Leonora Charlotte Malabi Larsen - sustain-
ability consultant at MT Højgaard with experience on DfD - a brief mapping of the criteria that could
be potentially inﬂuenced by DfD are presented below. It should be highlighted that a more valid and
detailed analysis can be performed only by a trained and certiﬁed DGNB auditor with full access to the
DGNB scheme. The purpose of the following analysis is only to serve as a brief demonstration of the
eﬀect of DfD within the DGNB certiﬁcation scheme.
Directly inﬂuenced indicator
The only indicator that refers directly to DfD is “TEC 1.6 Suitability for dismantling and recycling”, as
it acknowledges the impact of the construction sector in material consumption and waste generation in
Denmark and aims to increase focus and knowledge levels about dismantling, disassembly, sorting and
recycling of materials already in the early design phases [DGBC (2016), Malabi Larsen (2017)]. The crite-
rion evaluates whether the materials can be separated and sorted for recycling / reuse while maintaining
the highest possible value, as well as from the building’s general disassembly and reuse plan [DGBC
(2016)]. The evaluation is also based on the results obtained using DGNB’s LCA tool [DGBC (2016)].
This criteria counts for 1.5% of the total scoring in the certiﬁcation [DGBC (2016)], being one of the
lowest credited indicators of the DGNB certiﬁcation scheme compared to other criteria such as ENV1.1
Life Cycle Assessment, which "is paradoxical since TEC 1.6 has the means to improve the environmental
impacts assessed in ENV1.1." [Malabi Larsen (2017)]. DGNB considers 3 levels of disassembly: hard
(destructive disassembly), medium (manual labour and machines) and easy (manual labour and simple
tools) [Diraoui and Larsen (2016)].
The indicator includes some mandatory strategies similar to the Rules of Thumb, but it doesn’t provide
any concrete strategies or case examples for the implementation of DfD [Malabi Larsen (2017)]. In addi-
tion the method for evaluation does not specify any speciﬁc extend of achieved disassembly and recycling
/ reuse threshold to attain each grade (Minimum – Medium – High) [Malabi Larsen (2017)]. The lack
of readily applicable systems and strategies in the DGNB certiﬁcation system, along with the vague
grading system results in partial implementation of DfD in a building to be fully credited if thoroughly
documented.
Indirectly inﬂuenced indicators as results of DfD
DfD has an indirect positive eﬀect on several other indicators, besides for TEC 1.6, that are signiﬁcantly
credited. Speciﬁcally, the categories that involve life cycle perspective such as “ENV 1.1 LCA –Environ-
mental impacts”, “ENV 2.1 LCA – Primary energy” and “ECO 1.1 Building related lifecycle costs” can be
54
==PAGE:57==
strongly inﬂuenced by the high material reuse and recycling that is achieved through DfD [Malabi Larsen
(2017)]. Furthermore DfD can assist the “ECO 2.1 Flexibility and adaptability” criterion, which is a
tough criterion to meet with the traditional way of building [Malabi Larsen (2017)]. ECO 2.1 aims to
increase the buildings ﬂexibility and thus reduce the risk of the building losing its use value and not
being utilized optimally. ECO 2.1 evaluates 2 main criteria, the building geometry and the possibility of
division into several units, including the space use, space height, building depth, vertical access routes,
layout planning, construction and technical installations [DGBC (2016)]. Furthermore, in the case of
extractable ground screw foundations made of galvanized steel or similar types of foundation, the “ENV
2.3 Eﬀective land use” would be beneﬁted due to their negligible interference with the underground or-
ganisms and the fact that they can be fully extracted.
Indirectly inﬂuenced indicators through the guidelines of DfD
Guidelines for DfD also have a indirect positive inﬂuence on a wide range of criteria’s.
Speciﬁcally,
"ENV 1.2 Environmental risks regarding building products" and "ENV 1.3 Environmental impacts from
the extraction of the materials" focus on the choice of healthy, non-hazardous and certiﬁed materials
[Malabi Larsen (2017), DGBC (2016)] similar to the DfD guidelines. Furthermore the criteria "ECO 2.2
Robustness" is inﬂuenced partially by the high materials lifespan and the adaptability of the building
to diﬀerent functions (oﬃce - house) which are also included in the guidelines. Ultimately, “TEC 1.5
Building maintenance and cleaning equipment” could be also slightly inﬂuenced by the DfD principles as
they aim to enable the access and the optimal maintenance of all the building parts in order to maximize
their lifespans. Ultimately, “TEC 1.5 Building maintenance and cleaning equipment”could also be slightly
inﬂuenced by the DfD principles as they aim to enable the access and the proper maintenance of all the
building parts in order to maximize their lifespan.
All these guidelines and their corresponding inﬂuence in the total DGNB score are demonstrated in the
next Figure. It can be demonstrated that although only TEC 1.6 refers directly to DfD, the implementa-
tion of DfD can boost the overall performance and it can be demonstrated what Lewis (2017a) described
that DfD can unite all the sustainability issues.
55
==PAGE:58==
Figure 31: DGNB criteria. Red highlight demonstrates direct link of DfD and a criterion, yellow indirect
eﬀect through the results and green eﬀect through the guidelines for DfD [adapted from DGBC (2016)]
56
==PAGE:59==
4.3
Workshop results
The questions and the corresponding answers the attendants gave are demonstrated below. The answers
were set in an hierarchy according to the frequency that they appeared within the workshop, the previous
research and their relevance to the question. Workshop results are also available online [InnoBYG (2017)]
4.3.1
Market potential
Q1. What are the 3 biggest challenges DfD in a business context? Keywords: What is the driver? Public
regulation or free market forces? Grants / support schemes? Connected to legislation and standardiza-
tion?
1. How can someone have a guarantee that old materials will be used in new buildings?
2. Income from selling used materials should be higher compared to demolition expenses
3. There is a critical volume of a material that must be available in order to have a market
4. Only materials of high quality should be used in order to enable reuse
5. Higher cost of system and design
6. Higher requirements are needed during assembly
Q2. Name the most important ways in which the construction industry and its value chain will be aﬀected
by this. Keywords: Which parts of the market? Which actors could beneﬁt? Who shall own the materials
in terms of guarantees and responsibilities? Big or small companies would be the best option? Which
products have the biggest market potential?
1. Reuse phase shall be ﬁgured out (demolition, storage, logistics and quality control of reused materials,
reuse)
2. Market of used construction products
3. Material manufacturers have to adapt
4. Demolition branch has to adapt
5. DfD will help bigger companies
Q3. What 3 concrete ideas do you have on how this project can handle these issues?
1. Proof of concept (LCA, LCC, 1:1 mock up)
2. Cross sector cooperation (architects - engineers - suppliers)
3. Intensive studies on construction techniques (how can DfD principles be introduced - how can we
change from the nail gun to screwdriver)
4. Requirements of legislation
5. Provide examples. "Here-and-Now" Quality assessments. Value and verify these construction tech-
niques
4.3.2
Materials
Q1. Which 3 material properties / characteristics are most important in relation to DfD? Keywords:
Content of harmful substances? / Patination / Need for ﬁnishing / Quality / Value - Economic, Cul-
tural, Environmental / Aesthetics?
1. Robustness and dimensions
2. Health
3. Appropriate lifespans
Q2. In which 3 ways will DfD change the way we think of materials in relation to prefabrication today?
Keywords: Economic / Environmental / Business / New manufacturing methods/ Production lines?
1. Accept change in aesthetics
2. Use materials that can undergo disassembly
Q3. What 3 concrete ideas do you have on how this project can handle these issues?

4.3.3
Detailing
Q1. What are the 3 biggest challenges when building needs to be detailed in order to be separated again?
Keywords: Architectural / Technical / Tectonic / Standardization / Compliance with building engineering
requirements / Speciﬁc connections and joining techniques?
1. Layered construction and ﬂexibility regarding changes of functions of a building (missing knowledge
and strategies in the design phase with regards to ﬂexibility)
2. Careful with material choice and need for corresponding data
3. Need for consideration of ﬁreprotection, airtightness, thermal bridges due to regulations
4. Increased standarizing can create possible limitations on design and architecture
5. Demand and motivations for DfD
Q2. Name 3 examples of how to implement these conditions in prefabricated construction. Keywords:
Production lines / Manufacturing methods / New technologies / Element-, component-, material level /
Standardization?
1. Standardization of building components
2. Change the way we gather and develop components
3. Less choices lead to lower complexity
4. Less need for calculations
5. Legislation regarding requirements for DfD. More pilot projects that show the beneﬁts and the choices
6. Work on technical solution in smaller scale Focus on smaller buildings for start
Q3. What 3 concrete ideas do you have on how this project can handle these issues?
1. Identify what is the best way to work with standardization of components in order to minimize the
limitations
2. Document the deconstruction process
3. Measuring waste during disassembly process
4. Measure waste in each case
4.3.4
Material lifespans

2. Diﬀerent strategies for diﬀerent lifespans
3. Digitalizing and mapping of buildings’ materials and components
4. Construction / Deconstruction plan must be implemented

and responsibility / Big or small companies are the best option / Which products have the greatest market
potential?

2. Stricter legislation will be applied on the building industry
3. Installations must adapt

1. Element catalog - What can be available soon - Forecast of sales
2. Project can set the basis for legislation
