4.3.5
General workshop conclusions and proposals for the next steps of the project
Based on the discussion and the proposals that were either introduced through the group work or discussed
by the speakers and the audience the following proposals derived:
• Create a building element directory - an urban mining infrastructure that publishes what will be
available within an upcoming period.
• The project can be a demonstrator for establishing the development of the relevant legislation as
there is no such basis today.
• Provide examples of implementing DfD, veriﬁed by engineers and along with all the related costs.
• Examine diﬀerent business models for leasing of materials similar to previous cases (Ege Tæpper).
• Assess what DfD means economically for a building’s lifetime and investigate the overall conse-
quences.
• Investigate how components could be standardized in the DfD perspective, to minimize constraints.
• Measure waste production and streams when implementing DfD
• Provide proof of concept (LCA, business case, 1: 1 mock up)
• Test diﬀerent DfD ideas
• Examine how DfD can support maintenance of buildings.
• Establish collaboration across industries and with companies with several professional groups rep-
resented. For example, architects, engineers and suppliers.
• Make intensive studies of production about where to implement speciﬁc DfD principles. (For ex-
ample, replace the nail gun with a screwdriver.)
Ultimately, during the workshop, it was widely discussed that facades and installations could be considered
as "low-hanging fruits" for DfD and their more thorough examination should take place.
4.4
Summing major challenges
Based on the interviews and the workshop, a wide mapping of the challenges with regard to DfD took
place. Combining these results, the main challenges regarding the already ﬁgured 6 main aspects of DfD
are demonstrated in the next ﬁgure and are speciﬁcally deﬁned afterwards.
59
==PAGE:62==
Figure 32: Main challenges of DfD
60
==PAGE:63==
4.4.1
Business case
The challenges regarding the business case could be grouped under 3 main categories.
Time frame of the investment
The lifespan of a building in Denmark can be over 100 years and, only then, the beneﬁts from the decom-
missioning and reselling or reusing, at least of the structural layer, can be earned. Consequently, specially
in the case of Denmark, this investment seems meaningless in ﬁnancial terms for a developer according
to Mr. Bang. However DfD could me more meaningful for the rest parts of a building that
have a shorter lifespan. Such an example could be the renewal of the external facades, as in the case
of MT Højgaard [Malabi Larsen (2017)], or of the inner facades. Furthermore, in the common case that
the developer isn’t the ﬁnal user of the building, the developer aims for a quick, low-cost and low-risk
investment. According to Mr Bang, DfD is more appealing in organizations that have such long business
plans such as pension funds and also as part of the CSR policy of companies.
One more eﬀect of the lifespan of the investment is that it is quite diﬃcult to predict and guarantee the
beneﬁts from the reselling of the materials. Considering that the original seller will be possibly no longer
in the market after so many decades there is a gap regarding the guaranteeing of the investment. Even
more, it is unambiguous whether there will be market for used structural components. This problem
worsens when considering that there is no such business case to document for example that the materials
can indeed be reused at the EoL of the building when it take place. In order to handle this gap, new ways
of marketing building materials shall be identiﬁed. Such an example could be the leasing of the building
materials from the provider to the user of the building [Bang (2017)].
Lack of motives
Currently DfD seems to the developers and the clients as only one of the possible choices of working
with sustainability and considering the weaknesses of the investment it is often neglected. Environmental
drivers aren’t thoroughly documented and the stakeholders aren’t familiar with the potential environmen-
tal beneﬁts. Furthermore, considering the example of the legislation regarding the energy requirements
and its positive and wide eﬀects, legislative requirements could skyrocket the implementation of DfD.
Similarly, certiﬁcation schemes can also give a signiﬁcant push to DfD. Today though most certiﬁcation
schemes don’t have such a criterion, while DGNB in case of Denmark, although it includes a criterion for
DfD, it has a low direct contribution to the overall score and it doesn’t include neither detailed guidance
nor analytic benchmarks for the grading [Malabi Larsen (2017)]. Consequently the ﬁrst step would be
to better document the process within the framework of DGNB as its implementation is still in a quite
immature level in order to become mandatory. However in the long term, considering the impacts of the
building sector to resource depletion and assuming the continuous improvement of DfD techniques, it
could eventually be included in the legislation according of course to the orientation of the governments
on the ﬁeld as Mr Bang noted.
Changes all across the industry
Last but not least, DfD could cause signiﬁcant alterations in the building industry in general. The way
architects design, the reuse of structural frames, established markets of reused materials and increasing
market demands on the disassembly potential of buildings could cause signiﬁcant changes to the tradi-
tional structure of the industry. Product manufacturers could be the most inﬂuenced stakeholders as
they will have to change maybe their whole production lines. In the same time however new players
appear or even traditional players that are quickly adapted play a more vital role in the emerging DfD
market. It also seems that in this new market, a new generation of producers of prefabricated elements
and buildings is emerging.
4.4.2
Lifespans of Materials
Regarding material lifespans, the issues that were acknowledged can be grouped under 2 main issues.
Lack of accurate data and common standards for making estimations
61
==PAGE:64==
Currently there is a wide range of structural materials and the lifespans of a signiﬁcant fraction are
highly uncertain. Characteristically, as Mr Lombardi mentioned, there are even contradictions among
LCCbyg / LCAbyg and SBI reports. Uncertainties are even higher though for new materials that have
no documented examples of average lifespans, such as in case of CLT. Furthermore, several data are
provided from the producers and thus could be biased. As Mr Lombardi highlighted, the lifespans are
adapted based on the climatic data of another country and they should be adapted to the danish data.
This problem gets worse when it comes to making assumptions, as due to lack of standards for making
assumptions "you can point your calculations everywhere you want, so you use the numbers that reach
what you want to reach [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]". Consequently, there is an urgent need for data
on material lifespans and robust standards on making assumptions about lifespans.
Diﬀerent strategies for diﬀerent lifespans
The design of a building must consider the lifespans of the materials that are used in order to optimize their
use, fully exploit their lifespans and to ensure the hierarchical assembly of the building as it minimizes
interference with components that have longer lifespan and enable the easier accessibility to the more
often changed parts.
4.4.3
Detailing
Challenges in detailing can be divided in two main categories regarding architectural and structural de-
tailing.
Main challenges regarding structural detailing
In the case of structural detailing DfD must be considered from the very beginning as the appropriate
structural framework and structural grid shall be selected. A big challenge regarding structural detailing
is working with concrete elements. Although precast elements are more appropriate there are still several
challenges. Speciﬁcally connection of columns and walls is a huge challenge as concrete is poured in the
connections. Furthermore a big challenge is to consider the ﬂexibility of the building in combination with
working with prefabricated elements and trying to keep the dimensions of the elements in a speciﬁc range.
For example, when trying to create large column-free spaces but also using elements of manageable size in
order to be prefabricated, transferred to site through regular roads and assembled/ disassembled without
the use of crane lifts. Further challenge is the fact that the personnel is untrained regarding DfD and
also that there is a wider knowledge and experience gap on the ﬁeld.
Main challenges regarding architectural detailing
Architectural detailing faces several challenges with regards to DfD. DfD poses a new way of designing
and working with materials that architects aren’t familiar with. Consequently architects must be given
the necessary training and become familiar with the corresponding knowledge and practices along with
the rest of the personnel. This could be assisted with more detailed guidelines and examples from the sus-
tainability certiﬁcations schemes. Another huge challenges is the proper material choice. Material choice
is a signiﬁcant parameter for DfD as they have to be healthy, high quality, with high tolerances and no
needs for coating and glues. Considering that materials have also to fulﬁll legislative and budget demands,
material choice becomes a quite complex process. Furthermore, usually architects have limited inﬂuence
in the material choice, as most often it takes place by the developers and according to the available budget.
Architects have also to face the challenges by working with standardized prefabricated elements and
standard, simple structural grids. Architects have to use elements with simple geometries and be careful
with the material choice as already mentioned. Standardized production, as always used to, limits the
ﬁeld for architects and prefabricators as "Scandi byg" may end up with a "big catalogue of solutions
and you have just to pick the right one in that case and maybe we could ask an architect what we can
do about the facade" as Mr Kisslinger highlighted. As a result architects have to be innovative and
re-identify their role and the ways they can work with standardization within the DfD framework, while
both architects and their clients have to adjust to the potential changes in the aesthetics.
62
==PAGE:65==
4.4.4
Reuse of components and materials
For the reuse of building components materials, there are several challenges that have to be handled.
The biggest challenge is the lack of information, as it was almost unanimously accepted by all the stake-
holders. "Missing knowledge about the existing, makes it not possible to reuse as well sometimes" as Mr
Kisslinger highlighted. Documentation of the composition of a material is critical as small changes in the
composition can have dramatic changes in its properties as Mr Vibæek highlighted. In addition precise
documentation of impurities can lead to the production of high quality recycled products with "more
well known properties and higher value [Nielsen (2017)]". Furthermore documentation of the estimated
strength of a component - mainly in the case of structural components - is also critical for its reuse
according to Mr Nielsen. More speciﬁcally the reuse of the structural systems has several issues and
"there’s generally a problem with the theory of DfD [Horswill (2017)]" as for example the ﬂoor span and
the loading must be exactly the same.
Another challenge is that the old parts may become obsolete with time and incompatible with the regula-
tions. This is apparent during the last decade with the continuous tightening of the legislation regarding
energy consumption and also due to the identiﬁcation of unknown hazards from speciﬁc materials. Ul-
timately there are further legislative issues with material reuse, as the used materials are dealt as new
materials that have to be certiﬁed again.
Typical example is the case of reused bricks from Gamle
Mursten, where although the original bricks had a CE Marking, the salvaged ones didn’t.
4.4.5
Manufacturing of products
A huge challenge in manufacturing products that are disassemblable and ﬁt to DfD principles. Design of
the product shall also consider the manufacturability of the product and also the economic parameters
except from the disassembly potential according to Mr Vibæk. In case of existing products, there are
even challenges as changing a product to become more disassemblable, might lead to huge alterations in
its production line and its supply chain. Manufacturers have also to make decisions on challenging issues
as the collection or not of their used products and also to identifying the ideal level that their product
must be disassemblable.
4.4.6
Prefabrication
Prefabrication is repeatedly acknowledged as a basic principle of DfD. In case of prefabrication though
there are several challenges to be handled. Firstly developers are sceptic regarding untested prefab solu-
tions and prefab elements in general. Considering that constructions are usually signiﬁcant investments
and also some bad experiences with the prefab toilet units in the past, developers are afraid to be the
ﬁrst to invest. This problem is also magniﬁed due to the fact that prefabrication was traditionally linked
to cheap and low quality ways of building. Furthermore, except from trusting prefab solutions, the in-
terested parts have also to accept the change in aesthetics due to the use of standardized prefab solutions.
Ultimately, prefabrication imposes limitations on the size of the elements as they usually have to be
transferred by road to the site. This fact is in accordance with the guideline of having small size elements
that can be handled with out the use of crane lifts but could be an obstacle in cases of larger beams that
aim to create wide ﬂexible column-free spaces.
4.5
The case house
The case house until the end of this thesis wasn’t in its ﬁnal shape and many parts are still under discus-
sion, while no data are yet published. In addition due to the deriving patents, no detailing is presented
and only some basic data are presented here by. In addition, since the house is still in the design process
this study aims to identify its sustainability potential, highlight problematic parts of the design and to
assess diﬁerent alternatives.
In every case the house was adjusted similarly to the reference house in order to assist LCA comparison.
As a result, the dimensions of the house were suited to the reference house for a more accurate comparison
and are 16x10 meters (160 square meter gross area). The grid is orthogonal but the building shape and
the room conﬁguration are aimed to be easily adjustable to the needs of the user. This fact is aimed to
be facilitated by the conﬁguration of the outer walls, as for their construction smaller CLT "boxes" with
dimensions of 200x400mm and 300mm thickness are designed. These "boxes" are screwed on a frame
63
==PAGE:66==
that allows the removal of a fraction of them in order to be replaced for example from a window or a new
"box". This system also enables parallel assembly and disassembly of the outer wall. For the inner wall,
typical 3 layered CLT elements are considered to be used.
The foundations used are ground screws similar to the ones produced by Krinner [Krinner (2017)]. The
screws could be an ideal solution for removable foundations with a minimum impact to the environment.
For the facades, natural slate is examined as an alternative to OSB for the facades and also as an alter-
native to the roof tiles. In the case of the installations, they haven’t yet been examined and as a result
only a standard solution, similar to the LCAs of the reference house and the upcycle house [Rasmussen
and Birgisdóttir (2013)], was used for the LCA. The corresponding data were found in the inventory of
the SBi 2015:09 report. In a similar way the inventory for the roof of the case study derived after some
adjustments in order to ﬁt better to the DfD concept.
In the next table, the compliance of the case with the "Rules of Thumb" is examined. The table doesn’t
consider the detailing of the installations.

As the Table displays, the case house facilitates most of the rules. The main rules that aren’t fulﬁlled are
the documentation of the components’ properties and the development of the deconstruction plan, but
they could in the next stages of the design process. Ultimately, in the case of the "hierarchical assembly"
of the components, the rule wasn’t fulﬁlled for the ﬂoor frame as the insulation is below elements with
higher lifespans. This is aimed to be tackled by enabling full accessibility directly to the insulation so
that no other elements to be aﬀected. This example shows that "Most reusable / recyclable parts must be
the most accessible" (guideline No 18), could be an alternative to hierarchical assembly (Guideline No 20).

4.6
LCA results
In this section the key results from the LCA of the case house are presented. There are two basic scenarios
that are examined and they correspond to the two forecasted marginal electricity and heat sources, natural
gas (Scenario 1) and biomass (Scenario 2). The consumption of these energy sources is substituted by the
energy produced by the incineration of wood and wood based elements and they are both examined due
to the huge uncertainty over the future energy sources and the quite diﬀerent crediting that this choice
grants to this LCA. These basic scenarios do not include reuse and the reuse of elements is examined
separately. More analytic results from the LCA can be found in Appendices D to M.

The main results

Ceramics (23%)
Metals (2%)

material bill is introduced. As the ﬁgures shows, the

fact makes the proper handling of the EoL treatment
of wood and the decision of the future marginal energy
sources even more critical. It should be highlighted that
toxicity related results don’t include impacts from wood
ﬁbre insulation and CLT production as they weren’t
available in the corresponding EPDs. Considering that
these materials are almost the half of the material volume, make the corresponding results questionable,
but they are still included in the report due to the importance of the toxicity categories [Rasmussen et al.
(2013)].

The main results reveal that for both scenarios impacts have similar magnitudes. As expected, avoiding
the consumption of a fossil fuel, natural gas, credits the depletion of fossil fuels impact category for
Scenario 1. In addition, the GWP impacts from the use of natural gas are quite higher comparing to the
use of biomass and as a result the substitution of natural gas consumption gives high credits on the GWP
category for scenario 1. GWP and Abiotic depletion of fossil fuels are 55% and 63% reduced respectively
for Scenario 1. ODP is the only negative score and is the highest diﬀerence in favour of the biomass
scenario. Scenario 2 is performing also better in Eutrophication and slightly in all the toxicity related
categories except Marine aquatic ecotoxicity category.

Figure 34 shows that "Marine aquatic toxicity" is the most impacted category comparing to the rest
with at least an order of magnitude diﬀerence with the next most important impact categories. Next
are again toxicity related impact categories, and they also diﬀer more than one order of magnitude from
the rest of the results. According to the normalized results, the only impact category that scenario 2 is
clearly outperforming scenario 1, ODP, is the least signiﬁcant impact category. In addition, regarding
the most important categories, the toxicity related ones, the performance for the two scenarios is similar.
Ultimately, the addition of toxicity impacts from the production of wood ﬁbre insulation and CLT would
further raise these impacts, equally for both scenarios, as they represent a signiﬁcant fraction of the
materials according to Figure 33 and Appendix D.

4.6.3
Contribution of building parts and processes
Figures 36 and 37 demonstrate the share of impacts of each building part for the two basic scenarios.
Regarding the ﬁrst scenario, Figure 36, shows that windows and roof often are responsible for the major
part of the impacts and especially in the GWP and depletion of fossil fuels categories. This fact derives
by the crediting the other parts receive due to the high fraction of wood products they include. However,
considering the quite low fraction of glass in the material bill, the impacts of windows is rather high.
In addition the metal based materials and the corresponding parts that include them, foundations and
HVAC, tend to have low impacts as well. In general it is the building parts that contain materials which
are downcycled, e.g. roof tiles in case of roof, or landﬁlled, e.g. triple glaze glass in case of windows, that
have higher portion of impacts relatively to the quantity of the materials they contain.

which also dominates the MAETP category. Other products that tend to cause signiﬁcant impacts are
the hot water tank, for the TETP, HTP and FAETP categories and windows that have a standard and
rather high fraction of impacts among all categories comparing to the fraction of windows in the material

4.6.4
Stages the impacts occur
Figure 39a shows the distribution of the impacts across the diﬀerent stages when substituting natural gas
as a marginal energy source. The A stage is the production of the materials that are used in the house,
B includes only the replacements of materials in this case, C refers to the waste treatment processes
and D includes the "Next product system", where the beneﬁts from the avoided production of building
materials or energy due to recycling and incineration are credited. Consequently, the diﬀerence of the
two scenarios is only in the magnitude of the D stage. ODP isn’t presented in both Figures 39a and
39b, while ADPf is demonstrated only in 39b due to presentation obstacles that derive from the huge
ﬂuctuations. Distribution of GWP impacts are presented separately afterwards. Analytic distribution of
impacts across the stages is presented in Appendix J.

4.6.4.1
Distribution of the GWP impacts and carbon storage
Figure 40 demonstrates the distribution of GWP across the stages of the building’s lifecycle. Similar to
the rest of the impact categories, although the replacements (B stage) are only a fraction of the initial
bill [Appendix K], they have similar impacts with the production of the whole house (A stage). The
fact that CO2 sequestration is quite smaller in B stage, shows that the replacements are mainly non-
wooden products. The Figure demonstrates that the overall impact from biogenic carbon is neutral, as
the carbon that was credited in Stages A and B as an input, is released in Stage C. This was achieved
with the calculations in separate Excel sheets and the calculations are presented in Appendix F. This
adjustment was needed for two reasons. Firstly, there is a mismatch between the carbon content of the
several wooden products and the only one "wood building waste" process, and also because the wood
products are credited for further quantities of carbon storage, while the "wood building waste" process
isn’t. Ecoinvent credits to the wooden end-products the carbon sequestrated to the initial corresponding
quantity of wood in the forests. Although this quantity is credited in the GWP indicator, this doesn’t
mean that it is sequestrated within the building as well, but it refers to other by products and is allocated
to the products used for this LCA.

Figure 40: Distribution of the GWP impacts and the inﬂuence of carbon sequestration for the natural
gas substitution scenario
For calculating the actual carbon sequestration within the case house, as already analyzed in Chapter
3.3.8, the properties of the products regarding their carbon content and dry density, as they are given
by the Ecoinvent database, were used.
Based on calculations presented in Appendix G, the actual
carbon sequestration within the building products is estimated. Carbon sequestrated from materials that
are used as replacements, is replacing in the "carbon sink" the carbon that is released from the initial
materials, so the same quantity of carbon remains stored for the whole building’s lifetime. According to
the calculations presented in Appendix G and Table 27, there are approximately 13 tones of biogenic CO2
that are credited by Ecoinvent and excluding this amount, brings the permanent actual sequestration
from 85,7 tn to 72,6 tn CO2 for the whole building. This quantity is distributed in the building parts
and wood materials according to Figure 41.

4.6.5
Reuse
As already mentioned in Chapter 3.3.7, 3 scenarios are examined regarding reuse:
• Scenario 1: No reuse
• Scenario 2: One reuse cycle of outer and inner walls (CLT and insulation), wooden beams in the
roof and 25% of ground screws
• Scenario 3: Two reuse cycles of outer and inner walls (CLT and insulation), wooden beams in the
roof and 25% of ground screws
The impacts from the elements that are reused, along with the resulting total impacts, are demonstrated
in Appendix I.1. The reuse cycles refer almost exclusively to wood and wood based products. Considering
that these products have lower impacts comparing to other building materials and that they also have
algebraically negative contribution to the GWP, ADPf and ODP categories for the ﬁrst scenario [Appendix
I.1], the allocation of their impacts in more than one product systems, makes their minor impact more
negligible or even reduces their positive inﬂuence.

in ODP led to a decrease of the also negative total ODP, while similarly for TETP the changes was
negligible. The rest of the impact categories were improved and the most improved categories was POCP
(15% reduction for two reuse cycles). The total GWP and ADPf were both improved by 9% for 2 reuse
cycles.
4.6.6
Other scenarios
Based on the building’s parts’ and materials’ contribution to the LCIA, it was decided to implement an
alternative scenario for one of the building’s materials. Based on the analysis from Chapter 4.6.3, roof
tiles are one of the material categories that have signiﬁcant contribution to the results, while the speciﬁc
material choice isn’t mandatory or unavoidable for the system, as in the case of the hot water tank and
the windows. It was decided that a suitable alternative for the roof tiles would be the use of natural slate.
Based on a project for a Nordic LCA database by Magdalini Psarra and the author, it was identiﬁed
that although natural slate is a ﬁnite natural resource, on the other hand, it requires negligible treatment
for shawing, shaping and beveling, while it is considered that if it is properly installed, it can last for
centuries [Cárdenes (2016)]. Unfortunately the only available impacts derive by an EPD from CUPA
(2009), that contains only limited information and range of impact categories. Unfortunately MAETP is
one of the excluded categories. The results are demonstrated in the following Tables.

According to these Tables, natural slate improves the performance of the building regarding GWP by 8%

is itself a ﬁnite natural resource it performs equally with the roof tiles. Slate however performs about 5
time worse regarding the ODP indicator. Considering the normalization results, Figures 34 and 35, the
initial level of ODP impacts is rather negligible comparing to other impact categories. Considering the
simplicity of the production process and the inherent properties of natural slate, it would be certainly
meaningful to further examine natural slate as an alternative to the roof tiles when data are available,
but based on the current data, a safe conclusion can’t be made.

==PAGE:75==
5
Discussion
5.1
Rules of Thumb
In this section, the results of the review for the "Rules of Thumb" and the assessment tools are further
discussed. Furthermore proposals are made regarding the optimization of the RoT and the assessment
tools.
5.1.1
Generic guidelines
Generic guidelines include 34 guidelines out of which only 2 are referring to only one EoL scenario. This
fact shows that the proposal of Crowther (2001) is an eﬃcient way of presenting them. Comparing the
number of guidelines with other references in the literature, the following Table derives.
Table 21: Number of guidelines for DfD and the corresponding reference
Reference
Number of guidelines for DfD
Comment
Koukoulopoulos 2017
32
This thesis
Crowther (2001)
27
Introduced this structure of guidelines
Fletcher (2001b)
34
"Market" guidelines not counted
Durmisevic (2006)
37
Nordby (2009b)
25
"Industry level guidelines not counted
Thormark (2001)
18
As the table demonstrates the number of Rules of thumb is within the common results of the literature.
The main diﬀerences of the guidelines are the range of their context, the speciﬁcity on the system level
they are referring to and the way of structuring them, that is already discussed in the methodology part.
Potential improvements of RoT
The generic rules could be further built-up by concluding on "optimal - mediocre - basic" examples for
each guideline similar to Sassi (2002). In addition, the hierarchy of the rules could be also further built
up through the use of weightings. Some weightings are slightly introduced in the assessment tools in the
literature but they are quite limited. Such an example, Sassi (2002) introduces 2 diﬀerent weights for
technical and ﬁnancial criteria, while Nordby (2009b) also provides an example of implementing weighting
but it is proposed that it should be adjusted for each case by the user. Durmisevic (2006) uses the more
extended weighting system as weights assigned for each criterion of the assessment tool but not also for
the guidelines. Considering that prioritizing the guidelines is a diﬃcult process, as they have totally dif-
ferent nature and purposes, application of weighting would need ﬁrst a further grouping of the guidelines
according to speciﬁc technical results, e.g. "accessibility to connections". If such a grouping took place
then, the importance of each guideline towards this speciﬁc technical result, could be more easily weighted.
A useful and practical addition would be the introduction of minimum levels for each guideline that is
needed towards a speciﬁc EoL scenario. Furthermore, as Nordby (2009b) proposed, the guidelines would
ideally have a three-fold purpose: To be a pedagogic system able to communicate the principles, to be
convertible an assessment tool for assessing existing building components and ultimately to be used as
a checklist for designing salvageable components and materials. Consequently RoT could be adjusted to
this goal as well.
However, considering the many characteristics of each material, product or building layer, the plurality
of ways of connecting them and the lack of knowledge and experience on DfD, more detailed guidelines
and advice would also be quite useful. Such an example is Addis and Schouten (2004) that provided
speciﬁc guidelines for material reuse and recycling through DfD, for several building layers and a range of
materials. Usually though these guidelines derive along with the experience from case studies such as the
"De Fire Styrelser" project by MT Højgaard, 3XN Architects, Baslev and DEAS in 2014 on a building
with concrete elements or purely in forms of reviews such as Silverstein (2009) in case of steel structures.
In a wider perspective, while also considering the main challenges regarding DfD in general (Chapter 4.4),
signiﬁcant improvement for the RoT would be the further integration with the principles of other designing
73
==PAGE:76==
methods such as design for production / manufacturability and "design for economy" [Vibæk (2017)].
Also, ideally, guidelines could also assist the compliance with the legislation regarding for example ﬁre
safety and energy consumption. Ultimately, an interesting adjustment for RoT, would be the integration
with the DGNB guidelines, goals and grading system.
5.1.2
Guidelines for speciﬁc structural frames
Literature review regarding the structural frames that were considered (concrete, steel and timber) demon-
strated that they have quite diﬀerent DfD potentials. Back in the 16th century in Switzerland, DfD on
timber structures was ﬁrst documented and was acknowledged as widely implemented way of building
construction [Crowther (1999b)]. This fact derives by the simple principles that are easily implemented
and mainly focus on the use of bolts and screws instead of nails and glue. However designing a mod-
ern building could be challenging at it should also comply with the strict legislative demands and the
high aesthetic demands. In case of steel, DfD is also easily implemented as both welded and bolted
connections allow reuse but bolted connections shall be preferred as they enable the quick and low-cost
disassembly. In both steel and timber frames several simple innovations that are demonstrated in sub
chapter 5.1.2. in the design of the connections optimize the easy assembly / disassembly processes and
along with the generic guidelines regarding the geometries of the components etc boost the DfD potential.
The case of concrete though is the biggest challenge and implementation of DfD on concrete buildings
would have huge environmental and ﬁnancial beneﬁts as concrete is the largest waste stream of the build-
ing sector and 90% is down-cycled in Denmark. The problem derives by the lack of available mechanical
connections and technological innovations have to take place. According to Fletcher (2001b), precast
concrete has higher reuse potential as it has standard sizes and reinforcement while members could be
joined with mechanical fasteners. The most interesting existing case was identiﬁed to be the Peikko
systems, with the "Tenloc Panel Connector", the "Delta beams" and the system for stabilizing columns
with the use of bolts. However pouring concrete into the gaps of the connections is necessary for practical
and legislative issues and this problem has to be handled.
A signiﬁcant challenge that applies in all structural frames, is the documentation of the strength of their
structural components to enable reuse in a diﬀerent conﬁguration. This challenge is quite signiﬁcant
specially in case of concrete as there is a need of identifying and testing the reinforcement of an existing
component.
Potential improvements regarding the adaptation of the guidelines in the case of the structural frames and
in general
What could be improved regarding the guidelines for the speciﬁc structural frames, would be the adapta-
tion of all the generic guidelines to the characteristics of each material, with adapted weights, while also
choosing the most desired EoL scenario for each material as they could diﬀer. Furthermore, regarding
DfD of structural frames from speciﬁc materials, literature review can further expand and include more
case studies and a wide range of speciﬁc real-life examples for the implementation of each guideline in
order to make their implementation easier. The guidelines could also expand to more structural frames
and combinations of them such as steel-concrete [Addis and Schouten (2004)], steel-glass etc. Ultimately,
an interesting algorithm that could derive would be one that based on the choices of the user for one
building layer, to further suggest suitable additions for the other layers of the building. For example, this
system would ideally start with the choice of structural frame, and based on the choice of the user, the
algorithm would present possible choices for facades.
5.1.3
Guidelines for "Services" layer
The adaptation of RoT in the case of "Services" was decided based on the results of the workshop, as it
was considered a layer with high potentials on DfD itself, but also because placing the installations within
a building could be further optimized and assist so the independence of the layers. Furthermore due to
the totally diﬀerent nature, purpose and role within the building, installations are an interesting ﬁeld to
be examined. However only limited relevant literature was found regarding this purpose and concluded
on a range of speciﬁc guidelines. Furthermore, due to the fact that "services" demand a wide ﬁeld of
knowledge that the author is only "adjacent" to, they guidelines are just introduced and aren’t widely
examined or discussed.
74
==PAGE:77==
5.1.4
Assessment tools
All the assessment tools have quite a diﬀerent set up, content and aims. Considering that the tool of
Thormark (2001) and Sassi (2002) are quite limited, while the "Knowledge model" of Durmisevic (2006)
focuses on the assessment of the "Transformation Capacity". The "Multi-purpose system" of Nordby
(2009b), although mainly refers to the salvageability of materials, is a quite interesting model that tries to
merge 3 purposes under one system of guidelines: To be a clear, pedagogic system able to communicate
the principles, to be convertible an assessment tool for choosing building components and ultimately to
be used as a checklist for designing salvageable components and materials. Consequently this model could
be a basis for an improved assessment tool. Further improvements that could be considered in a new
assessment tool are the following:
• Set minimum requirements. Most of the guidelines must always be fulﬁlled at a minimum threshold
in order to enable DfD as for example reversibility of component connections.
• Introduce speciﬁc weighting on each criterion for more robust comparisons and for easier use of the
tool from a wider audience. Not all the guidelines must have the same weighting as for example
reversibility of connections shouldn’t have the same weighting with accessibility to components for
cleaning.
• Adjust weighting and add speciﬁc criteria for diﬀerent layers and materials. Each material has some
special characteristics.
• Include ﬁnancial criteria as they are necessary and would be quite useful for the comparison.
• Provide speciﬁc cases with what the best and worst grades represent.
• Diﬀerent weighting and grading for each EoL scenario would add complexity but could also add
accuracy more thorough understanding of the performance of a design.
• Use the EoL scenarios of Crowther (2001) as they consider the reuse in all levels (material-
component-building) which lacks from the existing tools
• Include guidelines for increasing the transforming capacity and adaptability of the building.
• Implement the multipurpose system idea of Nordby (2009a) where the tool is communicating also
the principles of DfD and also serve as a checklist for the design of new buildings
5.2
Challenges for DfD and potential solutions
Mapping the challenges regarding implementation and spreading of DfD was a key ﬁnding for this thesis
as it included an interview round with high proﬁle representatives from the danish building industry,
such as as the "Secretariat" of Bygherreforeningen, Henrik Bang, and the director of "Dansk Byggeri",
Michael H. Nielsen. In addition the main challenges were further analyzed in a workshop with more
than 30 professionals from the building industry. No such wide mapping of the DfD challenges from that
many groups of stakeholders was found in the literature, neither in Denmark nor in general. Considering
that the ﬁndings are based on the danish conditions raises their importance for the ﬁeld in Denmark.
In contrast, mapping of challenges in the literature, is limited a purely technical perspective. The most
analytic review was presented by Rios et al. (2015)
The importance of the derived groups of challenges varies widely according to their solution potential
and the importance of the obstacles they pose. From a wider perspective, the 4 out of 6 groups of chal-
lenges are highly related with the "technical feasibility and proper application of DfD". These groups
are: Detailing, Reuse of components and materials, Lifespans of materials and Manufacturing of products.
Detailing has signiﬁcant limitations that derive by the simultaneous fulﬁllment of DFD guidelines along
with the legislative criteria and aesthetic demands, while the lack of application examples raises the bar.
However, considering the fact that DfD promotes simple solutions that use existing tools, materials and
techniques, there are some speciﬁc examples that could be easily implemented in a large scale, such as in
the case of timber and steel structures. Even, in the case of concrete, new solutions appear in the horizon
as the example of the Peikko system and the "De Fire Styrelser" project. Thus it can be considered
that it is a matter of investments in research to close the knowledge gap, to identify and the necessary
75
==PAGE:78==
technical solutions, to create analytical guidelines and grant all the necessary assistance to the architects
and engineers and to promote innovations on the ﬁeld.
Similarly, investments on research along with political initiatives could tackle the challenges that are
related to the "Lifespans of Materials" group. Speciﬁcally there is a need for a thoroughly documented
database for lifespans, adapted in danish climate data, and a framework for estimating the lifespans of
the materials in an unbiased way when they aren’t available. Such examples of lifespan regionalization,
already exist in Germany according to Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017). Furthermore investment on re-
search could easily solve the documentation problems, and this could also easily happen through BIM.
3D BIM models are widely used from architects and engineers, while in several cases facility managers
use and update these models through the whole lifespan of the building. In addition, these models can
also store data regarding the characteristics of all the building elements already. However considering
that these data may be used a century later, the biggest challenge would be to identify the optimal way
of documenting them and to consider if BIM models would be appropriate.
Ultimately regarding "Manufacturing of products", investments on research and development shall also
take place in order to create new products or to adapt the existing ones that are appropriate for DfD
and for the optimization of their manufacturing processes. In addition, considering the major technology
breakthroughs in the past, the inability of the manufacturers to adapt to the changes in technologies and
demands, wasn’t an obstacle for the widespread of the technology in a mid-term and long-term perspec-
tive, as new players would ﬁnally emerge in the market to satisfy these needs.
Consequently most of the challenges that are related to the "technical feasibility and proper application
of DfD", could be handled with investments on research and development, and through pilot projects
that would provide experience and real life examples. Lack of knowledge from the professionals could
also be handled as DfD is proposing a diﬀerent way of using existing techniques and materials and as a
result this burden could be shifted easily.
Challenges regarding prefabrication focus mainly on the prejudice of clients and developers towards pre-
fabricated solutions as it is linked with low quality solutions and also the potential DfD solutions are
untested. Specially in case of Denmark, the problems that derived with the case of prefab toilets, where
the producer went out of business and the clients were left without support, make this problem even
bigger. Furthermore when it comes to testing a housing solution, the test period is several decades. In
addition, traditionally, prefab solutions pose some aesthetic limitations that the user must accept and
this is a challenge that architectural detailing has to deal with. However during the recent years, there
is a change taking place. For example the "row-houses got very popular here in Copenhagen and every-
where and this is where Scandi Byg really got into the market and people buy these houses for a lot of
money" [Lewis (2017a)]. Ultimately, the already wide use of prefab elements could makes Danes more
open towards such solutions. Concluding the main challenges regarding prefabrication can be considered
rather as "cultural" and during the last years there is a tendency of a positive change.
The last group of challenges is the "market potential" of DfD, or the "Business case". The major prob-
lem is the huge "lifespan of the initial investment", or the time that is needed to earn proﬁts from the
investment, that in case of Denmark it could be 120 years, at least for the structural parts of the build-
ing. Neither clients or developers have such a long investment lifespan, while even in this cases it is
very diﬃcult for them to trust the company that sells the solutions that they will exist all these years
to guarantee the beneﬁts. In addition, the fact that this a new and untested business case and the lack
of big companies to guarantee the beneﬁts and the assistance during the lifespan of the building, raises
signiﬁcant issues. Along with the facts that certiﬁcation schemes and legislation don’t provide currently
any strong drivers lead customers that want to work with sustainability to choose one more common way
among the huge range of choices. Considering that similar to all the ﬁelds of the market and maybe more
intensively on the construction ﬁeld, most of the changes are market driven, the tough problems of the
business case are the main challenges for DfD.
The most apparent boost for DfD, would be its promotion from the DGNB certiﬁcate and from the legis-
lation. However, according to Bang (2017), including such a strategy in the legislation can’t be expected
and deﬁnitely in the short term as a thorough analysis of the possible implications have to take place
while it also needs strong political motives that not all the governments have and of course a more robust
76
==PAGE:79==
basis. In case of DGNB, further development of real life examples and more thorough guidelines, could
assist in raising its importance within the certiﬁcate. Consequently the results of this project could also
assist in this direction.
With regard to the problems that derive from the long time frame of the investment, DfD could focus in the
ﬁrst place in the layers of the building that have the shorter lifespans, as are the "Services (Installations)"
and the "Skin (facades)". Such an example is the headquarters of MT Højgaard, where the facade can be
easily replaced so it will never become obsolete. This could be also blurry environmentally, but considering
that in the future there will be probably developed more sustainable materials and the existing facade
could be used in another building it could be proved to be beneﬁcial. Another strategy for tackling the
"lifespan of the investment" challenge according to the workshop would be the leasing of the materials
similarly to previous cases (e.g. Ege Tæpper), where the user of a house won’t own but just leasing
the materials. This solution could remove the uncertainties of the customers, but the viability of such a
business model has to be thoroughly examined and this is aimed also to take place during the InnoBYG
project.
Another way of removing the uncertainties would be the guarantee of the investment by a
strong player of the market [Bang (2017)]. Furthermore the examination of the "life cycle cost" of the
investment could also be critical as there could be signiﬁcant ﬁnancial savings also during the lifespan of
the building and this is an initiative that could also take place during the project. A signiﬁcant parameter
that should also be considered and that could be of high interest for the clients is the ﬂexibility and the
ability to relocate their building when they design their building with this aim. Considering that the
main reason that buildings are demolished is their inability to adapt to diﬀerent functions, ﬂexibility
could guarantee the avoidance of such problems. Similarly the ability to relocate a building can also be
critical for individual clients and businesses.
5.3
Case house
Since the case house is designed within the framework of the wider InnoBYG project, it’s compliance
with the interests of the attendants of the workshop is hereby discussed. Speciﬁcally, the fulﬁllment of
the relevant proposals that derived by the workshop, and the compliance of the design process and the
case house itself analyzed.
Firstly, the case house fully complies with the DfD principles according to Table 17. In addition, consid-
ering the fact that its design is discussed and analyzed with project partners and other professionals, it
can serve as a veriﬁed solution and an example for further application, being so an ideal pilot project.
Furthermore, the case house investigates innovative standardized and prefabricated solutions that fulﬁll
the DfD perspective, while adding ﬂexibility to the overall system. In addition, this LCA also is an input
for the "proof of concept", while an LCA could also be performed for a more advanced design of the
house at a later stage or ideally sooner in order to test potential alternative solutions.
Other goals of the workshop that could be fulﬁlled by this project would need a 1:1 mock up, to serve as
a demonstrator for the testing of relevant ideas in real life and to measure their eﬃciency and the eﬀect
in waste streams in comparison to traditional methods. In addition this project could theoretically serve
as a demonstrator and a basis for establishing the development of the relevant legislation. Moreover, the
related costs of this project could also be measured and serve as an input for an extended analysis of the
business case of DfD. Ultimately the further design, construction and maintenance of the house could
take place in an experimental wide-sector collaboration.
77
==PAGE:80==
5.4
LCA
This part of the discussion focuses on validating the LCA model though the comparison with other
buildings, performing a sensitivity analysis for the eﬃciency of the municipal waste incineration facilities,
analyzing the assumptions, and having a brief qualitative analysis of the uncertainty. The comparison of
the case house with other buildings also demonstrates the performance of the house in a wider frame, as
the chosen buildings are serving as benchmarks for assessing the environmental performance of the house
and the magnitude of the impacts similar to Brejnrod (2015).
5.4.1
Magnitude of results. Comparison with two reference buildings
The two reference buildings are the "Reference house", which represents the typical danish single-family
house and represents the "business-as-usual" scenario, and the "Upcycle house", which is considered a
top performing, in environmental terms, single-family house made of upcycled materials and is set as the
upper limit for the comparison. Both houses are presented in Chapters 3.3.13.1 and 3.3.13.2. It should
be highlighted that the Reference house was used as the basis for the inventory and the design of the case
house in order to enable and assist the comparison. Speciﬁcally, both the reference and the case house
have the same gross area, while the inventory for any parts of the building that weren’t yet designed,
such as the installations and the roof, the inventory of the reference house was mainly used as a basis.
Ultimately when SBi Tables didn’t provide any data for lifespans the lifespans that were considered by
Rasmussen (2015) (SBi 2015:09) were used, since this report is also from SBi.
Regarding the comparison, the results of the Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir (2013), were used for the Upcycle
house and unfortunately only its carbon footprint was available. For the reference house, although its
carbon footprint is calculated in the same report, another report of Rasmussen (2015) was chosen, as it
considered an updated version of the reference house so that it is compatible with the energy consumption
legislation of 2015. The latter report also includes a more analytic presentation of the LCA results and
mentions the results for the basic impact categories, excluding the toxicity related ones. Unfortunately,
the LCAs for both houses derived with the use of DGNB database.
For the comparison of carbon
footprints the several levels of reuse for the two basic scenarios of the case house, Scenario 1 (substitution
of natural gas) and Scenario 2 (substitution of biomass), were considered. Impacts from the Upcycle house
were linearly adjusted to the area of the case house. In Figure L, below, the comparison is presented.

Firstly, none of the scenarios exceeds the two benchmarks. As expected, the Scenario 1, where the energy
from incineration of wood materials is substituting energy production from natural gas, is quite closer
to the performance of the Upcycle house, while impacts from Scenario 2 are closer to the ones of the
reference house. The paradox of having higher GWP impacts due to reuse in Scenario 1, derives by
the allocation of the dominating beneﬁcial impacts from the substitution of natural gas consumption, to
more than one product systems. This fact is also explained in Chapter 4.6.5 and shown numerically in
Appendix I.1. The impacts of the diﬀerent scenarios of the case house comparing to the reference house,
are signiﬁcantly reduced in a range that varies from 24% to 66%. Correspondingly, comparing to the
Upcycle house, the increase of GWP impacts varies from 76% to 290%. The average value of the case
house scenarios is 1.66 kg CO2/m2/yr and it is 46% smaller than the one of the reference house and
176% higher than the one of the Upcycle house. Next, Figure 45 derives by the comparison of the case
house with the rest of the impacts from the reference house. More analytical results are demonstrated in

Figure 45 demonstrates that the case house performs also better in the POCP category, as both basic
scenarios have approximately the half impacts, while both scenarios out-perform the reference house in
the ODP category. On the other hand, the case house has 4,5 to 5 times higher impacts in the Eutroph-
ication potential impact category. According to Chapter 4.6.3, Eutrophication impact category is one
of the few categories that the wood products have higher portion of impacts. This fact explains that it
is the wood products that lead to this diﬀerence. Considering the normalized results in Figure 35, the
impacts of the Eutrophication impact category are of similar magnitude to the GWP and AP impact
categories.
Toxicity related impact categories were not examined in the comparison as they weren’t included in the
SBi report. However the toxicological impacts may not follow the same trends with the other impact
categories and they should ideally be included in LCAs of buildings [Rasmussen et al. (2013)]. In addition,
as already mentioned in Chapter 3.3.6.2, there are signiﬁcant impacts from the maintenance of wood
products in the toxicity categories [Sathre and González-García (2014)] and this fact makes the inclusion
of these categories in LCAs of wooden building products crucial. However, maintenance is also out of the
boundaries of the DGNB system (Figure 12), but results from maintenance in the lifespans of products
are considered anyway for the SBi tables. Summing up maintenance and toxicity impacts should ideally
be included for the comparison of wood houses to typical concrete houses. This fact is also highlighted for
79
==PAGE:82==
this case study by the importance of the toxicity related categories according to the normalized results,
even though the impacts from the production of the half material bill, CLT and wood ﬁber insulation,
aren’t included (Chapter 4.6.2).
5.4.2
Sensitivity analysis
Sensitivity analysis aims in general to identify uncertainties through the analysis of alternative scenarios.
Parameter sensitivity analysis (perturbation analysis) detects the parameters that most aﬀect the results.
This process takes place through a 10% change of the value of the parameter, while measuring the
corresponding change in the results. Due to time limitation and the great number of parameters, this
wasn’t possible to take place for all the parameters of this LCA, and only one scenario was examined. The
choice of this scenario took place by considering the uncertainty of the value of the diﬀerent parameters
and their potential impacts. As already discussed in Chapter 3.3.6.4, the choice of future energy grids and
the eﬃciency of incineration in the municipal waste management facilities, is widely acknowledged in the
literature for its importance, while the corresponding choices aren’t based on concrete data. Considering
that the choice of eﬃciency aﬀects directly the amount of avoided energy consumption that is substituted,
makes its examination necessary. Sensitivity analysis took place through OpenLCA’s project tab and the
"Compared product systems" function. The 10% increase of eﬃciency aﬀected uniformly eﬃciencies of
both electricity and heat production. Results are presented in the following table (Table 22).
Table 22: Reduction of impacts for the two basic scenarios due to a 10% raise in the eﬃciency of the
wood waste incineration in the waste management facilities

According to the Table 22, ODP is the most aﬀected category but as already mentioned several times,
it’s negligible total value makes its ﬂuctuations rather high, while according to normalization (Chapter
4.6.2), its importance is lower than the other impact categories. Regarding the rest of the categories, for
Scenario 2, the higher rates of biomass substitution don’t have any signiﬁcant change in the total impacts
and this fact shows how low the impacts from biomass incineration are. On the other hand, for scenario
1, total GWP and ADPf impacts are 13% and 17% lower respectively, and this fact reveals that this
parameter is highly sensitive for Scenario 1. Considering that the eﬃciency of the municipality’s inciner-
ation facilities could be signiﬁcantly higher (Chapter 3.3.6.4), for a more thorough understanding of the
deriving uncertainty, the potential improvement in the eﬃciency of natural gas utilization facilities should
also be considered and compared. Ultimately, comparing the inﬂuence of the sensitivity analysis for the
two basic scenarios, the most critical choice for such a system isn’t the eﬃciency of the energy production
facilities but the choice of marginal energy source, because in the case of more environmental friendly
energy sources, the impacts from the energy production could be minor or negligible. Consequently, in
this case, the eﬃciency of the wood incineration facility wouldn’t be such a critical choice. However when
fossil fuel consumption is substituted, the avoided impacts are noteworthy, as it is apparent from the
comparison of the two scenarios in Figure L, and in that case the forecasted eﬃciencies of the energy
production facilities can be a highly sensitive parameter, similar to this LCA.
5.4.3
Assumptions and uncertainty
As described in Chapter 4.5, the case house was designed in parallel with this thesis and even by its end
it was still in the design phase. This fact led to signiﬁcant assumptions regarding the material choice and
the corresponding quantities over several building parts. This fact is revealed by the low data quality
80
==PAGE:83==
(Table 8 and Appendix E) and increases signiﬁcantly the uncertainty. However most of the assumptions
were made in a standard and conservative way as the updated inventory of the "Reference house" (Ras-
mussen (2015)), a typical danish single family house, was used.
Other signiﬁcant assumptions took place due to lack of representative datasets in Ecoinvent. For example
all the wood products were dealt in the EoL stage with the only EoL process for waste building wood
(waste building wood, chrome preserved, municipal incineration) and the mismatch in carbon content
led to manual calculations (Appendix F). Although this manipulation of biogenic carbon is in line with
the literature, it is also under wide discussion (Chapter 3.3.8). Furthermore the manual manipulation
of data increases the possibility of human errors. Manual manipulations of data also took place for the
addition of the results from EPDs for wood ﬁbre insulation and CLT. Considering also the comparison
with the two reference buildings, uncertainty is raised also due to the use of diﬀerent databases.
Another signiﬁcant issue that derived by the use of EPDs is the fact that there weren’t included any
toxicity related results. Considering that these two products represent almost the half of the total ma-
terial mass, not including their production in the assessment, rockets the uncertainty over these results.
However, as already discussed in the previous sub-chapter (5.4.2), toxicity impacts are quite important
for wooden products, while for the proper calculation of their toxicity impacts, their maintenance should
be included as well, even though it is out of the DGNB boundaries (Figure 12). Hereby, it should also be
mentioned though, that marine aquatic toxicity is the most important impact category according to the
normalized results (Chapter 4.6.2), and consequently there is high uncertainty regarding the most crucial
theoretically impact category.
Furthermore, as derived by the sensitivity analysis in the previous sub-chapter and the initial analysis in
Chapter 3.3.6.4, forecasting the energy grid for 120 years later is a gigantic source of uncertainty. When
energy from fossil fuel is substituted, the eﬃciencies of the energy production facilities is also critical
according to the sensitivity analysis (Chapter 5.4.2). In addition, considering that the impacts of the
replacements (B stage) and the impacts of the initial building production (A stage) are quite similar, as
derived by Chapter 4.6.4, the future energy grid and production technologies, could inﬂuence signiﬁcantly
the impacts of the production of replacements and thus the total score for several categories. Similarly
uncertainties regarding the actual lifespans of the materials also inﬂuence the results. Regarding the
waste treatment processes, although they didn’t aﬀect directly the total LCA scores, the beneﬁts from
the "next product system" stage were a signiﬁcant fraction of the results (Figure 39) and led to quite dif-
ferent carbon footprints (Figures 40 and L). Consequently the forecasting of future EoL processes, waste
streams and waste wood treatment and reuse (Figure L) also inﬂuences signiﬁcantly the uncertainty of
the results. The analytic list of assumptions is demonstrated in Appendix M.
Ultimately, regarding the diﬀerent reuse scenarios (Chapter 3.3.7) and the corresponding changes in
impact scores (Chapter 4.6.5), the lifespans of the materials are purely assumed as there are no relevant
data, especially for CLT and wood ﬁber insulation, and the used lifespans are in contrast with SBi tables.
In addition, considering that two full reuse cycles (reuse scenario 2), correspond to a 360-year lifespan,
the EoL streams and energy grid forecasts should be adjusted to such a lifespan as well. Of course the
uncertainty over any scenarios for such a high lifespan will always be a major issue and will make the
quantiﬁcation of the beneﬁts from DfD problematic in case of buildings.
81
==PAGE:84==
6
Conclusion & Further research
6.1
Rules of Thumb
The ﬁrst deliverable of this thesis is the "Rules of Thumb (RoT)" (Tables 11 to 15). RoT are 32 key
principles for implementing Design for Disassembly and refer to all of the building layers except the
"Staﬀ(furniture)" according to the classiﬁcation of the building layers from Brand (1994). RoT aim to
"close the loop" in the building industry through the proper implementation of DfD, and in accordance
with the recycling hierarchy. It is aimed that RoT will be used as a tool for the easy communication
of the DfD principles to professionals and as a brief guide for implementing DfD. Furthermore, a range
of adaptations of the rules took place, regarding speciﬁc structural frames and for the building layer of
"Services (installations)".
RoT could be further built-up by including examples of "basic - mediocre - optimal" implementations
for each guideline. Furthermore, hierarchizing the guidelines, adding weighting and minimum levels of
application for each guideline could also be useful inputs. Considering the main challenges of DfD, as
they derived from this project, RoT could be also adjusted in a wider perspective in order to consider
compliance with other designing methods, e.g. optimal manufacturability, the legislation and the DGNB
criteria. Ultimately, the RoT could be further adapted for diﬀerent building layers and materials, similarly
with the adaptations that took place for this thesis. Through this prism, an algorithm that presents
suitable add-ons to a building design according to the choices of the user for each building layer, could
be derived.
6.2
Main challenges
The second deliverable of this thesis, was an extensive mapping of the DfD challenges, as they derived from
a round of interviews and the workshop on "DESIGN FOR DISASSEMEBLY i præfabrikeret byggeri".
The concluded main challenges are demonstrated in Table 32, while a more analytic presentation of
challenges takes place in Appendix C. The main challenges are summed up under the following six main
groups.
• Business case
• Prefabrication
• Lifespans of materials
• Detailing
• Reuse of components and materials
• Manufacturing of products
Among these groups of challenges, the most insurmountable are linked to the business case of DfD. Con-
sidering that the time frame for the reselling of the structural parts could be more than 120 years in case
of Denmark, clients and contractors aren’t willing to make an investment with such a time frame. In ad-
dition, currently there is no existing business model, and beneﬁts aren’t guaranteed. Furthermore, there
aren’t currently signiﬁcant motives for implementing DfD, since it isn’t mandatory from the legislation
and it is poorly credited by the DGNB certiﬁcation scheme. Considering also that there are several ways
of working with sustainability for a client of the building industry, DfD with all the uncertainties and the
weaknesses regarding its business case, isn’t one of the favourable choices.
However DfD could focus ﬁrstly on building layers with shorter lifespans, such as the installations and
the facades, similar to the facades of the headquarters of MT Højgaard. In addition, the challenge of
the long time frame of the investment and the relevant uncertainties could be tackled if the materials
were leased instead of sold. As a result the risk of the investment would be transferred to the provider,
strengthening so signiﬁcantly the business case. Ultimately the business case of DfD could focus on the
ﬂexibility it oﬀers to the user. Considering that this is the main reason buildings are demolished and
that the relocation potential of a building could protect a real estate investment against potential drops
in prices.
82
==PAGE:85==
6.3
Case house and environmental performance
The case house was designed within the framework of the InnoBYG project and complies with most of
the Rules of Thumb (Table 17) and thus with the key principles of DfD. A range of rules that aren’t yet
fulﬁlled is the documentation of the components’ properties and the development of the deconstruction
plan, but they could be implemented in the next stages. Considering the proposals of the attendants in
the workshop, the case house facilitates several of the proposals, but a 1:1 mock up would be needed for
further compliance with them. This case study could serve as a demonstrator of DfD and an input for
establishing the development of the relevant legislation.
For assessing the environmental performance of the case house, a comparison was performed with the
Reference House and the Upcycle House and their impacts served as benchmarks. The ﬁrst house rep-
resents the typical Danish single-family house and the second represents the state-of-the-art building in
terms of environmental impacts from materials. For the LCA of this report, two basic scenarios were
considered. The two scenarios diﬀer in the energy that is substituted from the incineration of the wooden
and the wood-based products and don’t consider reuse. Scenario 1 considers that the marginal energy
and power source in 120 years will be natural gas and Scenario 2 considers biomass (wood chips).
The results from the comparison with the reference buildings, show that the carbon footprint per gross
area and year for Scenario 1 is 1.06 kg CO2/m2/yr, for Scenario 2 is 2.35 kg CO2/m2/yr, for the
reference house is 3.1 kg CO2/m2/yr while for the Upcycle house is 0.48 kg CO2/m2/yr (or 0.60
kg CO2/m2/yr if the impacts are linearly adjusted to the gross area of the other houses). Regarding
the rest impact categories, the case house performs also better than the reference house in Photochemical
oxidation category, but has 4.5 to 5 times more impacts in the Eutrophication category. The eﬀect of
reuse to these results was minor. Due to lack of data, toxicity impact categories weren’t examined, but
their results could be critical for the overall assessment as they may not follow the same trends with the
other impact categories [Rasmussen et al. (2013)], while their importance is highlighted by the normalized
results as well. Furthermore, it was estimated that the case house stores permanently, during its’ lifespan,
approximately 72.6 tn of CO2. Although the signiﬁcance and advantages or disadvantages from tempo-
rary carbon sequestration is under discussion, if the house is replaced by another similar house or if wooden
components are reused, the carbon remains stored. In a wider frame, if the wood based houses became
the typical construction method, the cities in the future could perform as permanent carbon pools and
the signiﬁcant environmental beneﬁts from the permanent carbon storage are unanimously acknowledged.
For this LCA and any involved calculations, it should be highlighted that the results are characterized by
high uncertainty due to the low data quality, the high number and importance of assumptions, and the
sensitivity of the ﬁrst Scenario to the eﬃciency of the municipal incineration facilities. Except from the
toxicity related categories, future LCAs of the case house and buildings made of wood, in general shall
ideally also include the maintenance, despite the fact that they are out of the boundaries of DGNB, since
toxicity impacts from wood maintenance could severely inﬂuence the toxicity related categories. Other
proposals for future environmental assessment of the house would be, to include dynamic electricity and
heat consumption forecasts, to assess more scenarios for "marginal energy" sources and to adapt the
impacts from replacements to future scenarios as well due to their high share of impacts in this LCA. In
addition, considering the sensitivity analysis, more scenarios for eﬃciency of the waste treatment facilities
in the future shall be considered.
Ultimately, regarding the assessment of the environmental performance of the case house and DfD in
general, it should be highlighted that there are several aspects and some signiﬁcant beneﬁts that are
hardly quantiﬁable within the current lca practices and the DGNB boundaries. For example, the high
ﬂexibility that the case house oﬀers contributes to the reduction of renovations, and to the full exploitation
of the lifespan of a building and thus of the included materials as well. In addition the high adaptability
of the house to the user needs and even the relocation potential could lead to full occupation during
its lifespan and thus indirectly to mitigate needs for new buildings, as today a signiﬁcant fraction of
buildings remains permanently empty. Finally, a signiﬁcant obstacle for the concrete quantiﬁcation of
DfD is that the lifespans of the elements could reach even some centuries and as a result the uncertainty
of the scenarios is skyrocketing.
83
