B.5
Interview with Duncan Horswill
Interview 18.01 2017 / at Søren Jensen, Duncan Horswill, Kostas Koukoulopoulos & Line
Kjær Frederiksen
DH: I have been involved in another InnoBYG Spireprojekt with T.I, which was started last year. I think
it is kind of continuing into the year as well. But it is multi storey timber buildings. And actually I have
participated in a conference last year called Go2Wood, which was with Michael Green and. . .
LKF: Chroa Connection?
DH: Yeah, Chora Connection..
LKF: It was at KADK, right? Because I remember Anne helped a lot with arranging it.
DH: Yeah and I think she did part of the introduction and was involved in some of the discussion sessions
afterward. She’s been a big part of that. I mean that’s a lot of our focu i guess, over the last 2 or 3 years
has been. . .
LKF: On timber and CLT as well?
DH: Yeah exactly, and how we can use this new technology of engineered timber to challenge the existing
dominant industry which is precast concrete elements. Especially when it comes to multi storey resi-
dential buildings. We think that there’s a very strong argument for a new direction toward sustainable
developments and using sustainable materials as opposed to concrete for loadbearing walls and ﬂoors and
things like that, you know. When again, when you consider we are already building 3 or 4 or 5 storey
buildings which aren’t that challenging when it comes to structural systems. So we have all these internal
loadbearing walls and ﬂoors, so concrete in many ways is overkill. For many of these structures they can
support another 5 stories easily, but because the wall has to be a certain size, because that’s what they
make it, yo know, then that’s how people have just traditionally done it. And it’s just gotten stronger
and stronger until the point where no one questions it anymore, and it becomes this dominant industry
that we have right now. So we have put a lot of eﬀort into trying to present an alternative and say - we
don’t actually have to use concrete, we can use timber and actually they are doing this in many parts
of Scandinavia, Europe, Austria, Germany, Canada, Australia, the UK - London. So you know in many
ways a lot of the countries all around Denmark are already doing it, just not Denmark. So, we see that
as an opportunity for us as structural engineers to say, well actually we can solve the technical part of
it so actually we can go further and start to quantify the cost and the risk, to maybe then present it
to contractors, clients, architects, developers, and say actually there is an alternative. But as a part of
that we also talk about DfD because, CLT elements are put together using bolts and screws it is not
stuck together with in situ concrete, like with the Delta Beam system and many many precast concrete
systems. You have the individual concrete elements, which are separate and discreet, but then in order
for them to work as a whole system you need to cast them together with in situ concrete and the it is
almost impossible to take it apart after that. So when you see even a precast concrete building being
demolished, it’s very noisy and there’s lots of vibration, lots of dust. It’s essentially one of these machines
that just crunch and breaks up the concrete frame into small pieces and then they crush it even further
and then they put it on the roads and under buildings, that kind of thing. So yeah, it’s recycle but it is
not great.
LKF: I know that some companies/ architectural studios are looking very much into this, Finnish systems,
Peikko systems of precast concrete structures. But it seems to me like all they need is to ﬁll it out with
something else than concrete in the joints. But i don’t know what the status is in those projects.
DH: No. I had a chat with the guys from Delta Beam - Peikko - and yeah their system is really good; it
goes together very simply, it’s very easy to construct with. But as soon as you pour the concrete in all the
spaces between the beams and between the elements, it’s almost impossible to take that apart without
destroying part of the beam or the column or the precast element as well. You can’t really reassemble
with that system. Because there’s a large risk you loose the integrity of the elements. But yeah, when I
talked to them, they were like: “yeah we know about this whole thing of DfD - we’re working on it.“ But
I’m not sure they’ve made a whole lot of progress.
LKF: Who was that again?
DH: Delta Beam. I can’t remember the guy’s name. I think it was at Building Green last year. I can’t
remember. Not too long ago. So maybe they’ve made some progress since then.
LKF: I think this project will mainly focus on DfD in relation to Prefab. We’ll like to work with Scandi
Byg, a company in the North of Jutland, because we see a great potential in that industry and the way
they work with the materials now is a lot of glue, a lot of nails - joints that can’t be disassembled. If
that industry could take this DfD strategy, then it could make a very big impact. But that’s kind of the
overall focus on this project. So the questions we prepared today is mainly about design for disassembly
but also in relation to prefab structures. I think I’ll just read them out loud - then we get the chance to
105
==PAGE:108==
quite easily compare the answers from the diﬀerent people we interview. So i think i’ll just start. Where
do you see the biggest challenge for working with DfD?
DH: I think it is diﬃcult to identify the biggest challenge, because I think there’s a number of challenges.
I think maybe the biggest challenge is to identify the market for disassembled structural components to
be reused. Fx. i went to meet a developer this week. And it’s a collaboration between Søren Jensen
and COBE arch’s. and a contractor called BNS (BLS). And we’re pitching to “elf-developments” (?) -
the idea that multi storey residential building using CLT instead of precast concrete. They do a lot of
residential buildings in CPH and around CPH, so we thought there’s an opportunity for us to present to
them an alternative. All they now at the moment is precast concrete. They haven’t had they eyes open
to the possibility of anything else. So we thought that was an opportunity for us. But again, they are so
driven by risk and cost and all that relates to time. So if things like sustainability is very low on their
list of priorities. I think we managed to make some progress with them to try and again open their eyes
to the fact that we have a responsibility as people that built buildings to make sure they are sustainable
as well - and it’s not just about money. Their view on that is well, as long as we can use sustainabilty as
a marketing tool to sell buildings at a higher price, then that works for us. So again, they are very much
motivated by cost. So leading back to the question; one of the biggest challenges is creating a market for
disassembled structural components, because essentially as an engineer or an architect, we would have
to convince the stakeholders who hold the money, that this is something that they should be interested
in. And their ﬁrst question will be: “Is it cheaper? Is it lower risk than what I do right now?“ And I
think those are two big questions we would need to answer. The risk element is quite interesting, because
like we’ve just been talking about; when you try and take apart a structure, there’s a risk that things
get damaged, if they are not designed for disassembly. You just try and unassemble a structure, you
have to undo bolts, you have to cut things, you maybe have to break something out using mechanical
tools and the risk is that something gets damaged. And then there’s also the big question of who takes
responsibility? You know, is it the contractor or is it the client? And also how is the manufacturer
involved in this? They sell a brand new product from their factory to a client, and maybe they provide
some kind of guarantee or warranty with that product, to say that;”when it leaves our workshop we
guarantee it will last for another ten years without maintenance.” what happen when you take a building
component from a building that was maybe 25-30 years old an try and reuse it in a new building - what
are the legal issues with that? Again that’s something a client. . .
if you had to put yourself in the
position of a client who is investing a lot of money in building a new building. And maybe there’s also
stakeholders that they have to manage. Developers, they don’t necessarily have their own money. They
take money from banks, from pension funds, from other investors and put it all together and then make
a contract with a landowner or another investor and then start to work together with a design team; an
architect a contractor, an engineer to ﬁgure out how to build this building. And they have to manage
lots and lots and lots of risks. Structure is just one small risk - a small part of their project. I think
as much as possible, that’s the biggest challenge, in my view. Like with CLT, there’s inherent problems
with it - challenges you can say. There’s some things it’s very good at and some things it’s not very
good at. You know, there’s the whole thing of ﬁre, there’s the whole thing of acoustics, there’s the whole
thing about the weather. Immediately people jump to the conclusion, that because we are building a
timber building, and if it rains then it’s gonna become moldy and the water is gonna soak into it, we’re
gonna have lots of problems with expanding and cracking. People see risk, risk, risk. And in many ways
as engineers and architects we can solve those problems for the client. But what we can’t solve is their
perception of risk. We can try to reduce it as much as possible, but a lot of clients just want to do the
traditional thing. And I think that we live - or we work in very risk averse industry. So very few people
want’s to be the ﬁrst to do anything new. It is really hard to ﬁnd someone who wants to do something
new, who is prepared to be the ﬁrst mover. If it works and people see a business case in it then you’ll
have people queuing up to do it. Everyone want to be the second one or the third or the fourth but no
one wants to be ﬁrst - in case it goes wrong. I think that’s the problem.
LKF: The next question has to do with materials. We talked a little about bit about your engagement
in working with CLT timber, but in relation to working with design for disassembly, how do you choose
materials? Maybe you can talk about it in broader terms.
DH: Yeah, exactly.When we start to think about design for disassembly we need to start to think about
the nature of structural systems. Fx. some systems relate inherently very well to DfD. So fx. steel
frames they are constructed generally using bolted connections. So again, in theory you should be able
to take those bolts apart and take the structural components apart.
So when you see a steel frame
being demolished it is not crushed and smashed. It’s quite carefully taken apart and then those elements
are recycled. There’s generally a problem with the theory of DfD. it is quite diﬃcult to then ﬁnd an
106
==PAGE:109==
opportunity to reuse those structural components in a new building unless the ﬂoorspan is exactly the
same, unless the loading is exactly the same, it is quite diﬃcult to ﬁnd an opportunity to reuse them. I
guess the best example of sustainability is refurbishments. So in fact you don’t take apart the building,
you just refurbish it for a new use. So as long as you can justify the existing structure for the new use then
there’s no reason to disassemble it. However, disassembly has huge potential, because then you can move
structures from one place to another. You can maybe put it back together in a new way. There’s always
compromises to be made with refurbishments; that you are stuck with certain physical constraints and
conditions that you just have to accept and of course we can make structural alterations, we can make
additions, we can change a building - quite signiﬁcantly somethimes, but we are still very much constraint
by what is already there. So disassembly could open up a new way for building systems to be designed.
So steel works very well. Timber works really well with DfD - again most of the connections are bolted.
There’s steel plates inserted into the timber frame and they are bolted to other timber elements. And
then precast concrete; in theory it should be possible to take it apart. I mean there might be situations
where you don’t have to cast together all of the structural components. So you might be able to save the
columns or something like that and reuse those. But generally ﬂoor elements, they are usually connected
to beams or other ﬂoor elements, in situ concrete or . . . so generally precast concrete ﬂoor elements are
covered in a concrete in situ topping, which combines them all together. And then I guess lastly there’s
in situ concrete frames, which you just can’t take apart and generally those are broken down, crushed,
and then the reinforcement separated and then the reinforcement is recycled, the concrete crushed and
put on the roads. So steel and timber has potential I think, out of all the main structural systems.
There’s bricks and masonry, but again it’s very time consuming to take down a masonry building and
clean the bricks and then reuse them. I know there are industries that do that and you can buy recycled
or reclaimed bricks and reuse them. So there’s possibilities for that.
LKF: Would your perspective on this change if you knew that the project or the building would be made
by prefabricated modules?
DH: Yes. I mean the ability to manufacture oﬀ-site oﬀers a lot more potential, again, for DfD. If you can
make an apartment essentially, a housing unit, a residential unit oﬀsite - that oﬀers a major advantages
because the building site should be cleaner, because you don’t have so many trades on the building site,
you can make a lot more in the factory so it should be higher quality, it is safer, because you have got
people working in a clean, warm, enclosed environment instead of having to work up in very tall heights
in all weathers, when it’s very cold, when it’s wet, when it’s dark sometimes. You should be able to do
a lot of manufacturing oﬀsite and bring that to site, just lift them and stack these components. You
can do it with pre-cast, you can do it with timber - it’s very common with timber - construction either
CLT or these kinds of cassettes; where you have a number of individual timber elements and then a
top and a bottom sheet maybe in plywood or something, you can build those in walls and ﬂoors and
make a prefabricated box. You can even put all the electrical system in and windows and doors. And in
theory you should be able to stack those up, connect them together with bolts and screws and then take
them apart again afterwards. So yes that does oﬀer advantages. Again you are kind of dealing with lego
blocks then, so a bit like shipping containers. The problem I guess is when you start to develop standard
systems, so you make things more and more customized with individual doors and window placings and
things like that, so I guess it becomes more customized for that ﬁrst initial design so it is quite diﬃcult
to then reconﬁgure it because there might be certain constraints you impose on that initial design, which
means that you can’t then turn the box the other way around, you can’t turn the box 90 degrees. So
maybe if we are moving into the era of DfD we should make our designs more robust in the ﬁrst place, so
there is more redundancy in the design, so it can be taken apart and made into a 6-storey tower instead
of a 3-storey low-rise or something like that.
LKF: Allright. The next is about detailing. We would like to ask what is important for you when you
are working with DfD regarding detailing.
DH: I mean detailing something like a steel frame for example we try to avoid as hard as possible the need
for permanent connections, for example welded connections. Welded connections are really important
sometimes to make a structural design work but they can be problematic. They can be quite expensive,
because you might need someone to make quite complicated welded connections, sometimes the geometry
can be quite complex. So as far as possible we try with bolted connections, they are more simple.
LKF: Is this diﬀerent if you know that the assignment is pre-fab based?
DH: Absolutely yes.
If we know that the aspiration or the ambition of the project is to be able to
disassemble it afterwards, then from the beginning we would consider only bolted connections and no
welded connections. Again we would start considering the size of the components so that they can easily
be lifted or if they have to be made into big components how to take these big components apart.So
107
==PAGE:110==
for example, sometimes we have to design buildings with large column free spaces so that it is super
ﬂexible but that might impose a demand of the structural system that it has to be big heavy beams and
trusses and things like that. So again instead of designing a beam which is 20 metres long, which could
potentially be transported to site if there is good access and for transportation by road or maybe by ship,
you know that component could be manufactured at the factory and then brought to the site and lifted
upon the position but it is not so easy when it comes to taking a building apart and trying to reuse a
20m beam. So as far as possible then we start to consider breaking large components down in to smaller
components and combining them together using bolted connections. Timber is essentially the same, so
trying to avoid very very big, heavy components and making things out of smaller.
LKF: Has DfD inﬂuence of the planning of a project? I mean both in the project and its conceptualization.
Is it a part of the early phase? How does it ﬁt into your way of working?
DH: It should be one of the considerations right from the very beginning. It is very diﬃcult to change
design to suit DfD after you’ve been through the earlier stages. It could impact for example the foundation
design and instead of pouring concrete into the ground it might be screw piles so you can unscrew the
foundations after the building is gone and take them to be reused or recycled or something like that. You
might use foundations which again can be disassembled. In the past we have used what in England we
call like manhole rings, when you make a drain in the ground and you have these kind of precast concrete
rings and you stack them on top of each other and that forms the drain in the ground and you backﬁll
soil around them. We have used those as foundations before. You dig a hole, you put the rings in the
ground and you ﬁll them full of crushed concrete from another building or something.
LKF: Oh, i didn’t know you could do that.
DH: So you can make temporary.. Essentially they are permanent foundations but they are done in a
really sustainable way so in theory at the end of a building’s life you can the building apart, you can
expose the foundations and you can take those apart as well instead of having very large in situ concrete
labs in the ground.
LKF: That is quite clever. Do you know if that has been done in Denmark?
DH: I don’t think so. So that is something that has to be considered from the very beginning.
LKF: How do you see this when you work with the studios or architectural companies Do they respond
well to this?
DH: Generally yes. Again it starts to pose a few constraints to the architecture as well.. The size of
the components, the choice of materials. You know we have to start to think about what would best
suit a building designed for DfD and that might impose some constraints to the architecture, such as the
choice of the materials, the geometry or whatever.. It is deﬁnitely something that the whole design team
should understand from the beginning. It might even impacts in the strategyfor the ventilation or the
installations; Instead of having one very large mechanical plant in the basement or something like that,
it might be better to have a series of decentralized ventilation units around the building which can easily
be taken apart and reused in a new building. So there are a lots of considerations.
LKF: The last is about the lifespan of a whole construction and its components and what are your
thoughts about the lifespan of its building and its materials in relation to the layers of the building.
DH: In relation to the diﬀerent components?
LKF: Yes I was thinking you have the structural part of the building, you have the layering of materials,
and DfD oﬀers a new way of working with this in terms of the lifespan of the materials and the components.
DH: Yes. I mean generally the longest lifespan is the structure. Because this is the thing that is hardest
to replace. So generally the lifespan of the structure is 60 years or something like that, unless the client
has special demands. Like it might be a museum or an art gallery or a data centre. They might have a
demand for 100 years. Generally things like foundations, you never go back to them after the building
has been made; where things like cladding and membranes and insulation and exposed steel work –
balustrades, things like that, may have shorter lifespan or at least have some maintenance, what we call
life to ﬁrst maintenance. It might be even for a timber structure. That might be a situation where we
specify that the timber should have a speciﬁc treatment to avoid mould and insects and things like that,
that like to eat timber, so we might specify that it should be painted or not necessarily painted but made
with this varnish or some kind of protective chemicals.So it won’t last 60 years but it will maybe last 20
years before you might have to reapply the chemical treatment.So we say the life to the ﬁrst maintenance
is 20 years. The life to the ﬁrst maintenance of the window frames may be 5 or 10 years or something
like that depending if it is hardwood or PVC or whatever, but generally the lifespan is diﬀerent for all
these components. So again in the early design stages, we should kind of consider that as part of our
strategy for DfD. One strategy is to kind of almost overdesign things. Like I said before certain things
can be reconﬁgured without too many initial conditions or constraints imposed on that building system.
108
==PAGE:111==
So you could take a single store low rise building and reconﬁgure it into a 6 store or a 10 store tower,
but that implies that when you make your low rise building everything is overdesigned in terms of load
bearing capacity, but that oﬀers possibilities in the future but that oﬀers then possibilities in the future
that you don’t have to then throw that material away. You can reuse it. But the whole problem with
that is that how do you know that there is going to be a demand for this particular type of building in the
future, unless it is the same client who is willing to invest more money in the low rise building. Because
essentially when you overdesign you put in more material which costs the client more, whereas we could
just say we could just design this building and it will cost you less money and then when you want to do
your 6 store tower we will design it for that loading. We will make new structural components and recycle
the excisting ones and that is if it is the same client. If it is a diﬀerent client then you know it is quite
diﬃcult to anticipate what they want when you are designing a building for a diﬀerent plan. So there
are these kind of dilemmas involved. The other strategy is you just design things for the loads for that
particular design and actually you just make them very well reﬁned and optimized for that particular
condition and then you accept that at the end of that building’s lifespan you will throw away or recycle
as far as possible those components and start again.
LKF: Is this diﬀerent if the assignment is prefab based?
DH: I think it is the same kind of considerations.
KK: I have a question regarding the lifespans. Is this a problem that when we are discusing about DfD,
the proﬁts of reselling anything are after 60 years? So is this a major problem for the market that a
company has to make a decision and the proﬁts will be after 60 years?
DH: I think these are all new problems. The possibilities of DfD are very very interesting and I think
technically there are some challenges and usually as technical people can overcome those challenges by
doing research and testing and making documentation which is showing these things can be done. I think
the biggest problem is the business case for DfD. So yes it is possible to design technically something
that can be disassembled and reused. Who gets the beneﬁt of that? There is a client who would be able
to beneﬁt from these ready made prefabricated modules which can just be taken from the building next
door and made it to their new building and I guess the client who owns the existing building will beneﬁt
because he can sell those components instead of having them having to go to recycling plants or landﬁll
or that kind of thing. So there should be a market. I mean this whole circular economy thing is so closely
linked to DfD. You know that we need kind to consider who are the stakeholders in this whole circular
economy, who beneﬁts, what is the business case for it. We did something last year. I had a master
student from DTU who was interested in researching energy positive buildings and was the similar kind
of dilemma. You know, sure, as engineers we can design buildings that produce more energy than they
consume but what you do with that spare energy. You know who is beneﬁting from it? Is there a market
for it? Unless people want it. Unless you can sell it. Then there is very little beneﬁt in producing more
energy and making an energy positive building. And then it starts to throw up even more questions
about, you know, should we reconsider the whole energy business or energy infrastructure. You know
people are talking about an energy internet so that essentially you can produce energy, you can store it
and then you can put it in a sort of energy internet at the right time and then various people can draw
on energy from this or they can let it back out at diﬀerent times. So in theory this could decentralize the
whole energy industry. For the moment we rely on these huge co-operations like DONG to make energy
and I am sure they are linked with other stakeholders in global markets that aﬀect their pricesand the
amount of energy they can produce. We beneﬁt from that but we are also victims of it as well. We have
no ability to aﬀect it. So there is a move towards decentralizing that. But again there has to be a market
before you can get to that point.
LKF: I think that I am out of questions. Do you have any other questions?
KK: Just for the ﬁrst part of the interview that we were discussing about the CLT. I was wondering.. I
saw there is a possibility of interlocking the diﬀerent elements without using screws like they are ﬁtting
each other and I found this quite interesting as there are a lot of impacts that could be avoided that way.
Is this something that is being done?
DH: I think there is certainly a lot of research going on to CLT and there a lot of academic institutions
around the world, in Italy, in Canada, in UK, Austria, Germany. There is a lot of research going on
right now into how we can better use CLT. In the moment the knowledge is quite limited. So people are
using fairly simple methods of connecting CLT elements together. Either using bolts or steel plates and
screws. But I think as we get to knowing it better, as the research level increases we will ﬁnd better ways
of connecting it.. Maybe with wooden dowels or with connections which lock together without the need
for glueing screws. So yeah there’s a possibility. But things move very slowly so we have to wait some
time for that.
109
==PAGE:112==
KK: Yes that’s all then.
LKF: I think that was it. Thank you for your time.
DH: You are welcome to contact me again.

LKF: The ﬁrst question is about the challenges. We’d like to ask you where do you sexperience the
biggest challenges for working with DfD?
111
==PAGE:114==
JK: Well. . . The biggest challenges if you build, you don’t have the client. . . not really have the focus
on. . . He wants to have a house – he want’s not to have a house that can be disassembled in the ﬁrst step.
This is not the mail focus of building right now. So actually this means that we should focus a lot on the
advantages and also the challenges if we go more and more in this direction. Of course we can see it as a
kind of key – you know this way of joining materials and the way how to do it can be the key of many of
the main topics if we work with sustainability like LCA’s, like the thinking of ﬂexibility, the way of end of
life-thinking. Also the kind of how to deal with materials or avoid materials with contributed materials
or materials which have chemicals with environmental and health-problems. So it’s a very complex thing
if you deal with sustainability but everything has to do with; okay, what kind of materials do we choose
to build our buildings with and how to join these materials. So in that sense we think. . . and that’s the
very interesting point and that’s where I think we have a good chance to spread it out in architecture
oﬃces – that we’re dealing with the architectural detail again and discussing this. This is actually –
there’s a lot of architecture in it. So actually with the new focus on sustainability might mean (?) that
we can get the focus on the architecture detail more in again.
MSL: I think that, just to support what Jørgen just said, there is a little bit of a dilemma for building
owners. I have a lot to do with public building owners, mostly in housing. And there it’s of course
interesting what Jørgen was just pointing out; durability and durability for them it is accomplished
with. . . if you get a loan in bank that is supposed to last for 30 years, then you want that building to last
at least 30 years. But ideally 100. And most of the housing buildings we have today are from 50 to 100
years old. So the owners, what they want. . . They don’t want to tear them down, because it is politically
unviable. But it would be ideal if they could come and replace parts of it in a way that is sustainable,
in a way that doesn’t involve tearing things apart and that’s where the dilemma comes in. because they
really want fx windows that you can split them apart but the hole for the window will still be the same
size. Things like that. It is basic things. So what we experience is that that they want DfD in parts
but not in the hole/whole. And if it’s the hole/whole, that’s where LCA’s comes into the picture. It is
a more complex reality that we experience out in the ﬁeld. That’s how I experienced it a little bit. And
then of course there is the issue of facilities management. That’s where the challenges also come. It is
a positive challenge if you have things that can get disassembled easily change parts – I don’t know –
window is a good example, doors is another good example. What else. . . Balconies. But then the rest is
pretty much standard. “Let’s keep it the way it used to be”, no?
LKF: The next is about materials. In relation to DfD how do you choose materials?
MSL: I think it will always be an issue of how easy they are to. . . There is a diﬀerence in English when
you talk about tearing things apart and disassembly. And I think that in Danish you have the. . . it is
still hard to understand what the diﬀerence is between the two. Sometimes when you just split things
up – what do you do with the things you split up? The thing you can’t use? That’s another question.
What do you do with all thing garbage/thrash that comes out from buildings? So getting back to the
issues of materials is that: you need materials that are easy to disassemble without. . . Not composites
for example. Materials as raw as possible with as little treatment or painted fx are quite... Something
that has been looked away from. Even coatings of materials is not. . . As soon as you have to apply a
coating that can’t be removed easily, it’s. . . But we are getting back to the simple materials.
JK: In my role, with the responsibility of sustainable ability, together with Mathilde we have analyzed a
lot of projects, we have also worked on the DGNB certiﬁcation of the oﬃce. But there is not a written
down or ﬁxed deﬁned design guideline to how we deal with materials. But I think you can ﬁnd in a lot
of projects the kind of way to design with natural materials. If you look around here it is just concrete
structures which are not painted. We have wooden panels and window framing. So the materials they
are in a kind of basic condition and I think there was also a kind of design guide to have few materials.
And I think this is actually in kind of the complexity we are dealing with in terms of sustainability a
good way to start. To think again. . . Mies van der Rohe’s slogan “yes is more”. But I think if we are
dealing with few materials it is easier to come around and all these complex issues we have to deal with,
just to compare one material to the other and looking on LCA and what kind of chemicals are in the
materials. You have a lot of complex issues to deal with and there’s nobody to say how to do. The LCA
you have [no?] more that 7 parameters – that is the output, the result. So nobody says what is better.
There are plusses and minuses in these two materials and how to compare it? Just the comparison is
actually very, very diﬃcult. But then there is common sense thinking that we come longest with. To
make the complex things easy again. We know that wood is a good material to work with. It has also
some challenges because if you are dealing with it on the outside you are aware of the durability.
MSL: And maybe focus on materials that can be screwed instead of glued. Mechanical assembly. That
has become more interesting or relevant so to say.

==PAGE:115==
JK: But this is also kind of instead of looking into what kind of glue is better. The easiest way is to just
avoid using glue and use screws. Because I also, when I started working with the DGNB and made the
ﬁrst certiﬁcation, there is this very complex criterium about chemicals in materials and at some stage. . .
I think I worked on it really hard in two weeks, but I have to give up. I am not a chemist. So there
are some rules to look into but really, in the end it is really, really complex and if you have these long
chemical substances it is really hard to ﬁnd out what is wrong or right in those terms. You really have
to be a specialist in that.
MSL: I think that regulations too sometimes come in the way. It is not only an issue of materials but
also, what is allowed and what is not allowed. I am mentioning this because I came across an example:
We have some balconies we are building in a refurbishment right now and we noticed that (it is a long
story) there were some structural issues of this balcony. There was a bending happening. So we had to
reinforce them. It was a metal construction but some 40 of them were already mounted and when we got
back to them, we see they have a [lukkebånd?]-plate. It is an aluminum with a plastic sole and it is an
aluminum sandwich – a composite. But it is very good for ﬁre issues but due to ﬁre regulations it has to
be glued, because if you put screws in it, then you are making physical connection between the plate and
the rest of the structure and then it is no good any longer. It doesn’t serve any ﬁre protection purposes.
So we look at these plates and in order to split them we basically have to detroy the whole structure for
the balcony and replace it with a new one. So we are there with a solution which is no good for anything.
It was good ﬁre-wise but it wasn’t good to come afterwards and just make a simple reparation. And that
is why it is terrible. We didn’t think it the whole way through. We thought that the easiest way and
the one that is providing the balconies actually, he just looked at the bottom line and said this is the
cheapest, that is where we make the strongest proﬁt and as long as the architect is accepting it, there is
nothing to do.
JK: Another interesting issue is (maybe this is also connected to the ﬁrst question) value of materials.
We don’t really have focus on the value of the materials we build with because in the end of life phase all
the materials are dealt with like thrash. It has no value. You have to pay a lot of money to get rid of it.
Which has changed about in the I think 1960’s-70’s it was so, that when you tore a building down, the
company who did this actually payed you some money. Because the materials still had kind of a value.
And this changed. Now you have to pay to get rid of your building. And I think here, it is actually a very
interesting point to turn it back, you know. Turn it back to the level where you say; Okay, you invest
in a lot of steel, bricks, concrete, other materials, but in the end if you tear the buildings down, these
materials you have invested in have still a value. This could be a game changer. But this needs a kind
of . . . As Mauro said, it is not the way we think buildings. We think buildings in investment and also if
you just look at the privat plane [on a private level]; if you just buy your own house, you are not thinking
to tear it down, I think. You are thinking that this should be an investment, it should last forever.
MSL: We also experience many owners, particularly public owners, school owners fx., where there is
somebody making the investment and then somebody else who is actually maintaining the building,
doing the facility management.
So the ones investing in the building are the owners, theoretically.
Because schools fx they are rented by the school district, right. But there is somebody who actually owns
the buildings themselves. They are not interested in hearing about facilities management, they are not
interested in hearing what you have to do to maintain – change those windows and what you have to do
with those windows in ten years or in 5 years, you have to paint them or whatever. They just concentrate
in making the cheapest buildings so that they can get rented right away. And that happens with many
building owners particularly now where most buildings are owned by wealthy real estate companies. Very
few concentrate on what is happening after the building is being used and what is happening in 10 years
time, 20 years time and so on.

buildings. That means the façade of an oﬃce building, maybe in 30 years we have completely other
possibilities to update buildings, so why should the façade last for 100 years?
But the rest of the
construction, the main construction could last for 100 years or longer because I don’t think we will

I think this is a chance; we these
common sizes and I don’t think they will change rapidly.
Thinking in components, having a more

MSL: We are in a sort of standardization frenzy. It looks like everybody has to have these CE-. . . I had

LKF: About maybe returning to the question of materials, would your choice of materials be diﬀerent if
you know that a project must be constructed using pre-fab modules? Or if working with somebody like

JK: Of course, if you work with a system like Scandi Byg, it’s volume, standard sized, box system, you
have to design with it from the very beginning. We actually had a competition we made with Scandi
Byg and this was one of the main topics; How can we put in the room program into their box system?
And the issue was to minimize the number of boxes because their price is more or less ﬁxed on one box,
it doesn’t really matter so much about the size of it. It’s the number of the boxes – which are the main
structure – that’s where the prices is. So of course you have to design with it from the very beginning to
get a good understanding of that. It gives you some limitations in your design, but sometimes it gives you
also new possibilities. Like you can do overhangs, which in traditional buildings would be more costly
because the boxes are designed so that they actually can deal with that kind of overhang situation. So
the structure is able to do this. That’s sometimes the surprising or the good thing. We just had been
surprised. . .
we ended up having to quit the competition because Scandi Byg’s price calculation said
that they cannot build for this budget. Which was really surpricing that they weren’t able to compete
in that competition. Or they didn’t want to. That could also be..
MSL: There is also a little bit resistance toward prefabrication. . . Some traditional contractors are not
really into prefabrication unless it has been tested for the last 30 years or something like that you know.
If somebody has done it for 30 years, then: “OK, it might be something we’ll look into.” I have an
example with a big project with prefabricated bathroom units like the ones that are actually mounted
within a room, not the ones that you put in from the roof, and that was the easiest solution ever, and the
contractor, the [woman?], actually proposed to build them traditionally for roughly about 90% of what the
prefabricated will cost. So the owner actually ended up going with the traditional solution and 6 months
within the construction process, there are so any structural challenges with building these bathrooms
traditionally, that they are already roughly 1,5 times more expensive than the prefabricated solution that
we envisioned before. But there’s no way back. You can’t do the rest of the toilets prefabricated. So
there are many who believe that prefabricated solutions are expensive in the long run, but don’t really
have the numbers to document it.
JK: For many years, I made kind of a research project. It was part of an exhibition for DAC, part of

for Arkitema at the time and it was about how to think housing in a new way and to make a housing
factory, which could build houses in a new way. And we ended up to make a research on what are the
10 biggest challenges of the building industry and came up with, everything is manufactured still on site,
less industrialization in the building industry if you compare it with like, car fabrication and so on. And
then we found out that the key, where you have the main problem in prefabrication is the size and the
transportation. So the container size, more or less, or now you can do some. . . But everything has to
be transported on our normal street system and this gives the main limitation on what you can do in

MSL: I think in a way what drives buildings in our days is economy or the ﬁnancial picture whereas I
think if we start thinking DfD in a way that is feasible, we should disassociate the two to start with
at least. Of course it is going to be diﬃcult to convince somebody that I have to build up a building
that can be splitted apart with a screwdriver for example without looking how much it will cost. But I
think, we as designers have to think the whole way through in a way that it is not driven by how cheap
it will get because otherwise it is going to be diﬃcult to get the whole way around and it’s never going
to compete with traditional well established technologies and ways of building. I think at least if you
want to get to a very basic example, huts in Africa or huts in South America where I was a month ago,

MSL: We have to be able to rethink how to build buildings in a way that it is not associated with the

get the data which refer to this topic. And this means that if you really have to work with it, to put this
kind of aspects into it otherwise we would came up with a result which show us something diﬀerent but
there have been reports and studies which said ok the lifetime is longer for green rooﬁng. So there is a
lot of work to do and actually here we need some more precise detailed data for it and it is also hard for
us you know.. What is the lifetime of this material and what is the weakest part of this..
ML: Mostly the data available are diﬀerent. There is some data in tools like LCCbyg and LCAbyg there
is some data and when you look at SPI report where they have like table report of lifetimes of diﬀerent
components and materials, and these are not the same.
LKF: Some are on german data?
ML: I am not sure but some of the data are built on just experience. So the developer of the program
said ok based on experience this lasts a bit longer or shorter than expected so there is a need for also
developing the knowledge of lifespans for layers and materials or at least that there are standards and
those are the data and they are similar otherwise you can point your calculations everywhere you want
it so you use the numbers that reach what you want to reach. So that ‘s interesting as well.
MSL: There is also an industry for regionalizing building components. Staﬀthat has been made and used
in Germany for example to last 50 years for example and it’s a tone of german climate data. We have to
adapt it. We have to get data from here. And then you ran the whole regionalization process and it was
actually a component industry that was presenting this in a conference a long time ago and they were
saying that we get exactly to the same result but it has to made by somebody within Denmark’s borders
so that you can actually see that the materials are also compatible with the danish weather conditions
and with the danish standards and with danish cultural requirements and so on and so forth.
LKF: Some time it is a good thing. Isn’t that what it lacks with NGO sheets?
MSL: Yes.. I don’t know the whole story about the NGO but they been used and they are good enough
when they are used in the right way. The issue is whether that we use it in the right way. But I am
not sure. That’s another issue, it’s another component. It is also build for disassembly as matter of fact
because it’s mechanically attached in buildings when it is being used and this is about the logic of the
materials. You have to know how they work and how they can be used.
LKF: Do you have any other questions?
KK: It was really interesting to hear about this regionalizing because we also tried to investigate it in
previous semester for a project with Vandkunsten where we tried to regionalize the LCA impacts. It
was a project about Nordic LCA database but it was really hard to work with because for example were
discussing about the lifespan of materials in order to compare them and most producers had a lifespan
that wasn’t corresponding to the actual lifespan, the aesthetical lifespan because when you use a material
you can’t use it until it wears out completely, you change a material maybe at the half of its actual
lifespan and also it was quite diﬃcult to ﬁnd where a product was produced because the construction
company buys maybe the material from China so you can’t be sure this product was produced here.
MSL: I think we have to be aware that globalization has came. There is no way we can change that.
LKF: And on that note that’s it.
MSL: We are really interested to ﬁnd out the results of your research.
118
==PAGE:121==
C
Analytic DfD challenges according to the interviews
C.1
The business case
Building up the business case is maybe the main challenge as it can inﬂuence signiﬁcantly the market
trends and lead the building industry to reshaping.
The main challenges that derived regarding the
business case are the following:
Lack of a business case or a business model [Bang (2017)]
Currently there aren’t enough business cases or a business model regarding DfD and an interested part
has to "invent everything" on their own.
No existing market for disassembled structural components [Horswill (2017)]
“I think the biggest problem is the business case for DfD” and “creating a market for disassembled struc-
tural components”, in order to motivate stakeholders to invest money. "Most signiﬁcant factors are the
cost and the risk". The risk element is quite interesting as when taking apart a structure, there’s a risk
that elements will get damaged and there is the “big question of who takes responsibility? - is it the
contractor or is it the client? And also how is the manufacturer involved in this?”. Typically in building
investments, structure is only one small risk so now it becomes bigger and “that’s the biggest challenge”.
Furthermore “very few people want to be the ﬁrst to do anything new” without having a business case,
but “then you’ll have people queuing up to do it”. In general though, a client would be able to beneﬁt from
reusing these ready made prefabricated modules to their new building or by selling these components
instead of recycling or landﬁlling them. “So there should be a market”.
Lack of a large player to guarantee the beneﬁts and handling of the problems in the long term - Material
ownership [Bang (2017)]
Today there isn’t "a larger player for both securing quality and solving any problems for several years af-
ter". Professional clients seek guarantees that the solutions and materials are reliable by a "larger player",
"who you know is both here today selling the good ideas but is also here later when I might run into
problems with a solution that I have bought". In case of smaller businesses "most clients would see that as
a risk. Is that player in the market in 10 or 20 years time?". This challenge maybe demand a totally new
way of thinking as for example prefabricators owning the building and leasing it. Then long term viability
and recycling the materials "would be a part of their business model", minimizing the risks for the clients.
Financial criterion at the ﬁrst steps [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
Economy is the main driver in buildings but maybe in case of DfD, economy shall not be strictly consid-
ered at least in the beginning and ut afterwards economy into this. Designers shall consider the economy
so strictly as this could be diﬃcult and "it’s never going to compete with traditional well established
technologies and ways of building".
The lifespan of the investment is incompatible with the developers’ perspective
"The very long time span of the investment is incompatible with the developers’ perspective as they have
a very short time horizon" [Bang (2017)]. Specially in case of Denmark, the very long lifespans of build-
ings requires seeing "50-100 years ahead" [Bang (2017)]. Often investors, particularly public owners, are
diﬀerent from the end users [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. Consequently facility management or End of
Life, isn’t their concern, but it is to make "the cheapest buildings so that they can get rented right away"
[Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. On the other hand, private investors with that long time thinking could
be "some pension funds for instance, who are investors in oﬃce buildings" or the social housing associa-
tions as today a small fragment of them examining this perspective and work with pilot projects [Bang
(2017)]. Furthermore some companies maybe could work DfD "as part of their CSR-policy" [Bang (2017)].
The lifespan of the investment is incompatible with the clients’ perspective - DfD in parts dilemma
[Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
119
==PAGE:122==
Building a house is considered a life long investment for the owner and most of the housing buildings
today are "from 50 to 100 years old". So tearing it down would be "politically not viable", but replacing
parts in a sustainable way without tearing things apart "would be ideal" and that’s the dilemma as clients
want "DfD in parts but not in the whole". Thinking the building as components and taking facade of an
oﬃce building as an example, maybe in 30 years there are totally new possibilities for updating buildings,
so it could be then renewed. However the main construction "could last for 100 years or longer because
I don’t think we will see building types which are much deeper or much more narrow". Summing up,
"thinking in components, having a more detailed understanding of these lifecycles of building compo-
nents, this could also be a game changer in DfD".
Consultants as well as manufacturers have to rethink and adapt in order their business to survive [Lewis
(2017a)]
Generally one of the biggest challenges is getting people involved, "consultants as well as manufacturers
to rethink". In order to rethink, it needs strong motivation and being able to earn money. The biggest
motivation in this case is that "your business needs to reassess in order to survive". In case of Henning
Larsen Architects the longer term use of materials and the building is considered "if there is a certiﬁcation
involved".
There is a wide range of choices in working with sustainability and clients are not yet quite familiar with
DfD [Bang (2017)]
The most crucial challenge for DfD today according to Mr Bang is the DfD is only "a small fragment of
this whole universe of sustainability for building clients" as there is an "overwhelming number of things
associated with sustainability a professional client has to keep an overview". It was highlighted that
clients are still struggling to implement sustainability, while the so wide range of ways to work with
sustainability leads to the risk DfD not being addressed as a choice. Clients recently started becoming
familiar with DfD. Market drives are crucial for the spreading of the idea as developers follow market
trends. "If developers were to be more conscious of recycling and lifespan of building materials, it would
have to come from the consumers I would say".
Cultural challenges [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
Cultural challenges can be described as people associate with good building ethics and "good building
ethics is building solid". Consequently everybody tries to have a brick house and if that isn’t possible,
a house that has cladding brick even if all the interior is made of gypsum board. That takes derives by
status issues "or because what they believe is solid". However this is a problem for DfD when everything
must be cladded in bricks or materials have to be mixed. "So there are some cultural changes that need
to happen so that DfD is the whole way round".
DfD could unite all sustainability issues [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017), Lewis (2017a)]
"Of course we can see it (DfD) as a kind of key (..) of many of the main topics if we work with sustain-
ability like LCA’s, like the thinking of ﬂexibility, the way of end of life-thinking" [Kisslinger and Lucardi
(2017)]. DfD aﬀects all the LCA environmental impacts and also aﬀects the health of the indoor air
climate, as the need for adhesives is reduced, and more [Lewis (2017a)]. "All these things that tend to
go in their diﬀerent directions", so DfD is "actually uniting some of these diﬀerent paths" [Lewis (2017a)].
Not being mandatory by the regulations [Bang (2017)]
Mr Bang stated that if the next generation of building regulation included requirements for recycling of
materials it would be "a strong driver". However he mentioned his uncertainty if we are ready for this
due to the lack of a comprehensive business model and the unknown implications it would cause to the
building material market as it could lead to the excessively high standards and increase of prices.
Changes in all the current chain (design - manufacturing - assembly - disassembly) [Nielsen (2017)]
A deﬁnite challenge is that there are going to be signiﬁcant challenges in all the levels of the building
industry. Material suppliers must make new design systems. Subsequently those who assemble will have
120
==PAGE:123==
to think in another way to develop their assembly techniques. Mr Nielsen highlighted the importance of
the unavoidable interrelation of all the parts of the chain.
C.2
Material reuse
The section regarding materials includes a wide range of challenges regarding the reuse of the materials.
Furthermore a subchapter with the challenges regarding the manufacturing of materials that are suitable
for DfD is included.
Need for documentation
A challenge for reusing material is the lack of information [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. Having a library
about the existing materials in a building or if each component had some data attached it would help to
know what their capabilities are and identify how they could be reused [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)].
"Missing knowledge about the existing, makes it not possible to reuse as well sometimes"[Kisslinger and
Lucardi (2017)]. One of the product characteristics that must be documented is the strengths of the
diﬀerent elements and of the structural components to enable and easy their reuse Nielsen (2017). Fur-
thermore precise documentation of impurities in the construction materials can lead to start taking some
clean fractions out of the waste and consequently to produce recycled products of high quality [Nielsen
(2017)]. Purer products have more well known properties and higher value [Nielsen (2017)]. Modern
techniques in case of recycling impure concrete as an aggregate destroy the recycling potential of the next
part as well and the authorities are not yet fully able to handle it but Dansk Byggeri pressures them to
do so Nielsen (2017).
How documentation shall be stored [Nielsen (2017)]
Another challenge in documenting material characteristics would be the way these data should be saved
in order to be compatible with the future technologies to ensure that they can be retrieved and be easily
used in 30-40 years. There should be at least some thorough considerations about how data should be
stored. And that also applies when we start to recycle, that is, recycle our waste products. What are the
characteristics of the waste products we have.
Old parts may become obsolete with time and incompatible with the regulations [Nielsen (2017)]
There is a signiﬁcant challenge regarding the handling methods for diﬀerent parts of the building. Typ-
ical example in this case is an old existing window that consists of several elements: wood/ plastic/
aluminum frame, glass, insulation, ﬁttings, handles, etc. Some of them could be preferable to be recycled
as raw materials or to be used for other products instead of using them again as parts of the old window.
Furthermore old windows have became hopelessly obsolete, as modern windows are energy-eﬃcient. So,
believing that the building components can be dismantled and reused always in the same way is quite
ambitious and specially when the requirements for the component are tightened.
Reusability of structural systems [Horswill (2017)]
Regarding the reuse of structural systems "there’s generally a problem with the theory of DfD" as for
example the ﬂoor span and the loading must be exactly the same.
Legislation limitations in reusing materials [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
Reuse of materials is a complex issue as in terms of bureaucracy, CE-certiﬁcations for materials are
needed. For example in case of reusing bricks (as it was the case for hundred years), although the bricks
were CE-marked before, to be reused "they also have to be CE-marked, which doesn’t really give any
logical meaning". This derives by the fact that their durability is unknown but "common sense says it
shouldn’t be a problem". Considering that "everybody have to have these 25 diﬀerent markings and
patents" it becomes complex "how you have to do it". This creates a serious problem for the market for
salvaged materials right now.
121
==PAGE:124==
C.3
Materials’ lifespans
Material lifespans are critical for forecasting and setting the allowed times a material can be reused and
for estimating environmental impacts, ﬁnancial revenues, etc.
Lack of unbiased, accurate and widely accepted data
Lifespans of building materials is a quite unambiguous issue. Sometimes the industry provided data seem
to exaggerate or are limited. These inaccuracies aﬀect proper LCA and LCC comparisons. Often data
in LCCbyg and LCAbyg are diﬀerent than SPI reports.
Lack of standards for making assumptions regarding lifespans
Furthermore several of these data are based merely on experience and rough estimations. Consequently
there is a need for data regarding lifespans of materials or at least standards as "otherwise you can point
your calculations everywhere you want it so you use the numbers that reach what you want to reach".
Need for regionalizing lifespans in Denmark based on climatic data
Moreover there is also the aspect of regionalizing building components and n example in which components
lifespans were estimated based on german climatic data. Consequently this also should be acknowledged
and lifespans must be adjusted to the danish weather conditions, standards, cultural requirements and
so on.
C.4
Product Manufacturers’ challenges
Materials’ manufacturers have to deal with a diﬀerent range of challenges regarding the production of
materials or the adjustment of the existing ones in order to facilitate the DfD principles.
Combine DfD with practical and ﬁnancial aspects [Vibæk (2017)]
Designing a product always includes the cost criterion and the optimization and eﬃciency of the produc-
tion. Design for disassembly has to be as much as possible compatible with these aspects.
Huge alterations in the production line and the supply chain in case of an existing product [Vibæk (2017)]
In the case of changing an existing product to ﬁt the DfD principles there is the challenge of creating
an already existing product. Furthermore, the smallest product modiﬁcations can have very big conse-
quences somewhere in the supply chain and even to the properties of the product. Characteristically,
in case of Soft Cells if the composition of the "akustiske felt", all tested samples would be smashed.
Probably an existing product must be redesigned from the beginning.
Contamination aﬀects reuse [Vibæk (2017)]
A challenge with the reclaimed products and specially in the case of Kvadrat Soft Cells is that the re-
claimed products are often contaminated or have other problems leading to lower recycling potential.
Identifying the needed level of disassembly for expanding the product lifecycle [Vibæk (2017)]
For example in case of Kvadrat Soft Cells, they aim to extend the lifespan of the frame of aluminum,
through DfD, to improve the environmental performance of the product. For example in case of cor-
porate headquarters, they wouldn’t have the same decor for 50 years as the building but if the textile
surface could be changed - and that may be the case - the product could be there forever. Consequently
identifying the level of the product that DfD must reach is crucial.
Deciding the optimal solution for collecting or not the used products can be ﬁnancially and environmen-
tally blurry. [Vibæk (2017)]
122
==PAGE:125==
Kvadrat Soft Cells - as numerous companies - never sees again customers after they buy the product. In
order for this to change, there are numerous diﬀerent ways of thinking. Such solutions would be leasing
the products, or - as in case of Soft Cells - cooperating with a company that are working to recycle
textile ﬁbers. In case of Soft Cells the considered choices where 3. The ﬁrst was to just make everything
recyclable and let the products to end up directly into corresponding recycled streams through their
clients and no initiatives were to be taken for this scenario as it quite simple. The second choice was to
extend the product’s life by selling service packages for maintaining it and taking it back at the end. It
helps a little in the environmental accounts due to the longer life span. The third choice would be to
expand this service pack, but then to return the panels like using them reconﬁgured. In the last two cases
though it should be considered that there are some scattered clients all over the world and the reclaiming
process should be carefully designed to optimize the environmental beneﬁts.
C.5
Detailing
Structural and architectural detailing face a range of challenges regarding technical, practical and leg-
islative issues.
DfD must be considered from the early stages for both structural and architectural designs [Horswill (2017),
Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
DfD must be considered from the very beginning as "it is very diﬃcult to change design to suit DfD after
you’ve been through the earlier stages", as it impacts for example the foundation design [Horswill (2017)].
The decision of designing the structural system according to DfD should be incorporated from the very
beginning in order to make the correct choice for the structural system [Horswill (2017), Kisslinger and
Lucardi (2017)]. For example considering the position of the installations, if it should be in the ﬂoor, a
lighter structure such as wood, is preferable but if it must be ﬁlled up with concrete, it is much harder
[Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. In another example in a steel building to consider only bolted connections
and no welded, and to consider also the size of the components in order to enable them being lifted [Hor-
swill (2017)]. In other cases large column-free spaces are needed for high ﬂexibility but that might lead to
big heavy beams and trusses [Horswill (2017)]. So again instead of designing a beam which is 20 metres
long, then it could be considered breaking large components to smaller components and combining them
together with bolted connections [Horswill (2017)]. "Timber is essentially the same" [Horswill (2017)].
Steel and timber frames relate inherently very well to DfD while precast concrete "in theory it should
be possible to take it apart" but practically isn’t yet [Horswill (2017)]. Speciﬁcally, regarding concrete,
Peikko systems is a really good case and "very easy to construct with" but the fact that concrete is poured
in all the gaps between the elements makes it "almost impossible to take that apart without destroying
part of the beam or the column or the precast element as well" [Horswill (2017)]. So reassembly of these
elements isn’t feasible as "there’s a large risk you lose the integrity of the elements" [Horswill (2017)].
"So steel and timber has potential I think, out of all the main structural systems" [Horswill (2017)].
Furthermore, bricks and masonry is a choice as there are industries that sell recycled or reclaimed bricks
although the process of taking it down and cleaning the bricks is very time consuming [Horswill (2017)].
It poses a few constraints to the architecture as well as for example components’ sizes, material choice,
geometry, etc [Horswill (2017)]. It may even inﬂuence the ventilation or the installations as instead of
having for example one very large mechanical plant in the basement, it could be better having a se-
ries of decentralized ventilation units around the building that be taken apart and reused easier. "It is
deﬁnitely something that the whole design team should understand from the beginning" [Horswill (2017)].
Material choice is complex as legislative requirements (energy eﬃciency, air-tightness, etc), maintenance,
environmental impacts and other parameters have to be also considered [Lewis (2017a), Kisslinger and
Lucardi (2017), Nielsen (2017)]
"Materials are a really complex area because there are so many diﬀerent requirements to materials"
[Lewis (2017a)]. It is important to choose materials that can serve the function they are being used for
without much treatment [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. Another challenge is "to incorporate mechanical
ways of attachment without compromising the quality of the material or the quality of the solution itself
or the overall picture aesthetically speaking" [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. Furthermore regulations
aﬀect signiﬁcantly the detailing and the material choice in a building [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. As
123
==PAGE:126==
Mauro Lombardi described a case where for solving a bending problem in balconies in a steel structure
they wanted to use an aluminum part with a plastic sole and it was performing very good regarding ﬁre
issues [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. However it had to be glued due to ﬁre regulations as "if you put
screws in it, then you are making physical connection between the plate and the rest of the structure -
It doesn’t serve any ﬁre protection purposes" [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]. The building regulations
also require mandatory high energy eﬃciency and air-tightness levels [Nielsen (2017)]. However achieving
high air tightness in a fully dismantlable building could be a challenge [Nielsen (2017)].
Most architects aren’t familiar with the DfD and even having an external auditor isn’t enough [Lewis
(2017a)]
Based on a real life case, Mrs Martha Lewis described the challenge of having an external auditor for
DGNB certiﬁcation. Specially now that archotects are not yet familiar with the DfD principles a closer
contact is needed. "I would really put a big question mark on the process if it is not an architect that
has that role".
Considering future structural needs (overdesign) [Horswill (2017)]
One strategy for facilitating any possible future needs for construction of more ﬂoors is to overdesign
things in terms of load bearing capacity allowing for example the reconﬁguring of a low-rise building into
a 6 store building. The problem is that how can one predict this demand unless it is the client who makes
this call, as overdesign costs more. The other strategy is to design things for the loads of a particular
design and for a higher building everything must be disassembled and then redesigned for the new loads.
Limited inﬂuence of architects to the ﬁnal material choice and to the working site [Lewis (2017a)]
Regarding the ﬁnal material choice Mrs Martha Lewis highlighted "I have to be really clear about this
because we have only a limited inﬂuence as an architect". Architects can only push towards a speciﬁc
solution but it is not their call. Clients and contractors have a ﬁxed ﬁnancial agreement and contractors
choose the speciﬁc materials. "It is very complicated what materials end up in the ﬁnal building".
Material choice for the non structural part doesn’t take place in the beginning [Lewis (2017a)]
Material choice "isn’t so relevant in the early phases because it is in the early phases they are just looking
at two lines on a piece of paper, they’re not looking at what is that baseboard actually made of?". It
becomes relevant in the construction/ documentation phase. Architects draw the details when they look
for tenders. "People work on a project maybe for 2 years before someone looks at that baseboard and
says what is that actually made of?". Consequently if DfD concept shall be applied, it has to take place
by adapting to existing design.
C.6
Prefabrication related challenges
This section of the challenges refers the limitations that could derive from the use of prefabricated ele-
ments.
Contractors are sceptic regarding untested prefab solutions and prefab elements in general [Kisslinger and
Lucardi (2017)]
Traditional contractors are afraid of untested prefabrication f they haven’t been "tested for the last 30
years". Furthermore there is a wide spread misconception that "prefabricated solutions are expensive in
the long run, but don’t really have the numbers to document it".
Previous problems with modular toilet / bathroom units create a bad background for prefab [Bang (2017)]
In the case of modular toilet units /bathroom units that were installed in numerous residential buildings,
there were problems with the long time live-ability of the providing businesses. Users wanted to make
changes involving the units after 20-30 years and the prefab suppliers were no longer there. Furthermore
124
==PAGE:127==
"some of the solutions that were chosen were quite shady and very work with, very diﬃcult to renovate".
Limitations regarding size [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
The main limitation derives by the size and the transportation of components as all the parts shall be
transported through regular streets.
Prefabrication was always linked to cheap and low quality ways of building [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
Prefabrication was always linked to cheap and low quality ways of building and this is still a huge problem.
The latter started to change recently and this can be demonstrated with the popularity of the row-houses
that got very popular in Copenhagen - people spend a lot of money for these - and "this is where Scandi
Byg really got into the market". Ultimately creating concrete elements that are able to be disassembled
is really challenging.
Buildings are customized for the initial design and reconﬁguring is diﬃcult [Horswill (2017)]
DfD in prefab is "kind of dealing with lego blocks then". The challenge then is that as everything gets
more and more customized with individual doors or window placings, etc, it "becomes more customized
for that ﬁrst initial design so it is quite diﬃcult to then reconﬁgure it because there might be certain
constraints you impose on that initial design". Consequently "if we are moving into the era of DfD we
should make our designs more robust in the ﬁrst place, so there is more redundancy in the design, so
it can be taken apart and made into a 6-storey tower instead of a 3-storey low-rise or something like that".
Standardization versus architecture [Kisslinger and Lucardi (2017)]
A signiﬁcant question is about standardization. Will it be needed or not? Will it be a limitation or will
it give new possibilities? In case of Scandibyg box system there is a limited range of possible solutions
and probably Scandi Byg will have a big catalogue of potential solutions to pick one from and maybe an
architect could be asked for the facade. So there is an issue whether standardization will limit the ﬁeld
for architecture. However modular or pre-fabrication building aimed always to standardize and to enable
building a lot in the same way.

F
Biogenic carbon balance
For the calculations, biogenic carbon emissions from the "treatment of waste building wood, chrome
preserved, municipal incineration - CH" process of Ecoinvent were retrieved to be 1.44955 kg CO2 per
kg of waste wood. The reason and the method for calculations is described in Chapter 3.3.8. Similar
calculations also took place for the wood ﬁber insulation and the "treatment of waste wood, untreated,
municipal incineration - CH" process.

==PAGE:141==
M
Assumptions
Inventory
1. All the wood and wood-based products are assumed to be produced from wood produced with
sustainable forestry practices.
2. Installations, roof, sanitary inventories of the case house were set similar to the reference house.
3. Steel, low alloyed, at plant was used instead of hot-dip galvanized steel for the foundations.
4. Total window area is considered 25,5 m and the wooden frame is the 10% of this area.
5. Assumptions for several lifespans took place when they weren’t included in the Sbi tables. These
assumptions are mentioned in the Inventory.
6. Glue content in CLT was assumed to be 2% of polyurethane with density 1,1 g/cm3.
7. Exact dimensions of the internal walls were based on the reference house.
8. Replacements and avoided products are supposed to have the exact same production processes,
technologies and any inputs with the current practices.
End of Life
1. The treatment of all the wood products was modelled at EoL with the same process (waste building
wood, chrome preserved, municipal incineration)
2. Ground screws were considered that as long they are not maintained their lifespan is the same with
building. In cases were they are out of the ground their lifespan can be unimited. It was considered
that 25% of scews are in the second category
3. From water tank recycling it is assumed 260 kg steel, low-alloyed, hot rolled will be avoided
4. Ecoinvent processes for EoL and next product systems are demonstrated in Appendix D
5. Energy for crushing tiles, ceramics, concrete was assumed to be 0,082Mj/kg of waste
6. Assumptions regarding the crediting of energy production due to incineration of building elements
are analyzed in the methodology part
7. For modelling the recycling of steel, the "treatment of waste reinforcement steel, collection for ﬁnal
disposal" process was used
8. It is assumed that due to DfD full recovery of steel is possible, while all the wood goes to incineration
9. Credits for glue that is incinerated as part of CLT to be 3.47MJ/kg of waste electric energy and
7MJ/kg of waste thermal energy glue incineration similar to a speciﬁc process for glue incineration
in Ecoinvent
10. Wood ﬁber is treated as "waste wood, not preserved", while all the rest wood products are treated
as "waste building wood, chrome preserved"
11. Credits for incineration of wood ﬁber were 2.2708MJ/kg of waste electric energy and 6.9746MJ/kg
of waste thermal energy glue incineration assuming same eﬃciency with the EPD
12. All the sequestered carbon in the wooden materials is released in the environment.
13. The impacts from carbon sequestration for the whole life cycle is neutral.
14. The possible future marginal energy sources are considered to be natural gas and biomass. Energy
and heat production will be taking place in the same facilities only.
15. All the products are assumed to be produced and treated in EoL in Denmark and Europe
